Valid Double Measurement in Salam Contracts
Ruling on double measurement in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on double measurement in a salam contract and identify the condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
Two separate measurements in salam validate receipt for both buyer and seller.

In salam contracts, the buyer pays in advance and later receives goods. This passage addresses a specific scenario where the buyer orders the seller to measure the goods first for the seller's own benefit, then for himself. The ruling confirms validity, but only because two distinct measurements occur. Let's explore why this separation matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract
Recall that rabb al-salam is the buyer and muslam ilayh is the seller. The buyer orders the seller to receive (measure) the goods.
2
Distinguish the two orders
First order: measure for the seller's benefit. Second order: measure for the buyer's benefit. These are separate instructions.
3
Recognize the two measurements
The seller actually measures twice: once for each order. The commentary emphasizes 'two measurements have occurred'.
Each measurement is an independent act of receipt.
4
Conclude the ruling
Because the measurements are separate, each is valid. The overall transaction is sahih (valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو أمرَ رَبُّ السَّلَم بقبضه له ، ثمّ لنفسه، فاكتاله له ثم لنفسه
(وكذا لو أمر ربُّ السَّلَم بقبضه له ، ثمّ لنفسه، فاكتاله له ثم لنفسيه) : قوله : وكذا أي يصح في هذه الصورة كما يصح في الصورة الأولى، وهي ما إذا اشترى المُسَلَّمُ إليه كُرًا أمر ربُّ السَّلم بأن يقبضه لأجلِ المُسَلَّم إليه، ثم لنفسه، فاكتالَهُ للمسلم إليه، ثم اكتاله لأجل نفسه يصح، وإنما يصح؛ لأنه قد جرى فيه الكيلان.
And likewise if the owner of the salam ordered him to receive it for him, then for himself, and he measured it for him then for himself. (And likewise if the owner of the salam ordered him to receive it for him, then for himself, and he measured it for him then for himself): He said: 'Likewise' means it is valid in this case as it is valid in the first case, which is when the one to whom salam was made purchased a kurr and the owner of the salam ordered him to receive it for the salam seller, then for himself, and he measured it for the salam seller, then measured it for himself, it is valid, and it is only valid because two measurements have occurred in it.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn with commentary. The matn states that if the buyer in a salam contract (rabb al-salam) orders the seller (muslam ilayh) to receive the goods for him, then afterwards for himself, and the seller actually measures it for him and then for himself, this is valid. The commentary explains that 'likewise' refers to a previous ruling that this is valid just like the case where the seller purchased a kurr and the buyer ordered him to receive it for the seller and then for himself. The validity hinges on the fact that two separate measurements take place, each for a different purpose. This is a straightforward ruling without explicit proof, but it is based on the principle that each measurement is an independent act of receipt.

Key Terms
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract; the party who pays in advance.
مسلم إليه (muslam ilayh): The seller in a salam contract; the party who receives payment and delivers goods later.
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume; here it signifies actual receipt and transfer of possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the double measurement in this salam scenario considered valid?
A) Because the buyer owned the goods from the start.
B) Because two separate measurements occur, each for a different purpose.
C) Because the seller agreed to be an agent.
D) Because the goods were in the buyer's container.

Key Takeaway
Separate measurements in salam are valid because each is an independent act of receipt.

intermediate

Measuring into Counterparty's Container: Invalid Receipt
Ruling on measuring into container by order, and its exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on measuring into the counterparty's container in salam and sale, and explain the legal reasoning based on the nature of the right (dayn vs. 'ayn).

Core Idea
Receipt requires possession of the container; measuring into another's container does not transfer possession.

In Hanafi sales law, the validity of receipt (qabd) is critical for transferring liability. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah examines two cases: salam and ordinary sale. In both, if the seller measures the goods into the buyer's container but the buyer is absent, the receipt is invalid because the buyer's order does not encounter his ownership effectively. The proof lies in the distinction between a debt (dayn) and a specific item ('ayn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts
The passage discusses salam (forward sale) and ordinary sale (bay'). In salam, the buyer's right is a debt in the seller's liability, not a specific item. In ordinary sale, the buyer owns a specific item from the moment of contract.
2
Analyze the buyer's order
In salam, the buyer orders the seller to measure the goods into the buyer's container. The order is invalid because the buyer does not own the specific goods; they are still the seller's property. In sale, the buyer owns the goods but the container belongs to the seller (or is borrowed), so the buyer's order to measure into the seller's container does not give him possession of the container.
3
Understand the role of the container
Possession of the container determines possession of its contents. If the buyer has not taken possession of the container (i.e., it is still in the seller's hand), the goods placed in it remain in the seller's possession.
The text compares this to measuring into a corner of the seller's house.
4
Note the exception of presence
The passage states that if the buyer is present, the measurement counts as receipt because the buyer's action (handling) transfers possession. This indicates that presence can compensate for lack of container possession.
5
Derive the general principle
The ruling shows that valid receipt requires either actual physical possession of the goods or possession of the container that holds them. An order alone does not suffice if the ordered action does not result in the buyer's control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان المسلم إليه في ظرف ربِّ السَّلم بأمرِهِ بغيبته أو كال البائع في ظرفه أو ظرف بَيْتِهِ بأمر المشتري لم يكن قبضاً ; لأنَّ في السَّلَم لم يصح أمرُ ربِّ السّلم بالكيل ; لأنَّ حقه في الدين لا في العين، فأمرُهُ لم يصادف ملكه، فالمسلم إليه جعلَ مَلكَهُ في ظرف استعارَهُ من ربِّ السَّلَم ، وفي البيع لم يصح أمر المشتري ; لأنَّه استعار الظرف من البائع، ولم يقضه، فيكونُ في يد البائع ، فكذا الحنطة التي فيه، وإنما قال : بغيبته حتى لو كان حاضراً يكون قبضاً ; لأنَّ فعلهُ ينتقل إليه. تحقق الاستبدال الذي هو حرام ; لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك، يلزم تمليك الشيء نسيئة، ألا ترى أنه لو باع كذا بكر لغير عينه لا يجوز. [٢] ١ ] قوله : لأن حقه...الخ ; يعني أنَّ حق ربِّ السلم في الدِّينِ لا في العين، والدين وصف ثابت في الذمة، فالأمر بالكيل لم يصادف ملك الأمر، بل إنَّما صادف عيناً مملوكاً للبائع. [٢] قوله : لأنه ... الخ ; حاصله : إنَّ المشتري ملك البر بنفس الشراء، فيصح أمره لمصادفته ملكه، فيكون قابضاً بجعله في الظرف ، ويكون البائع وكيلاً في إمساك الظرف ، فيكون الظرف في يد المشتري حكماً ، فكان الواقع فيه واقعاً في يده حكماً ; ولهذا اكتفى بذلك الكيل في الصحيح ، ألا ترى أنه لو أمره بالطَّحن لو بإلقائه في البحر، ففعل، يكون على الآمر في الشراء، ويتقرَّرُ الثمنُ عليه، وفي السلم على المأمور، لما قلنا. فإن قلت : البائع لا يصح أن يكون وكيلاً في القبض حتى لو وكله به لا يصح توكيله ولا يكون قابضاً له، فكيف يتصور أن يكون وكيلاً له هاهنا ؟
بخلاف كيله في ظرف المشتري بأمره
[۱] (بخلاف كيله في ظرف المشتري بأمره) : أي إذا اشترى حنطة معيَّنة، فأمر المشتري البائع أن يكيله في ظرف المشتري بغيبته، ففعل يصيرُ قابضاً ; لأنَّه مَلَكَ العين بالشراء، فأمرُهُ صادفَ مُلْكَه.
قلت : لَمَّا صح أمره لكونه مالكاً صار وكيلاً له ضرورة، وكم من شيء يثبت ضمناً، وإن لم يثبت قصدا. وقيَّد بكون الظرف للمشتري ; لأنه لو كان للبائع فأمره المشتري بالكيل فيه ففعل، لم يصر قابضاً ; لكون المشتري استعارَ ،ظرفه، ولم يقبضه، فلا يصير في يده ، فكذا ما يقع فيه، فصار كما لو أمره أن يكيله في ناحية من بيت البائع، فإنَّ المشتري لا يكون قابضاً، فإنَّ البيت بنواحيه في يد البائع، كذا في «المنح»، وغيره.
And if the one to whom salam was made is in the container of the owner of the salam by his order in his absence, or the seller measured in his container or the container of his house by the order of the buyer, it is not receipt; because in salam, the order of the owner of the salam for measuring is not valid because his right is in the debt not in the specific item, so his order did not encounter his ownership; thus the one to whom salam was made placed his property in a container he borrowed from the owner of the salam. And in sale, the order of the buyer is not valid because he borrowed the container from the seller and did not take possession of it, so it remains in the hand of the seller, and likewise the wheat in it. And he only said: 'in his absence' so that if he was present, it would be receipt because his action transfers to him. There is realized the substitution which is prohibited; because if it were not so, it would necessitate making something owed a debt for non-specific item. Do you not see that if he sold such and such a kurr for a non-specific item, it is not permissible. [2] 1] He said: 'Because his right... etc.' means that the right of the owner of the salam is in the debt, not in the specific item, and the debt is a description established in the liability, so the order to measure did not encounter the ownership of the orderer, but rather it encountered a specific item owned by the seller. [2] He said: 'Because... etc.' The summary: The buyer owns the wheat by virtue of the purchase itself, so his order is valid because it encounters his ownership, so he becomes a possessor by placing it in the container, and the seller becomes an agent in holding the container, so the container is in the hand of the buyer virtually, and what is placed in it is in his hand virtually; therefore that measurement suffices in the sound view. Do you not see that if he ordered him to grind or throw it into the sea, and he did, it would be on the orderer in the purchase, and the price becomes established on him, and in salam on the one ordered, as we said. If you say: The seller cannot be an agent in receiving, such that if he appoints him as an agent it is not valid and he is not a possessor, how can it be conceived that he is an agent here? 
Contrary to measuring in the buyer's container by his order
[1] (Contrary to measuring in the buyer's container by his order): That is, if he bought a specific wheat, and the buyer ordered the seller to measure it in the buyer's container in his absence, and he did, he becomes a possessor because he owned the specific item by purchase, so his order encountered his ownership. I say: Since his order is valid because he is an owner, he becomes an agent by necessity, and many things are established implicitly even if not established intentionally. And he restricted it to the container being the buyer's because if it were the seller's and the buyer ordered him to measure in it and he did, he does not become a possessor because the buyer borrowed his container and did not take possession of it, so it does not come into his hand, and likewise what falls into it. Thus it is as if he ordered him to measure it in a corner of the seller's house, for the buyer does not become a possessor, because the house with its corners is in the seller's hand, as in al-Manh and others.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof. It addresses two scenarios: salam and ordinary sale (bay'). In salam, if the buyer orders the seller to measure the goods into his own container but the seller is absent, it does not constitute valid receipt. The proof given is that the buyer's right in salam is a debt in the liability (dhimmah), not in a specific item, so his order to measure does not pertain to his property. The seller is placing his own goods into a container borrowed from the buyer, but the buyer lacked authority. In an ordinary sale, if the buyer orders the seller to measure into the seller's container or the seller's house, it is also not valid receipt because the buyer has not taken possession of the container. The commentary elaborates: in the sale scenario, the buyer actually owns the specific wheat by the contract, so his order is valid in principle, but the condition of the container matters. The text then contrasts with the case where the buyer orders measuring into the buyer's own container: then it is valid receipt because the buyer's order encounters his ownership, and the seller becomes an agent by necessity. The restriction to the buyer's container is crucial; if the container belongs to the seller and the buyer has not taken possession of it, the measurement does not transfer possession. The passage also addresses an objection about agency in receipt, answering that agency can be established implicitly even if not explicitly intended.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Physical or legal possession; here it refers to valid receipt of goods that transfers liability.
ظرف (zarf): Container used for measuring and storing goods; its ownership and possession affects the validity of receipt.
دين (dayn): Debt; in salam, the right is a debt in the liability, not a specific identified item.
عين (ayn): Specific, identified item; in the sale scenario, the buyer owns a specific 'ayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an ordinary sale, the buyer orders the seller to pour wheat into the seller's own container while the buyer is absent. According to Hanafi fiqh, does the buyer become a possessor?
A) Yes, because the buyer ordered it and owns the wheat.
B) Yes, because the wheat is in a container.
C) No, because the container is in the seller's hand.
D) No, because the buyer is absent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, valid receipt of goods placed in a container requires that the buyer either possess the container or be physically present; otherwise, an order to measure does not transfer possession.

Exception: Measuring into Buyer's Own Container Constitutes Valid Receipt
Exception to the general rule: measuring into buyer's own container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that when a buyer orders the seller to measure goods into the buyer's own container, valid receipt occurs through implicit agency, even if the buyer is absent.

Core Idea
When the container belongs to the buyer, the seller becomes an agent by necessity, and receipt is valid.

The previous lesson established that measuring into another's container does not constitute receipt. However, this passage carves out a crucial exception: if the container belongs to the buyer and the buyer orders the seller to measure into it, the buyer becomes a possessor even if absent. The commentary explains this through the concept of implicit agency (wikalah darurah), where the seller holds the container as an agent for the buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptional scenario
The exception is explicitly stated: 'Contrary to measuring in the buyer's container by his order.' In an ordinary sale of a specific item, if the buyer orders the seller to measure into the buyer's own container, the buyer becomes a possessor.
This only applies to ordinary sale (bay') of a specific 'ayn, not salam.
2
Understand the legal basis: ownership of the specific item
In an ordinary sale, the buyer owns the specific wheat immediately upon contract. Therefore, his order to measure encounters his own property, making his order valid.
In salam, the buyer does not own a specific item, so the exception does not apply.
3
Analyze the implicit agency
The buyer's valid order makes the seller an agent by necessity (wakil darurah) in holding the container. Although the seller cannot be explicitly appointed as an agent for receipt, the law recognizes implicit agency in this context.
The text addresses the objection: 'The seller cannot be an agent in receipt...' and answers that agency can be established implicitly.
4
Contrast with the general rule
If the container belongs to the seller, or if the container belongs to the buyer but is not in his possession (e.g., left with the seller), the receipt is invalid. The key is that the buyer's container must be under the buyer's control? Actually, the text says if the container is the buyer's but he hasn't taken possession? Wait: The text says 'if it were the seller's and the buyer ordered... not valid because the buyer borrowed the container and did not take possession.' So for the exception, we need the container to be the buyer's AND presumably the buyer already has possession? Or does ordering the seller to measure into the buyer's container automatically give the buyer virtual possession? The text states: 'because he owned the specific item by purchase, so his order encountered his ownership, so he becomes a possessor by placing it in the container, and the seller becomes an agent in holding the container, so the container is in the hand of the buyer virtually.' So even if the container is in the seller's physical location, if it is the buyer's container, the buyer is considered to have virtual possession. This is a key nuance: ownership of the container gives virtual possession when the buyer orders the seller to fill it.
Contrast with the seller's container: even if the buyer orders, no virtual possession arises because the container belongs to the seller.
5
Apply the principle to other actions
The text provides analogies: if the buyer orders the seller to grind the wheat or throw it into the sea, the buyer bears the loss, indicating that the buyer is considered in control. This reinforces that the buyer's order transfers risk.
This is consistent with the principle that possession (qabd) can be constructive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان المسلم إليه في ظرف ربِّ السَّلم بأمرِهِ بغيبته أو كال البائع في ظرفه أو ظرف بَيْتِهِ بأمر المشتري لم يكن قبضاً ; لأنَّ في السَّلَم لم يصح أمرُ ربِّ السّلم بالكيل ; لأنَّ حقه في الدين لا في العين، فأمرُهُ لم يصادف ملكه، فالمسلم إليه جعلَ مَلكَهُ في ظرف استعارَهُ من ربِّ السَّلَم ، وفي البيع لم يصح أمر المشتري ; لأنَّه استعار الظرف من البائع، ولم يقضه، فيكونُ في يد البائع ، فكذا الحنطة التي فيه، وإنما قال : بغيبته حتى لو كان حاضراً يكون قبضاً ; لأنَّ فعلهُ ينتقل إليه. تحقق الاستبدال الذي هو حرام ; لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك، يلزم تمليك الشيء نسيئة، ألا ترى أنه لو باع كذا بكر لغير عينه لا يجوز. [٢] ١ ] قوله : لأن حقه...الخ ; يعني أنَّ حق ربِّ السلم في الدِّينِ لا في العين، والدين وصف ثابت في الذمة، فالأمر بالكيل لم يصادف ملك الأمر، بل إنَّما صادف عيناً مملوكاً للبائع. [٢] قوله : لأنه ... الخ ; حاصله : إنَّ المشتري ملك البر بنفس الشراء، فيصح أمره لمصادفته ملكه، فيكون قابضاً بجعله في الظرف ، ويكون البائع وكيلاً في إمساك الظرف ، فيكون الظرف في يد المشتري حكماً ، فكان الواقع فيه واقعاً في يده حكماً ; ولهذا اكتفى بذلك الكيل في الصحيح ، ألا ترى أنه لو أمره بالطَّحن لو بإلقائه في البحر، ففعل، يكون على الآمر في الشراء، ويتقرَّرُ الثمنُ عليه، وفي السلم على المأمور، لما قلنا. فإن قلت : البائع لا يصح أن يكون وكيلاً في القبض حتى لو وكله به لا يصح توكيله ولا يكون قابضاً له، فكيف يتصور أن يكون وكيلاً له هاهنا ؟
بخلاف كيله في ظرف المشتري بأمره
[۱] (بخلاف كيله في ظرف المشتري بأمره) : أي إذا اشترى حنطة معيَّنة، فأمر المشتري البائع أن يكيله في ظرف المشتري بغيبته، ففعل يصيرُ قابضاً ; لأنَّه مَلَكَ العين بالشراء، فأمرُهُ صادفَ مُلْكَه.

In an ordinary sale, if the buyer orders the seller to measure into the seller's container or the seller's house, it is also not valid receipt because the buyer has not taken possession of the container. The commentary elaborates: in the sale scenario, the buyer actually owns the specific wheat by the contract, so his order is valid in principle, but the condition of the container matters. The text then contrasts with the case where the buyer orders measuring into the buyer's own container: then it is valid receipt because the buyer's order encounters his ownership, and the seller becomes an agent by necessity. The restriction to the buyer's container is crucial; if the container belongs to the seller and the buyer has not taken possession of it, the measurement does not transfer possession. The passage also addresses an objection about agency in receipt, answering that agency can be established implicitly even if not explicitly intended.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Physical or legal possession; here virtual possession through implicit agency.
ظرف (zarf): Container; its ownership is key.
وكالة ضرورة (wikalah darurah): Agency by necessity; arises when the law recognizes an agent relationship to give effect to a valid order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal mechanism that makes the buyer a possessor when the seller pours goods into the buyer's container by the buyer's order?
A) The buyer's physical presence.
B) The seller becomes an agent by necessity.
C) The container is borrowed from the buyer.
D) The contract is a salam.

Key Takeaway
In an ordinary sale of a specific item, the buyer's order to measure into his own container creates valid receipt through implicit agency, even in the buyer's absence.

Mixing Specific and Salam Goods: Intra-Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on mixing specific and salam goods and apply the ruling to identify the legal consequences for the buyer.

Core Idea
Order of measurement determines whether mixing constitutes consumption, splitting the Hanafi school into three rulings.

This passage deals with a compound transaction: a buyer purchases specific wheat from a seller and also has a salam debt of wheat from the same seller. When both are measured into a single container, the order of measurement critically affects the validity of the sale. We will trace how Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani evaluate the mixing and the resulting options for the buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction Structure
Recognize that the buyer has two rights: ownership of specific purchased wheat and a debt of wheat from a salam contract. The seller measures both into a single container.
The specific wheat is already owned by the buyer; the salam wheat is still the seller's property until delivery.
2
Analyze the Ruling if Specific is Measured First
If the seller measures the specific wheat first, all three imams agree: the buyer becomes possessor of both the specific wheat and the salam debt. No dispute.
The salam debt becomes connected to the buyer's ownership by necessity.
3
Analyze the Ruling if Salam is Measured First
If the seller measures the salam debt first, the mixture triggers disagreement. Abu Hanifa deems the specific wheat consumed (istihlak), rescinding the sale. The Sahibayn give the buyer an option: rescind or become a partner in the mixture.
The key point is whether mixing constitutes consumption of the specific wheat.
4
Distinguish Between the Two Sahibayn
Within the Sahibayn, Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer becomes possessor of both goods, just as if specific was measured first. Muhammad holds that the buyer becomes possessor only of the specific wheat, making the two partners in the mixture.
Qadi Khan attributes the option view specifically to Muhammad.
5
Identify the Practical Consequences (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
The dispute directly affects ownership: under Abu Hanifa, the buyer loses the specific wheat and must reclaim the price; under Abu Yusuf, the buyer owns both; under Muhammad, the buyer shares ownership. This determines risk of loss and liability.
Later Hanafi fatwa may prefer the Sahibayn's view to avoid hardship to the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن بدأ بكيل العين المشترى في الظرف صار قابضاً للعين ; لصحة الأمر فيه، وللدين المسلم فيه ; لمصادفته ملكه كمَن استقرضَ حنطة وأمر المقرض أن يزرعها في أرضه.
وإن بدأ بالدين لم يَصِرْ قابضاً لشيء منهما ، أمَّا الدين ; فلعدم صحة الأمرِ فيه ، وأما العين ; فلانه خلطه بملكه قبل التسليم، فصار مستهلكاً عند أبي حنيفة ، فينتقض البيع، وهذا الخلط غير مرضي ; لجواز أن يكون مراده البداية بالعين ، وعندهما: المشتري بالخيار إن شاءَ نقض البيع، وإن شاء شاركه في المخلوط ; لأنَّ الخلط ليس باستهلاك عندهما ، وخصه القاضي خان بقول محمد . أما عند أبي يوسف له إذا بدأ بالدين يصيرُ قابضاً لهما جميعاً، كما لو بدأ بالعين، ضرورة اتصاله بملكه في الصورتين، إذ الخلط ليس باستهلاك. وقال محمد : يصير قابضاً للعين دون الدين، فيشتركان فيه، ولم يبرأ عن (۱) «منح الغفار» (ق٢ : ٦٥/أ - ب).
For if he began with measuring the purchased specific item in the container, he becomes a possessor of the specific item due to the validity of the order regarding it, and of the debt (salam) due to it encountering his ownership, like one who borrowed wheat and ordered the lender to sow it in his land. And if he began with the debt, he does not become a possessor of either of them. As for the debt, because the order regarding it is not valid; and as for the specific item, because he mixed it with his property before delivery, so it becomes consumed (mustahlakan) according to Abu Hanifa, so the sale is rescinded, and this mixing is not pleasing because it is possible that his intention was to start with the specific item. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the buyer has an option: if he wishes, he may rescind the sale, and if he wishes, he may become a partner in the mixture because mixing is not deemed consumption according to them. Qadi Khan specified it as the view of Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, if he began with the debt, he becomes a possessor of both, just as if he began with the specific item, due to the necessity of it being connected to his ownership in both cases, since mixing is not consumption. And Muhammad said: He becomes a possessor of the specific item only, not the debt, so they become partners in it, and he is not freed from (1) Munh al-Ghaffar (q2:65a-b).
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific case: a person has both a salam debt against the seller and also purchases a specific quantity of wheat from that same seller. He gives a container to the seller to measure both. If he starts by measuring the specific purchased wheat into the container, he becomes the possessor of both the specific wheat and the salam debt (the latter by virtue of it becoming connected to his ownership). If he starts by measuring the salam wheat first, there is disagreement. According to Abu Hanifa, he does not become possessor of either: the order for the salam is invalid, and mixing the specific wheat with the salam wheat before delivery consumes (istihlak) the specific wheat, thus the sale is rescinded. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), mixing is not consumption; the buyer has an option: rescind the sale or become a partner in the mixture. However, Qadi Khan specified that this option view belongs to Muhammad only, because Abu Yusuf holds a different position: if he starts with the salam, he becomes possessor of both, arguing that the mixing does not amount to consumption and both become connected to his ownership by necessity. Muhammad's view is that he becomes possessor of the specific item only, not the salam debt, so they become partners in the mixture, and he is not freed from the salam debt. The passage contains a footnote reference to Munh al-Ghaffar.

Key Terms
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption; here meaning that mixing the specific goods with the seller's goods before delivery destroys the specific identity, thus voiding the sale according to Abu Hanifa.
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing; the physical combination of two batches of wheat.
خيار (khiyar): Option; the buyer's right to choose between rescinding the sale or becoming a partner in the mixture according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is automatically rescinded.
B) The buyer becomes a possessor of both the specific and the salam goods.
C) The buyer has an option to rescind or become a partner.
D) The buyer becomes a partner only in the salam goods.

Key Takeaway
The effect of mixing specific and salam goods depends on whether mixing is considered consumption; the Hanafi school has three positions, with practical differences in ownership and liability.

Validity of Receipt When Measuring Debt and Specific Goods
Ruling on validity of delivery when measuring debt and specific goods together

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of receipt when measuring debt and specific goods together, and explain the intra-madhhab difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Receipt of debt requires buyer's ownership; mixing without valid receipt voids sale (Abu Hanifa) or gives option (Sahibayn).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq comments on a scenario where a buyer instructs the seller to measure both a debt (salam obligation) and a specific good into the buyer's container. The validity of receipt depends on which item is measured first, because the buyer's command is only effective if he already owns the item. We will examine the ruling and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases a debt (salam kurr) and a specific kurr. He orders the seller to put both in the buyer's container. Determine which item is measured first.
The order is a command to act as the buyer's agent for receipt.
2
Distinguish the two sequences
If the seller begins with the specific good, the buyer becomes a possessor. If he begins with the debt, the buyer does not become a possessor according to Abu Hanifa.
The ruling hinges on the validity of the buyer's command.
3
Understand the basis of command validity
The command is valid for the specific good because the buyer already owns it. For the debt, ownership is not yet established (the debt is in the seller's dhimmah), so the command is invalid.
One cannot delegate receipt of something one does not yet own.
4
Analyze the consequence of invalid receipt
Since the debt was not validly received, it remains in the seller's possession. When the seller later mixes it with the buyer's specific good, the mixture triggers a dispute: Abu Hanifa considers it consumption (istihlak) voiding the sale; the Sahibayn consider it a partnership giving the buyer an option.
The key issue is whether mixing equals istihlak.
5
Apply to analogous case
The same principle applies to a loan: if a borrower gives his container to the lender to measure the borrowed item while the borrower is absent, receipt is not valid because ownership of the loan occurs only after physical receipt.
The command to measure with the borrower's container is ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he measured debt and specific goods in the buyer's container: if he begins with the specific goods, he is a receiver; if he begins with the debt, he is not, according to Abu Hanifa. Explanation: when a man buys a kurr by salami contract and a specific kurr from another, and he orders the seller to put both kurrs in the buyer's container, if he begins with the sale of the specific goods, he becomes a receiver. As for the specific goods, due to the validity of the order; as for the debt, due to its connection to the buyer's property. If he begins with the debt, he does not become a receiver because the order is not valid for the debt, so he does not become a receiver of it; it remains in the seller's hand, and he mixes the buyer's property with his own; thus it becomes consumed according to Abu Hanifa, so the receipt and the sale are rescinded. According to the two companions, the buyer has an option: if he wishes, he may rescind the sale, and if he wishes, he may become a partner in the mixture, because mixing is not consumption according to them. Similarly, if a man borrows a kurr from another and gives him his container to measure it, and he does so while he is absent, he is not a receiver, because a loan is not owned before receipt, so the kurr remains the property of the lender, so the order is not valid. Thus in al-Bahr. [1] Commentary: 'due to the validity of the order' – because it coincides with property, so the act of the commanded is like the act of the commander. It was refuted that he cannot act as a deputy for the buyer in receipt, as if he expressly appointed him as an agent. And it was answered: it is established implicitly, even if not explicitly. Thus in al-Inaya. [2] Commentary: 'due to its connection to the buyer's property' – and by such he becomes a receiver, because receipt may be by his hand or by vacating, and may be by connection to his property. [3] Commentary: 'not valid' etc. – because coincidence with property is not established, since his right is in the debt, not in the specific goods, and this is a specific good, not a debt.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school concerning the validity of delivery when a seller measures both a debt (e.g., a salam obligation) and a specific good (eye) into the buyer's container. The key principle is that the buyer's command to the seller to act as his agent is only valid if the buyer actually owns the item being measured at the time of the command. Since the debt is not yet owned (it is a liability in the seller's dhimmah), the buyer cannot authorize the seller to receive it on his behalf. If the seller begins with the specific good, the buyer becomes a possessor of that good, and the subsequent measuring of the debt into the same container attaches it to the buyer's property, making him a possessor of the debt as well. If he begins with the debt first, the buyer does not become a possessor, and the seller's mixing of the goods is considered consumption (istihlak) according to Abu Hanifa, voiding the sale. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that mixing is not consumption; the buyer may choose to rescind or become a partner. The passage also extends this rule to a loan: if a borrower gives his container to the lender to measure the loaned item in his absence, the borrower does not take possession because ownership of the loan occurs only after physical receipt.

Key Terms
العين (al-'ayn): The specific, tangible good that is owned and present (as opposed to debt).
الدين (al-dayn): A debt or liability, something owed but not yet delivered, existing in the dhimmah.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or delivery, physically or constructively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The sale is void because the debt is considered consumed by mixing.
C) The buyer has a choice to rescind or become a partner.
D) The seller must repeat the measurement.

Key Takeaway
The validity of receipt depends on the buyer's ownership at the time of the command; an invalid command for debt leads to a dispute on the effect of mixing.

Ruling on Mutual Rescission in Salam When the Capital Dies
Ruling on iqalah when capital dies after receipt, with extension to barter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that iqalah is valid even if the capital perishes, as long as the muslam fih remains in the seller's liability, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Iqalah depends on the subject matter (muslam fih), not the price; so capital death doesn't invalidate rescission.

This lesson examines a specific ruling on mutual rescission (iqalah) in a salam contract where the capital is a female slave. The passage distinguishes two scenarios: death of the slave after the iqalah, and death before the iqalah. The key principle is that iqalah is a dissolution of the contract, and its validity hinges on the continued existence of the subject matter (al-ma'qud alayh), which in salam is the commodity to be delivered (al-muslam fih), not the capital. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and its application to barter (muqayada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two cases: (1) slave dies after iqalah, (2) slave dies before iqalah. Note the difference in the timing of death relative to the rescission.
2
Determine the subject matter in salam
In salam, the muslam fih (the future commodity) is the subject matter of the contract, not the capital. The capital is like the price.
This is crucial for understanding the ruling.
3
Apply the condition for iqalah validity
Iqalah is valid only if the subject matter (al-ma'qud alayh) exists at the time of rescission. Since the muslam fih (the kurr) remains in the seller's dhimma, iqalah is valid in both scenarios.
The condition is on the subject matter, not the capital.
4
Handle the slave's death
In scenario 1, the slave died after iqalah; so the seller must pay its value because he cannot return it. In scenario 2, the slave died before iqalah; the iqalah is still valid because the muslam fih remains.
Value assessed on day of receipt due to liability like ghasp.
5
Extend to muqayada
The same ruling applies to barter, where each item is both sold and price in different respects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he gives a female slave as capital in salam for a kurr, and she is received, then they mutually rescind, and she dies in his hand and the kurr remains, its value on the day of receipt is obligatory. Commentary: 'And if he gives a female slave as capital in salam for a kurr, and she is received, then they mutually rescind, and she dies in his hand and the kurr remains, its value on the day of receipt is obligatory' means: he bought a kurr by salam contract, made the slave the capital, delivered her to the seller (muslam ilayh), then they rescinded the salam contract, then the slave died in the seller's hand, the rescission remains, so the value of the slave must be paid by the seller to return it to the buyer (rabb al-salam). 'And if she dies and then they mutually rescind, it is valid' means: in the aforementioned scenario, if the death occurs before the rescission, the rescission is valid, because the validity of rescission depends on the survival of the subject matter of the contract, which is the muslam fih (the kurr). [1] Commentary: 'and she is received' – this is a condition because if they part without receipt of her, the rescission is invalid due to the invalidity of the salam. [2] Commentary: 'because...' – summary: the condition for validity of rescission is the survival of the contract, which remains by the survival of the subject matter. The subject matter in salam is the muslam fih, which remains in the seller's liability after the slave's death. So when the contract is rescinded, he must return the slave, but due to inability by her death, he must pay her value. The value is assessed on the day of receipt because that is the cause of liability, like usurpation. [3] Commentary: 'validity of rescission depends...' – because the slave is the capital, which is like the price in the contract, and the sold item is the muslam fih. Validity of rescission depends on the existence of the sold item, not the price. So the slave's death does not change the status of the rescission: in the first case (death after rescission) the rescission remains, in the second (death before rescission) it is valid. [4] Commentary: 'depends' – because rescission is a dissolution of the contract, which cannot happen without the contract. [5] Commentary: 'in both aspects' – meaning death after rescission and rescission after death, because each of the two items (slave and kurr) is a sold item in one respect and a price in another. For the remaining item, the sold aspect is considered, and for the perished item, the price aspect.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effect of mutual rescission (iqalah) in a salam contract where the capital (ra's al-mal) is a female slave. The key principle is that iqalah is a dissolution of the original contract, and its validity depends on the continued existence of the subject matter (al-ma'qud 'alayh). In salam, the subject matter is the muslam fih (the commodity to be delivered later), not the capital. The capital is treated like the price (thaman). Therefore, if the slave dies after the iqalah, the iqalah remains valid, but the seller must pay the slave's value because he is unable to return the slave itself. The value is assessed on the day of receipt because that is when liability attached, akin to ghasp. If the slave dies before the iqalah, the iqalah is still valid because the muslam fih (the kurr) still exists in the seller's liability. The passage also notes that the same ruling applies to barter (muqayada), where each item is both sold and price from one perspective.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of both parties.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or principal delivered immediately in a salam contract.
المسلم فيه (al-muslam fih): The commodity to be delivered in the future under a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a slave given as salam capital dies after iqalah, what must the seller pay?
A) Nothing, because the contract is rescinded.
B) The value of the slave on the day of receipt.
C) The value of the slave on the day of death.
D) A similar slave in kind.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah in salam remains valid even if the capital perishes, because its validity depends on the survival of the muslam fih, not the capital.

Iqala in Salam When the Capital Dies
Ruling and rationale for iqala in salam with capital destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on iqala in salam when the capital perishes and explain why the sequence of events (death before/after iqala) matters.

Core Idea
Iqala in salam depends on the musallam fih, not the capital – the capital's death does not invalidate iqala.

In salam, the capital can be a specific commodity like a slave-girl. If the parties cancel the contract by mutual consent (iqala), what happens if the capital is destroyed? The Hanafi school distinguishes between destruction before and after iqala, based on the nature of iqala as a rescission and the identity of the subject of the contract. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two sequences: (a) iqala occurs, then the capital dies; (b) capital dies, then iqala occurs. Note the different rulings: in (a) the iqala remains and the value is due; in (b) the iqala is valid.
The ruling depends on what is considered the subject of the contract (ma'qud alayh).
2
Recognise the key principle
Iqala is a rescission (faskh). Its validity requires that the contract still exist. The contract exists as long as the subject (musallam fih) remains in the dhimma. The capital is like the price, not the subject.
Hanafis treat iqala as a rescission, not a new sale.
3
Apply to scenario (a): death after iqala
Since iqala rescinds the contract, the musallam ilaihi must return the capital. If it perishes after iqala, he is liable for its value on the day of possession, because he cannot return it in kind.
Value is assessed at the time of possession because that is when liability arose.
4
Apply to scenario (b): death before iqala
Even if the capital perishes before iqala, the contract still exists because the musallam fih (the deferred goods) remains a debt in the dhimma. Iqala rescinds the contract, so it is valid.
The capital's existence is irrelevant for iqala; only the musallam fih matters.
5
Extend to muqayada
In barter, each item is both price and subject. The text states the same ruling applies: if one item perishes after iqala, the cancellation stands and value is due; if before, iqala is valid.
The reasoning is parallel: the subject that remains a debt (dhimma) determines validity.

(۳) قوله : صحة الإقالة تعتمد...الخ ; لأنَّ الجارية رأس المال، وهو في حكم الثمن في العقد، والمبيع هو المسلّمُ فيه، وصحة الإقالة تعتمد قيام المبيع دون الثَّمن، فهلاك الأمة لا يغيِّرُ حال الإقالة من البقاء في الأولى والصحة في الثانية، كذا في «الدرر»، وغيره.

أي إذا باع أمة بعرض فهلك أحدهما دون الآخر فتقايلا صح التقايلُ، ولو تقايلا ثُمَّ هلك أحدهما بقي التَّقابل ، فقولُه : وكذا... إلى آخره، تقديره بقي تقايل المقايضة، وصح تقايلها في كلا الوجهين أما البقاء ففي صورة تقدم الثقايل على الهلاك. وأمَّا الصَّحَّةُ ففي صورة تأخره عنه.
If he gives a slave-girl as capital in salam for a kurr, and she is taken possession, then they cancel (iqala), and she dies in his hand while the cancellation remains, her value on the day of taking possession must be given. And if she dies, then they cancel, it is valid. Similarly, exchange (muqayada) in both aspects. Commentary: (If he gives a slave-girl as capital in salam for a kurr and she is taken possession, then they cancel, so she dies in his hand while the cancellation remains, her value on the day of taking possession must be given): Meaning he purchased time with a salam contract, made the slave-girl the capital, handed her to the musallam ilaihi, then they cancelled the salam contract, then the slave-girl died in the hand of the musallam ilaihi while the cancellation remained, so the value of the slave-girl is obligatory on the musallam ilaihi to return to the rabb al-salam. (And if she dies, then they cancel, it is valid): In the mentioned picture, if the death was before the cancellation, the cancellation is valid; because the validity of iqala depends on the continued existence of the ma'qud alayh, which is the musallam fih. (Similarly, exchange in both aspects): [1] His statement: 'and she is taken possession' – he restricted it because if they separate without taking possession, iqala is not valid due to the salam not being valid, as in 'al-Nahr'. [2] His statement: 'because...' – the summary: the condition for validity of iqala is the continued existence of the contract, and it continues with the continued existence of the ma'qud alayh, and the ma'qud alayh in salam is the musallam fih, which remains in the dhimma of the musallam ilaihi after the destruction of the slave-girl. As if the contract is rescinded, he must return the slave-girl, and being unable due to her death, he must give her value, just as if they had taken possession and then cancelled after one died, or one died after iqala. The day of taking possession is considered because it is the cause of liability like usurpation, as in 'al-Manh'. (3) His statement: 'the validity of iqala depends' – because the slave-girl is the capital, and it is in the position of the price in the contract, and the sold item is the musallam fih. The validity of iqala depends on the existence of the sold item, not the price. So the destruction of the slave-girl does not change the state of iqala from continued existence in the first case and validity in the second, as in 'al-Durar' and others. [4] His statement: 'depends' – because iqala is the rescission of the contract, and that cannot be without the contract. [0] His statement: 'in both aspects' – meaning death after iqala, and iqala after death; because each one is from one aspect a sold item and from another aspect a price. So in the remaining case the sold item is considered, and in the destruction the price aspect. That is, if he sold a slave-girl for a commodity and one of them perished without the other, and they cancelled, the iqala is valid; and if they cancelled and then one perished, the cancellation remains. So his statement: 'similarly...' etc., its meaning: the iqala of muqayada remains, and its iqala is valid in both aspects: as for remaining, in the case of iqala preceding destruction; and as for validity, in the case of iqala following it.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter on salam (advance payment sale). The matn gives two scenarios: (1) If the capital (a slave-girl) is taken possession and then the contract is canceled by mutual consent (iqala), and after that the slave-girl dies in the hands of the musallam ilaihi (the seller who received the capital), the cancellation remains in effect, and the musallam ilaihi must pay the value of the slave-girl on the day he took possession. (2) If the slave-girl dies first, and then they cancel, the iqala is valid. The commentator explains the reasoning: iqala is a rescission of the contract, and its validity depends on the continued existence of the subject of the contract (ma'qud alayh). In salam, the subject is the musallam fih (the deferred goods), not the capital (the slave-girl). The capital is like the price (thaman) in an ordinary sale. Therefore, as long as the musallam fih remains in the dhimma (liability) of the musallam ilaihi, the contract exists and iqala can take place. If the capital perishes after iqala, the musallam ilaihi is liable for its value because he cannot return it. If it perishes before iqala, the iqala is still valid because the real subject (musallam fih) still exists. The same ruling applies to muqayada (barter) where the slave-girl is exchanged for another commodity, because in barter each item is both price and subject from a certain perspective. The commentator also notes that the ruling requires that the capital was already taken possession before separation, otherwise the salam contract itself would be invalid.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqala): Termination of a contract by mutual consent, treated as a rescission (faskh).
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid in a salam contract; in this passage, a slave-girl used as capital.
المسلم فيه (al-musallam fih): The deferred goods to be delivered in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The slave-girl is replaced by another of equal value.
D) Nothing is due because the capital was destroyed.

Key Takeaway
In salam iqala, the validity hinges on the musallam fih; the capital's destruction does not invalidate the iqala.

Iqala in Ordinary Sale: The Necessity of the Sold Item's Existence
Hanafi ruling on iqala after destruction of sold item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqala when the sold item perishes in an ordinary sale and identify the condition that makes iqala invalid.

Core Idea
Iqala requires the sold item to exist; if it is destroyed, the rescission is void.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of iqala (mutual rescission of a sale) depends on the existence of the sold item. This lesson unpacks a short passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that clarifies the rule for ordinary sales: if the item perishes, iqala becomes impossible. Understanding this condition helps distinguish ordinary sales from salam contracts, where the ruling differs due to the nature of the subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes an ordinary sale of a slave-girl for dirhams or dinars. Two temporal sequences are considered: (a) iqala occurs first, then the slave-girl dies; (b) the slave-girl dies first, then iqala occurs.
Focus on the order of events.
2
State the ruling for each sequence
In case (a), the iqala does not remain effective after death. In case (b), the iqala is invalid from the start. So regardless of sequence, the death of the slave-girl nullifies the iqala.
Ruling: invalid in both orders.
3
Identify the underlying reason
The slave-girl is the 'asl' (original subject) of the sale. For a rescission to be valid, the subject of the sale must exist at the time of iqala. Since the slave-girl is destroyed, there is no locus for iqala to operate on.
Reason: existence of the sold item is a condition.
4
Contrast with salam
The passage begins with 'contrary to purchase with price' – meaning that this ruling differs from salam. In salam, the capital paid (ra's al-mal) is analogous to the price, not the subject; the subject (musallam fih) is a debt. Therefore, the destruction of the capital does not have the same effect. This contrast helps solidify the condition for ordinary sales.
Contrast highlights the unique condition in ordinary sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الشراء بالثمن فيهما : أي إن اشترى بالدراهم أو الدنانير أمةً ، ثُمَّ تقايلا، ثم ماتت الأمة في يد المشتري لم يبقَ التَّقايل، ولو ماتت ثم تقايلا لا يصح التقايل. قوله : فيهما ; أي في المسألتين ; لأنَّ الأمة هي الأصل في البيع، فلا تبقى بعد الهلاك، فلا تصح الإقالة ابتداء، ولا تبقى انتهاء بعدم محلها.
Contrary to purchase with price (thaman) in both aspects: That is, if he bought a slave-girl with dirhams or dinars, then they cancelled, then the slave-girl died in the hand of the buyer, the cancellation does not remain; and if she died and then they cancelled, the iqala is not valid. His statement: 'in both aspects' means in the two cases; because the slave-girl is the original subject in sale, so she does not remain after destruction, so iqala is not valid initially, nor does it remain eventually due to lack of its locus.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This short passage contrasts the rule for salam with that for ordinary sale. In an ordinary sale where a slave-girl is bought with cash (dirhams or dinars), if the slave-girl dies in the buyer's hands after iqala, the iqala does not remain valid; if she dies before iqala, the iqala is also invalid. The reason is that in ordinary sale, the slave-girl herself is the subject of the sale (al-mabee). For iqala to be valid, the subject must exist. Since the slave-girl is gone, there is nothing to rescind. This differs from salam where the capital (slave-girl) is not the subject; the subject is the musallam fih. Hence, the ruling for salam is different. The phrase 'فيهما' refers to both cases: death after iqala and death before iqala.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by both parties.
الثمن (al-thaman): Price paid in an ordinary sale (here dirhams or dinars).
الأصل (al-asl): The original subject or basis; here, the slave-girl is the core of the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The iqala is invalid because the slave-girl no longer exists.
C) The buyer must pay the value of the slave-girl.
D) The seller must provide another slave-girl.

Key Takeaway
For a valid iqala in an ordinary sale, the sold item must exist at the time of rescission; destruction of the item renders the iqala invalid regardless of the order of events.

Dispute Over Conditions of Defect and Credit in Salam
Intra-Hanafi dispute on procedural rule in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over whose statement is accepted when salam parties dispute a condition (defect or credit) and apply the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Imam presumes validity from avoiding sin; Sahibayn prioritize denial of imposed rights.

When two Muslims disagree over whether a condition was part of their salam contract, the law must decide whom to believe. The Hanafi school has a nuanced internal debate: Imam Abu Hanifa consistently accepts the party who asserts the condition (claiming validity), while his two disciples distinguish between conditions that impose a right and those that do not. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and its practical effect on salam disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Two parties to a salam contract differ: one claims a condition (defective quality or credit period) was agreed; the other denies it. Determine who is the claimant and who is the denier.
The condition may be shart al-rada'a (defective goods) or al-ajal (credit period).
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Imam accepts the claimant's statement in all such disputes. His reasoning: the claimant is asserting that the contract is valid with the condition; the default assumption (zahir) is that Muslims avoid invalid contracts (which are sinful), so the claimant is believed.
This applies regardless of whether the claimant is rabb al-salam or musallam ilaihi.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its basis
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) distinguish: if the claimed condition imposes a right against the other party (e.g., credit period delays payment), the denier's statement is accepted because he denies a right (inkar haqq). If no right is imposed (e.g., defective quality), the claimant's statement is accepted.
Their principle: when speech is litigation, the denier is believed if he denies a right; otherwise the claimant is believed.
4
Analyze the root of disagreement
Both sides agree on the general rule that the claimant of validity is believed in a single contract dispute. But Imam treats both conditions as claims of validity, while the Sahibayn see credit as a right that triggers inkar haqq exception.
The key difference is whether the condition is seen as part of contract description (wasf) or as an additional obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider a case: musallam ilaihi claims a credit period, rabb al-salam denies. Under Imam, musallam ilaihi's claim is accepted (contract valid with credit). Under Sahibayn, rabb al-salam's denial is accepted (no credit, contract may be valid without it).
The dispute affects the outcome: whether the credit period is enforced or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلف عاقدا السّلم في شرط الرداءة والأجل، فالقول لمدعيهما
أي قال المسلم إليه : شرطنا الرَّديء، وقال ربُّ السّلم : لم نشترط شيئاً حتى يكونُ العقد فاسداً ، فالقولُ قولُ المسلم إليه) (۱) قوله : فيهما ; أي في المسألتين ; لأنَّ الأمة هي الأصل في البيع، فلا تبقى بعد الهلاك، فلا تصح الإقالة ابتداء، ولا تبقى انتهاء بعدم محلها. (٢) قوله : ولو اختلف...الخ ; يعني لو قال أحد المتعاقدين : شرطنا طعاماً رديئاً، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط ، أو قال أحدهما : شرطنا التأجيل، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط شيئاً، فالقول لمدعي شرط الرداءة والأجل مطلقاً، سواء كان مدعيهما ربُّ السّلم أو المُسَلَّم إليه عند الإمام ; لأنَّ المدعي يدعي الصحة، فكان القول قوله وإن أنكر خصمه، إذ الظاهر شاهد له ; لأنَّ العقد الفاسد معصية، والظَّاهِرُ من حالِ المُسَلَّم التحرز عنه. وقالا : القــول للمنكر إن كان المنكرُ ربُّ السّلم فيما إذا ادعى المسلم إليه التأجيل ; لأنه ينكر حقاً عليه، وهو الأجل، أو كان المنكرُ المُسلَّم إليه فيما إذا ادعى التأجيل ; لأنه منكر. والأصل: أنَّ من خرج كلامُهُ تعتاً فالقولُ لصاحيه بالاتفاق، وإن خرج خصومة بأن ينكر ما يضرُّهُ مع اتفاقهما على عقد واحد، فالقول لمدعي الصحة، وعندهما: القولُ للمنكر، سواء أنكر الصحة أو غيرها. (۳) قوله : فالقول قول المسلّم إليه ; وقد يستدل عليه بأن العاقدين متفقان على عقد واحد، ومختلفان في ما لا بدَّ منه في صحةِ السلم، أعني بيان الوصف، ومن ادعى
And if the two parties to salam dispute over the condition of defect (rada'a) and credit (ajal), then the statement is for the claimant of both. That is, the musallam ilaihi said: 'We stipulated defective (goods),' and rabb al-salam said: 'We did not stipulate anything, so the contract would be invalid.' So the statement is the statement of the musallam ilaihi. (1) His statement: 'in both' means in the two cases; because the slave-girl is the original in sale, so she does not remain after destruction, so the iqala is not valid initially, nor does it remain eventually due to lack of its locus. (2) His statement: 'And if they differ...' meaning if one of the two parties said: 'We stipulated defective food,' and the other said: 'We did not stipulate,' or one said: 'We stipulated credit,' and the other said: 'We did not stipulate anything,' then the statement is for the claimant of the condition of defect and credit absolutely, whether the claimant is rabb al-salam or the musallam ilaihi according to Imam (Abu Hanifa); because the claimant claims validity, so his statement is accepted even if the opponent denies, since the apparent (zahir) supports him; because an invalid contract is a sin, and the apparent state of the Muslim is to avoid it. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The statement is for the denier if the denier is rabb al-salam when the musallam ilaihi claims credit, because he denies a right against him, which is the credit; or if the denier is the musallam ilaihi when credit is claimed, because he is a denier. The principle: If one's speech comes out as opposition (litigation), then the statement is for the other by agreement; and if it comes out as litigation by denying what harms him while they agree on one contract, then the statement is for the claimant of validity. And according to the two, the statement is for the denier, whether he denies validity or something else. (3) His statement: 'The statement is the statement of the musallam ilaihi' – and it may be argued that the two parties agree on one contract and differ over what is necessary for the validity of salam, namely the description (wasf), and the one who claims...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (dispute within the school) concerning a situation where the parties to a salam contract differ over whether a condition was agreed upon – specifically, a condition of defective quality (rada'a) or a credit period (ajal). The matn states that the statement of the claimant (of the condition) is accepted. The commentator explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, whoever claims that a condition was made (whether rabb al-salam or musallam ilaihi) is believed, because he is claiming that the contract is valid (since the condition is part of the contract description). The justification is that the default assumption (zahir) is that a Muslim avoids invalid contracts, which are sinful. Therefore, the one who claims the condition is effectively claiming the contract is valid, and the other's denial is not accepted. The two disciples (Sahibayn – Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ. They distinguish cases: if the claim of a condition imposes a right against the other party (e.g., credit imposes a delay right against rabb al-salam), then the denier's statement is accepted. If the claim of a condition does not impose a right (e.g., claiming defective goods), then the claimant's statement is accepted. Their principle is based on whether the speech of the denier is mere denial of a right (inkar haqq) or a claim of validity. The commentator also mentions a general principle: if one's speech is opposition (khasaamah, i.e., litigation), the other is believed; if it is a denial that harms him, the claimant of validity is believed according to Imam, while the Sahibayn believe the denier is believed. The passage ends abruptly, but it is clear that Imam's position is that the claimant of the condition is always believed, while the Sahibayn make a distinction based on the type of condition.

Key Terms
شرط الرداءة (shart al-rada'a): Condition that the goods to be delivered in salam are of defective quality.
الأجل (al-ajal): Credit period agreed upon in a salam contract.
المنكر (al-munkir): The party who denies a claim or right asserted by the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract dispute over a credit period, who does the Sahibayn believe?
A) The claimant of the credit period, because he claims validity
B) The denier, because he denies a right against himself
C) The rabb al-salam always, because he is the buyer
D) The musallam ilaihi always, because he is the seller

Key Takeaway
When salam parties dispute a condition, Imam accepts the claimant of any condition (presumption of validity), while the Sahibayn accept the denier if the condition imposes a right against him.

Ruling on Iqalah in Muqayadah When One Item Perishes
Explains ruling on rescinding a barter sale after partial loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqalah in a barter sale when one item perishes and distinguish it from the ruling in a regular sale.

Core Idea
In barter, iqalah remains valid even after one item is destroyed because each item serves as both price and commodity.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies the ruling on mutual rescission (iqalah) in a barter contract (muqayadah) when one of the exchanged items perishes. It presents two scenarios: rescission after destruction and rescission before destruction. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct ruling in actual transactions. The commentator also contrasts it with a regular sale using currency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for iqalah in muqayadah
First, determine whether the iqalah occurs after one item has perished, or before any destruction. These two cases are treated differently.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In muqayadah, if the rescission happens after one item perishes, it is valid. If it happens before destruction and then one item perishes, the rescission remains effective.
The key is that in barter, each item is both price and commodity, so the contract is not entirely destroyed by partial loss.
3
Contrast with a regular sale involving currency (dirhams/dinars)
In a regular sale, if the purchased slave-girl dies, iqalah is invalid because the subject matter (the slave-girl) is gone. The price (currency) is not subject to destruction in the same way.
The contrast highlights the unique nature of barter contracts.
4
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that in muqayadah, since each item functions as both price and commodity, the destruction of one does not eliminate the entire subject matter of the rescission. Hence iqalah remains possible.
This is the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الشراء بالثمن فيهما أي إذا باع أمة بعرض فهلك أحدهما دون الآخر فتقايلا صح التقايلُ، ولو تقايلا ثُمَّ هلك أحدهما بقي التَّقابل ، فقولُه : وكذا... إلى آخره، تقديره بقي تقايل المقايضة، وصح تقايلها في كلا الوجهين أما البقاء ففي صورة تقدم الثقايل على الهلاك. وأمَّا الصَّحَّةُ ففي صورة تأخره عنه. (بخلاف الشراء بالثَّمن فيهما ) : أي إن اشترى بالدراهم أو الدنانير أمةً ، ثُمَّ تقايلا، ثم ماتت الأمة في يد المشتري لم يبقَ التَّقايل، ولو ماتت ثم تقايلا لا يصح التقايل. (۱) قوله : فيهما ؛ أي في المسألتين ؛ لأنَّ الأمة هي الأصل في البيع، فلا تبقى بعد الهلاك، فلا تصح الإقالة ابتداء، ولا تبقى انتهاء بعدم محلها.
Contrary to purchase with a price in both [cases]: i.e., if he sold a slave-girl for a commodity, and one of the two [items] perished without the other, and they mutually rescinded, the rescission is valid. And if they mutually rescinded, then one of them perished, the rescission remains. So his statement (may God have mercy on him): 'and likewise... to the end,' its import is: the rescission of the exchange remains, and its rescission is valid in both respects. As for the remaining, it is in the case where the rescission preceded the destruction. As for the validity, it is in the case where it was delayed after it. (Contrary to purchase with the price in both [cases]): i.e., if he bought with dirhams or dinars a slave-girl, then they mutually rescinded, then the slave-girl died in the hand of the buyer, the rescission does not remain; and if she died then they rescinded, the rescission is not valid. (1) His statement: 'in both [cases]'; i.e., in the two issues, because the slave-girl is the principal in the sale, so it does not remain after destruction, thus the rescission is not valid initially, nor does it remain ultimately due to the absence of its subject matter.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Hidayah, expanded upon by the commentator. It clarifies the ruling on mutual rescission (iqalah) in a barter contract (muqayadah) when one of the exchanged items perishes. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) If the rescission occurs after one item has perished, the rescission is valid. (2) If the rescission occurs before any destruction, and then one of the items perishes, the rescission remains effective (the contract is effectively unwound from the time of the iqalah). The commentator explains that in muqayadah, each item serves as both price and commodity, so the contract is treated as a single exchange; therefore the iqalah is valid even after partial destruction. In contrast, in a regular sale with a price (such as dirhams or dinars), the price is not subject to destruction in the same way, so if the purchased slave-girl dies, the iqalah is invalid because the subject matter of the sale is gone. The footnote clarifies that 'in both [cases]' refers to the two scenarios of iqalah after destruction and iqalah before destruction, emphasizing that the slave-girl is the principal object of the sale, so its destruction nullifies the possibility of rescission.

Key Terms
تقايل (taqāyul / iqālah): Mutual rescission of a contract, whereby both parties agree to undo the sale and return the exchanged items.
مقايضة (muqāyaḍah): A barter sale where goods are exchanged for goods, not for money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid
B) Invalid
C) Valid only if the remaining item is returned immediately
D) Invalid because the contract becomes void automatically

Key Takeaway
In barter, iqalah remains valid after partial destruction because each item is both price and commodity, unlike a regular sale where destruction of the commodity invalidates rescission.

Whose Statement is Accepted: Dispute over Conditions in Salam
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidentiary rule in salam contract condition disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whose claim is accepted when parties in a salam contract dispute a condition, and apply the distinction between muta‘annit and mukhaṣim.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa: claimant of the condition is believed; Sahibayn: denier is believed.

Salam contracts require precise specification of quality and delivery time. When the parties disagree on whether such specifications were stipulated, whose word prevails? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and the key concepts of obstinate denial vs. genuine dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Parties in salam differ: one claims a condition (quality or term) was stipulated, the other denies. Determine what is at stake—validity of the contract depends on the condition.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa accepts the statement of the claimant (who asserts the condition). His dalil: the claimant asserts validity; invalid contracts are sinful; apparent state of Muslims is to avoid sin.
The claimant is not being obstinate but rather claiming what benefits him.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad accept the statement of the denier. Their dalil: the denier denies a right upon himself or simply denies; in disputes the denier is generally preferred.
They do not distinguish between obstinate and genuine denial here.
4
Understand the refinement: muta‘annit vs. mukhaṣim
Abu Hanifa's view is refined by the commentary: if the denier is muta‘annit (denying what benefits him), his statement is rejected; if he is mukhaṣim (denying what harms him), his statement may be accepted. In salam cases, the denier denies a condition that benefits him, so he is muta‘annit.
This explains why Abu Hanifa's rule leads to the claimant's statement being taken.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifa, the contract is enforced with the claimed condition. According to the Sahibayn, the contract is enforced without the condition (or invalid if no condition existed). The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position.
Most Hanafi fatwa manuals follow Abu Hanifa on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٢) قوله : ولو اختلف...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال أحد المتعاقدين : شرطنا طعاماً رديئاً، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط ، أو قال أحدهما : شرطنا التأجيل، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط شيئاً، فالقول لمدعي شرط الرداءة والأجل مطلقاً، سواء كان مدعيهما ربُّ السّلم أو المُسَلَّم إليه عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ المدعي يدعي الصحة، فكان القول قوله وإن أنكر خصمه، إذ الظاهر شاهد له ؛ لأنَّ العقد الفاسد معصية، والظَّاهِرُ من حالِ المُسَلَّم التحرز عنه. وقالا : القــول للمنكر إن كان المنكرُ ربُّ السّلم فيما إذا ادعى المسلم إليه التأجيل ؛ لأنه ينكر حقاً عليه، وهو الأجل، أو كان المنكرُ المُسلَّم إليه فيما إذا ادعى التأجيل ؛ لأنه منكر. والأصل: أنَّ من خرج كلامُهُ تعتاً فالقولُ لصاحيه بالاتفاق، وإن خرج خصومة بأن ينكر ما يضرُّهُ مع اتفاقهما على عقد واحد، فالقول لمدعي الصحة، وعندهما: القولُ للمنكر، سواء أنكر الصحة أو غيرها. (۳) قوله : فالقول قول المسلّم إليه ؛ وقد يستدل عليه بأن العاقدين متفقان على عقد واحد، ومختلفان في ما لا بدَّ منه في صحةِ السلم، أعني بيان الوصف، ومن ادعى بيان الوصف ادعى صحة العقد، والظاهرُ من حالِ العاقدين أنهما باشرا . عقداً صحيحاً لا فاسداً متحرزاً عن المعصية، والقول يكون لمن يشهد له الظاهر ؛ لكونه أقرب إلى الصدق والصواب. [١] قوله : متعنت ؛ المتعنت لغة : : من يطلب العنت، وهو وقوع الإنسان فيما لا يستطيع الخروج عنه، والمراد بالمتعنتِ شرعاً : مَن ينكر ما ينفعه، والمخاصم من ينكر ما يضره ، ذكره العلامة السغناقي له ناقلا عن «الفوائد الظهيرية». [٢]قوله : زائد على رأس المال ؛ فإن قيل : لا نسلم بل رأس المالِ خيرٌ وإن قل من المسلم فيه وإن جلّ ؛ لأنَّ ذلك نقد، وهذا نسيئة ، قلنا : نعم ؛ كذلك إلا أنَّ ذلك متروك هاهنا بالعرف والعادة. فإنَّ الناس مع وفورِ عقولهم يقدمون على عقدِ السّلم، وما ذلك إلا لفائدة رأوها فيه، فكان إلغاء مزية النقدِ بمقابلة فائدة زائدة رأوها في المسلم فيه، مع كونه نسيئة، كذا في «النهاية».
