[٤] قوله : لأنه يدعي الصحة ؛ وإن كان صاحبه منكراً، وعندهما: القولُ للمسلم إليه ؛ لأنَّه منكر، وإن أنكر الصحة ، كذا في «الهداية»). (۱) ((الهداية» (۳ : ۷۷)
And if the two contracting parties in salam differ over a condition of poor quality or a term, then the statement is of the one who claims them [i.e., the conditions]. i.e., the Muslim 'alayhi (the party obliged to deliver) said: we stipulated inferior quality, and the rabb al-salam (the buyer) said: we did not stipulate anything, so the contract would be invalid, then the statement is of the Muslim 'alayhi. (1) His statement: 'in both [cases]'; i.e., in the two issues; because the slave-girl is the principal in the sale, so it does not remain after destruction... (2) His statement: 'and if they differ... etc.'; meaning if one of the two contracting parties says: we stipulated inferior food, and the other says: we did not stipulate, or one says: we stipulated a term, and the other says: we did not stipulate anything, then the statement is of the one who claims the condition of inferior quality and the term absolutely, whether the claimant is the rabb al-salam or the Muslim 'alayhi according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa]; because the claimant claims validity, so his statement is taken even if his opponent denies it, since the apparent circumstance witnesses for him; because an invalid contract is a sin, and the apparent state of the Muslim is to avoid sin. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the statement is of the denier, if the denier is the rabb al-salam when the Muslim 'alayhi claims a term, because he denies a right upon him, which is the term; or if the denier is the Muslim 'alayhi when the term is claimed, because he is a denier. The principle: if one's speech emerges as obstinate (ta'attun), then the statement is of the other by agreement; and if it emerges as a dispute (khusumah) by denying what harms him while they agree on a single contract, then the statement is of the one who claims validity. According to them (the two), the statement is of the denier, whether he denies validity or something else. (3) His statement: 'so the statement is of the Muslim 'alayhi'; and it may be inferred that the two contracting parties agree on one contract and differ over something necessary for the validity of salam, i.e., specification of the description. And whoever claims specification of the description claims validity of the contract, and the apparent state of the contracting parties is that they undertook a valid contract, not an invalid one, being cautious about sin. And the statement is given to one whom the apparent circumstance witnesses for, because it is closer to truth and correctness. [1] His statement: 'obstinate'; linguistically, obstinate is one who seeks hardship, and it is a person falling into something from which he cannot extricate himself. Legally, what is meant by 'obstinate' is one who denies what benefits him, and 'disputant' is one who denies what harms him. This was mentioned by 'Allamah al-Sighnaqi, quoting from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah. [2] His statement: 'more than the capital'; for if it is said: we do not concede, rather the capital is better even if small than the muslam fihi even if great, because the former is cash and this is credit; we say: yes, indeed, but that is set aside here by custom and habit. For people, despite their abundant intellects, proceed to the salam contract, and that is only for a benefit they see in it. So the advantage of cash is eliminated in exchange for an additional benefit they see in the muslam fihi, despite it being credit. This is from al-Nihayah. [3] His statement: 'so it is obligatory... etc.'; because an invalid contract is a sin, and the apparent state of the Muslim is to avoid sin. [4] His statement: 'because he claims validity'; even if his companion denies it. According to them (the two), the statement is of the Muslim 'alayhi because he is a denier, even if he denies validity. This is in al-Hidayah. (1) (al-Hidayah 3:77).
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a disagreement in a salam contract. The matn states that when the parties differ over the stipulation of inferior quality or a term, the statement of the one who claims the condition is accepted. The commentator explains that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa: because the claimant asserts the validity of the contract, and the apparent state of Muslims is to avoid invalid contracts, so the claim of validity is more credible. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the statement of the denier is accepted, because the denier is either denying an obligation upon himself (if the term is claimed against him) or simply denying any condition. The commentary then elaborates on the principles: if the denying party is being obstinate (muta'annit) by denying what benefits him, then the other's statement is taken; if the denying party is a disputant (mukhasim) by denying what harms him, then the claim of validity is preferred according to Abu Hanifa. The footnotes further define 'muta'annit' and 'mukhasim', and support Abu Hanifa's view by noting common custom that people enter salam for a perceived benefit, so the claimant of validity is closer to the truth.

Key Terms
متعنت (muta‘annit): One who denies what benefits him, acting obstinately.
مخاصم (mukhāṣim): One who denies what harms him, i.e., a disputant in a genuine conflict of interest.
رداءة (radā’ah): Inferior quality of the delivered item in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In salam condition disputes, the claimant's statement is accepted because he asserts validity, and the denier is considered obstinate for denying a beneficial condition.

Who Gets the Say in Salam Disputes? Abu Hanifa vs the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qawl in salam disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whose statement is accepted in disputes over validity and term in salam contracts, and explain the underlying presumption (zahir vs istishab) that leads to the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
In salam disputes, Abu Hanifa backs the validity claimant; the Sahibayn back the denier.

When a buyer and seller in a salam contract disagree — did they stipulate a condition or a term? — a judge must decide whose word is law. This lesson unpacks a famous intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa sides with the one who claims the contract is valid, while the two Sahibayn side with the denier. The disagreement turns on which presumption is stronger: the apparent good faith of Muslims (zahir) or the original absence of any stipulation (istishab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dispute scenarios
The text presents two concrete cases: (a) buyer claims a condition of inferior quality was stipulated, seller denies; (b) they differ about whether a term (ajal) was agreed. Recognize that in both, one party claims the contract is valid (by affirming a condition or term that exists) and the other denies it.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that the one claiming validity (mudda'i al-sihha) gets the say. His proof: the apparent state (al-zahir) of Muslims is to enter valid contracts, avoiding sin. The claimant of validity is supported by this presumption, so his statement is stronger.
Note the term 'mut'anni' – the buyer who denies validity is obstinate because denial harms him.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the denier (munkir) gets the say. Their proof: the original state (istishab al-asi) is absence of any stipulation; the denier is simply affirming that original state.
Istishab al-asi is a presumption of continuity of the original state.
4
Contrast the two presumptions: zahir vs istishab
Abu Hanifa prioritizes the apparent good faith of the contracting parties (zahir al-hal), assuming they acted correctly. The Sahibayn prioritize the legal maxim that things remain as they originally were until proven otherwise. This is the root of the ikhtilaf.
This is a methodological difference in usul al-fiqh that appears in many fiqh chapters.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in a court, the judge will accept the testimony of one party over the other based on which view he follows. Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer or the one affirming the term wins; under the Sahibayn, the denier wins.
The text does not specify the mufta bihi, but later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Hanifa in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ ربَّ السَّلَم متعنت في إنكارِهِ الصَّحة؛ لأنَّ المُسَلَّم فيه زائد على رأس المال عادة، فإنكارُهُ الصَّحَةَ دعوى أمر يكون ضرراً في حقه، فكان متعنتاً. ولو ادعى ربُّ السَّلم شرط الرداءة، وقال المسلم إليه لم نشترط شيئاً، فالواجب أن يكون القولُ لربِّ السَّلم عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأنه يدعي الصحة، فالحاصل أن في الصورتين القول لمدَّعي الصحة عنده، وعندهما القولُ للمنكر. ولو اختلفا في الأجل، فقال أحدهما: شرطنا الأجل، وقال الآخر: لم نشترط فأيهما ادعى الأجل، فالقول قوله عند أبي حنيفة له؛ لأنه يدعي الصحة، وعندهما القولُ للمنكر. بيان الوصف ادعى صحة العقد، والظاهرُ من حالِ العاقدين أنهما باشرا عقداً صحيحاً لا فاسداً متحرزاً عن المعصية، والقول يكون لمن يشهد له الظاهر؛ لكونه أقرب إلى الصدق والصواب.
Because the owner of the salam is being obstinate in denying its validity, because the subject matter of the salam is usually more than the capital, so his denial of validity is a claim of something that would harm him, so he is being obstinate. And if the owner of the salam claims a condition of inferior quality, and the one obligated to deliver the salam says 'We did not stipulate anything,' then the statement is that of the owner of the salam according to Abu Hanifa, because he claims validity. So the conclusion is that in both cases the statement is of the one claiming validity according to him, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the statement is of the denier. And if they differ regarding the term, one saying 'We stipulated the term' and the other saying 'We did not stipulate,' then whichever claims the term, his statement is accepted according to Abu Hanifa, because he claims validity; and according to the two, the statement is of the denier. Explanation: He claimed validity of the contract, and the apparent state of the contracting parties is that they entered into a valid contract, not an invalid one, avoiding sin. And the statement is with the one for whom the apparent state testifies, because it is closer to truth and correctness.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding disputes in salam contract. It addresses two scenarios: (1) the buyer (rabb al-salam) claims that a condition of inferior quality was stipulated, while the seller (al-muslam ilayhi) denies any condition; (2) they differ about whether a term (ajal) was stipulated. Abu Hanifa's position: In both cases, the statement (al-qawl) is with the one who claims the contract is valid (i.e., the buyer in the first scenario, and the one who affirms the term in the second). His reasoning is that the default assumption is that Muslims enter into valid contracts, avoiding sin. The one claiming validity is supported by this apparent state (al-zahir). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the statement is with the denier, because denial is the original state (istishab al-asi). This difference stems from prioritizing the presumption of validity (for Abu Hanifa) versus the presumption of non-existence of the disputed condition (for the Sahibayn). The text also notes that the buyer's denial of validity is considered obstinate because he would be claiming harm to himself, since salam is usually profitable.

Key Terms
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer in a salam contract, i.e., the one who pays the price in advance.
المسلم إليه (al-muslam ilayhi): The seller in a salam contract, i.e., the one who receives the price and is obligated to deliver the commodity later.
متعنت (mut'anni): One who is obstinate, seeking hardship by denying something that benefits him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, who gets the say when the buyer (rabb al-salam) claims a condition of inferior quality was stipulated and the seller denies it?
A) The buyer, because he claims validity
B) The seller, because he is the denier
C) Both, because it is a mutual claim
D) Neither, the contract is void

Key Takeaway
In salam disputes, Abu Hanifa gives the say to the validity claimant (zahir), while the Sahibayn give it to the denier (istishab).

Istisna'a (Manufacturing Contract): Definition and Ruling
Definition and ruling of istisna'a with term distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istisna'a, identify the two scenarios (with term and without term), and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Istisna'a without a term is a valid sale; with a term it becomes salam.

This passage from a Hanafi text defines the contract of istisna'a and establishes its legal ruling. The distinction between a specified term and no term is crucial: when a term is set, the contract is treated as salam; when no term is set and the item is customarily made to order, the contract is considered a valid sale (bay'), not a mere promise. This ruling is based on customary practice (ta'amul), a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The passage defines istisna'a as an order to an artisan to make an item from the artisan's own materials, with specified type, quality, and price. This sets the foundation for the contract.
The artisan provides both labor and materials.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes istisna'a with a term (ajal) and without a term. With a term, it is salam; without a term, in customarily dealt items, it is a valid sale.
The term transforms the contract into salam regardless of custom.
3
Recognize the basis in custom
The validity without a term depends on whether people customarily deal in such items (ta'amul). Examples given: khuff (sock), qumqum (kettle), taste (basin).
Custom (urf) is a recognized source for Hanafi rulings.
4
Note the legal effect
Without a term, the contract is a binding sale (bay'), not a mere promise (idda). This means both parties are bound and the contract is enforceable.
A promise (wa'd) alone is not binding; here it is a contract.

Section on istisna'a. And istisna'a with a term is salam, whether people customarily deal in it or not. And without a term, in what people customarily deal, like a sock, a kettle, and a basin, it is valid as a sale, not as a promise. Istisna'a is: that one says to the artisan, like the sock-makers: 'Make for me from your property a sock of this type, of this description, for such and such.'
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines istisna'a (a contract for manufacturing) and distinguishes it from salam. Istisna'a is a contract where the customer orders an artisan to make an item from the artisan's own material. Key points: (1) If a term (ajal) is specified, it becomes salam regardless of whether people customarily deal in that item, because the presence of a term brings it under the rules of salam (which requires immediate payment and deferred delivery). (2) If no term is specified, then for items that people customarily deal in on order (like socks, kettles, basins), the contract is valid as a sale (bay'), not merely a promise (idda). This is based on customary practice (ta'amul). The examples given are khuff (sock), qumqum (a kettle), and taste (a basin). The validity is established by custom, which is a recognized source in the Hanafi school. The contract must specify the type, quality, and quantity of the item. If the item is not customarily dealt in, or if a term is set, the ruling changes. The text also notes that istisna'a without a term is a sale, not a mere promise, meaning it is binding.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'a): A contract to order a specific manufactured item from an artisan, where the artisan provides the material and does the work.
سلم (salam): A sale where the price is paid immediately and the commodity is delivered later, with a specified term.
عدة (idda): A promise; the contract is valid as a sale, not merely a non-binding promise.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an istisna'a contract without a specified term is a binding sale if the item is customarily manufactured on order; with a term, it becomes salam.

Istisna': Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Setting a Term
Defines istisna', its ruling with/without term, and intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna' with and without a term, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the effect of a fixed term.

Core Idea
Istisna' without a term is valid as a sale by istihsan; with a term, Abu Hanifa says it becomes salam, but the two companions say it remains istisna' if customarily transacted.

Understanding the distinction between istisna' (manufacturing contract) and salam (forward sale) is crucial in Hanafi fiqh because the default qiyas would invalidate selling a non-existent item. However, the Hanafi school validates istisna' by istihsan due to continuous custom. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines istisna', sets its conditions, and presents an internal debate: what happens when a fixed term (ajal) is mentioned? Abu Hanifa's view differs from that of his two foremost students, and the later school adopts the latter as the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Define istisna' and list its conditions
Identify the legal definition of istisna' (commissioning a craftsman to produce an item from his own materials). Then enumerate the conditions for validity: specification of genus, type, quantity, and description; the item must be customarily transacted in; no fixed term (according to Abu Hanifa).
The condition of 'no term' is contested as per the intra-Hanafi dispute.
2
Identify the ruling without a term
Without a fixed term, istisna' is valid as a sale (bai') by istihsan, even though the item is non-existent at the time of contract. This is an exception to the general prohibition of selling non-existent items.
The justification is based on prophetic practice and continuous Muslim custom.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on setting a term
Compare the positions: Abu Hanifa: any known term converts istisna' into salam. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if the item is customarily transacted in, the term does not change its nature; it remains istisna'. The term is for completion, not demand. If the item is not customary, it becomes salam by agreement.
The two companions' reasoning rests on the primacy of custom and the reality of the contract.
4
Understand the dalil for istihsan
The overall validity of istisna' (without term) relies on istihsan over qiyas. The proof: the Prophet (saw) commissioned a ring and a minbar, and Muslims have practiced it continuously. This custom is treated as binding, similar to the contract of entering a bathhouse for a fee.
Custom (ta'amul) takes the force of consensus (ijma') in this regard.

Source Text

فصل في الاستصناع
والاستصناع بأجلٍ سَلَمٌ تعاملوا فيه أو لا، وبلا أجل فيما يتعامل كخف، وقمقمة، وطست صح بيعاً لا عدة
(الاستصناع : أن يقول للصانع كالخفاف مثلاً : اصنع لى من مالكَ خُفّاً من هذا الجنس بهذه الصفة بكذا) قوله بأجل؛ المراد به ما يضرب مثلُهُ للسَّلم ؛ لأنه لَمّا قال: على أن تفرغه غداً أو بعد غد لا يكون سلماً ؛ لأنَّ ذكر المدَّة للفراغ من العمل لا للمطالبة. وعن الهندواني : إن كان ذكر المدَّة من قبل المستصنع فليس بسلم ؛ لأنه للاستعجال ، وإن كان من قبل الصانع فهو سَلَم ؛ لأنه للاستهمال، وقيل: إن ذكر أدنى مدة تتمكن فيها من العمل فهو استصناع، وإن ذكر أكثر من ذلك فهو سَلّم، كذا في «كمال الدراية». قوله الاستصناع؛ في اللغة: طلب الصنعة، وفي «القاموس»: الصناعة ككتابة حرفة الصانع وعمله الصنعة. انتهى. وفي المصباح: الصنعة: عمل الصانع. انتهى. فعلى هذا يكون معنى الاستصناع : طلب عمل الصانع. وأما شرعاً : فهو طلب العمل من الصانع في شيء مخصوص على وجه مخصوص، كما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : أن يقول...الخ، ومن شروطه بيان جنس المصنوع ونوعه وقدره وصفته، وأن يكون مما فيه تعامل، وأن لا يكون مؤجلاً وإلا كان سَلَماً، وعندهما: المؤجَّلُ استصناع، إلا إذا كان مما لا يجوز فيه الاستصناع، فينقلبُ سلماً في قولهم جميعاً. كما صرحوا به.
فإن أجل أجلاً معلوماً كان سَلَماً سواء جَرَى فيه التعامل أو لا، فيعتبر فيه شرائط السَّلَمُ وإن لم يؤجل، فإنْ كان مما يجري فيه التعامل صح بطريق البيع. قوله كان سلماً سواء...الخ؛ وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا: إنَّ ضرب الأجل فيما تعاملوا فيه، فهو استصناع ؛ لأنه حقيقة فيه، فيحمل عليه، ويكون ذكر الأجـل للاستعجال لا للاستهمال، بخلاف ما لم يتعاملوا فيه ؛ لأنه استصناع فاسد فيحملُ على السلم الصحيح. ولأبي حنيفة له : إنَّ الاستصناع يحتمل السلم، فكان حمله عليه أولى؛ لأنَّ جوازه بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وجواز الاستصناع بالتعامل في عرفهم، فلا يحمل عليه.
قوله صح بطريق البيع؛ استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يصح؛ لأنه بيع المعدوم وهو منهي عنه، وبه قال زفرُ والأئمَّةُ الثلاثة. ووجه الاستحسان : إنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم استصنع خاتماً ومنبراً، وأنَّ المسلمين تعاملوه من لدنه صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى يومنا هذا من غير نكير والتعامل بهذه الصفة أصل كبير في الشرع، فصار كدخول الحمام بأجر، فإنه جاز استحساناً للتعامل، وإن أبي القياس جوازه؛ لأنَّ مقدار المكث وما يصب من الماء مجهول. والمعدوم قد يعتبر موجوداً حكماً، كطهارة المعذور، وكالنَّاسي للتسمية عند الذبح، فإنَّ التسمية جعلت موجودة بعذر النسيان، فكذلك المستصنع المعدوم جعل موجوداً حكماً للتعامل، ونَزَل منزلة الإجماع للتعامل.
Chapter on Istisna'. And istisna' with a fixed term is salam whether people customarily transact in it or not. And without a term, in what they customarily transact in like a shoe, a kettle, and a basin, it is valid as a sale, not as a counted order. (Istisna': That the buyer says to the craftsman, for example to shoemakers: Make for me from your property a shoe of this type with this description for such price.) His statement: 'with a fixed term' means that which has a similar term used for salam; because when he says: 'on condition that you finish it tomorrow or the day after' it is not salam; because mentioning the period is for completion of work, not for demand. And from al-Hinduwani: If the mention of the period is from the one commissioning (mustasni'), it is not salam because it is for haste; and if it is from the craftsman, it is salam because it is for procrastination. And it is said: If he mentions the minimum period in which the work can be completed, it is istisna'; and if he mentions more than that, it is salam. This is as in 'Kmal al-Diraya'. His statement: 'istisna'' in language: seeking of craftsmanship. And in al-Qamus: 'al-sina'ah' like writing: the craft of the craftsman, and his work is al-sin'ah. End. And in al-Misbah: al-sin'ah: the work of the craftsman. End. So based on this, the meaning of istisna' is: seeking the work of the craftsman. As for [its meaning] in Shari'ah: it is seeking the work from the craftsman in something specific in a specific manner, as the commentator indicated by his statement: 'that he says...' etc. Among its conditions are: specification of the genus of the manufactured item, its type, its quantity, and its description; that it is something in which people customarily transact; and that it is not with a deferred term; otherwise it is salam. And according to them (the two companions): the deferred term [contract] is istisna', unless it is something in which istisna' is not permissible, then it becomes salam according to all of them. As they have stated. Then if he sets a known term, it is salam whether customarily transacted in or not, so the conditions of salam are considered in it. And if he does not set a term, then if it is something in which customarily transacted, it is valid as a sale. His statement: 'it is salam whether...' etc. This is according to the Imam. And they (the two companions) said: Setting a term in what they customarily transact in, it is istisna' because it is the reality in it, so it is interpreted accordingly, and the mentioning of the term is for haste not for procrastination. Contrary to what is not customarily transacted in, because it is a fasid istisna', so it is interpreted as a valid salam. And for Abu Hanifa is his proof: That istisna' bears the possibility of salam, so to interpret it as salam is preferable, because its permissibility is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus, while the permissibility of istisna' is by custom in their 'urf, so it should not be interpreted as such. His statement: 'it is valid as a sale' is by istihsan. And by qiyas it would not be valid, because it is a sale of a non-existent item, which is prohibited, and this was the view of Zufar and the three Imams. The basis of istihsan: That the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم commissioned a ring and a minbar, and the Muslims have customarily transacted in this from his time to this day without any rejection. And transacting in this manner is a great principle in the Shari'ah, so it has become like entering the bathhouse for a fee, for it is permitted by istihsan due to custom, although qiyas prevented its permissibility because the duration of stay and the amount of water poured are unknown. And a non-existent item may be considered existent by way of legal fiction (hukman), like the purity of the excused person, and like the one who forgets the Basmala at the time of slaughter: the Basmala is considered existent by reason of forgetfulness. So too, the non-existent commissioned item is considered existent by legal fiction due to custom, and it takes the place of consensus because of custom.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of Istisna' from Al-Hidayah. It begins by defining the basic ruling: if a fixed term (ajal) is set, the contract becomes salam (forward sale), regardless of whether people customarily engage in such transactions. If no term is set, and the item is one that people customarily order (like shoes, kettles, basins), then the contract is valid as an ordinary sale (bai'), not as a counted order (istisna' in the strict sense is treated as a sale by istihsan). The commentator then clarifies the definition of istisna' linguistically and legally: it is the act of commissioning a craftsman to produce a specific item using his own materials. Conditions include specifying the genus, type, quantity, and description of the item, and that it be something customarily transacted. The key dispute is: what happens if a term is set? According to Imam Abu Hanifa, setting any known term makes it salam, even if the item is customarily transacted in. His reasoning: istisna' is ambiguous, so interpreting it as salam is safer because salam's validity is established by Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus, whereas istisna' only relies on custom. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the opposite view: if the item is customarily transacted, a term does not change it to salam; it remains istisna', and the term is merely a deadline for completion (not for demand). Only if the item is not customarily transacted does it become salam. The text also addresses the broader validity of istisna'. By qiyas (analogy), selling a non-existent item is prohibited; thus Zufar and the three other Imams consider istisna' invalid. However, the Hanafi school accepts it by istihsan (juristic preference) because of the continuous practice of Muslims since the time of the Prophet (saw), who himself commissioned a ring and a minbar. This practice (ta'amul) is treated as a binding custom, similar to the contract of entering a bathhouse for a fee, where the fee is known but the exact service is uncertain. The commentator gives further analogies: just as the purity of an excused person is legally considered valid despite doubt, and just as the forgetful slaughterer's Basmala is considered existent, so the non-existent manufactured item is legally deemed existent due to custom. Thus, the custom takes the force of consensus (ijma'). This passage is crucial for understanding how Hanafi fiqh balances textual proof, analogical reasoning, and customary practice.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'): A contract where a party commissions a manufacturer to produce a specific item from his own materials.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a precisely described item to be delivered at a future date.
تعامل (ta'amul): Customary practice among people in transactions.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that sets aside qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or other considerations.
بيع (bai'): A contract of sale where ownership of a specific item is transferred for a price.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline for delivery or completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the juristic basis (dalil) for the validity of istisna' (manufacturing contract) without a fixed term?
A) Qiyas on the sale of existing items.
B) Istihsan based on continuous custom from the Prophet's time.
C) Ijma' of the Companions on its permissibility.
D) Direct textual evidence from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is valid by istihsan due to custom; setting a term triggers a dispute within the Hanafi school, with the two companions' view being the mufta bihi.

The Effect of a Fixed Term on Istisna': Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of a term in istisna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of a fixed term in an istisna' contract, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

Core Idea
A fixed term in istisna' triggers a dispute: Abu Hanifa treats it as salam; the Sahibayn uphold istisna' when custom exists.

This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi disagreement over a seemingly simple question: what happens when a customer and craftsman agree on a delivery date for a custom-made item? Is it still istisna' or does it transform into a salam contract? The answer has significant practical implications for payment timing, conditions, and options. We will follow the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) to understand their reasoning and the resulting differences in rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline ruling and the point of dispute
Identify the initial ruling given in the text: if a known term is set, it is a salam. Determine the exact issue over which the Imams disagree—does setting a term in a customarily dealt item change the nature of the contract?
The baseline ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifa.
2
Present the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Explain why the Sahibayn hold that despite a term, the contract remains istisna' when there is customary dealing. Note their key argument: the term is for haste (isti'jal), not for deferment (istihmal).
Custom (ta'amul) is the basis for their view.
3
Explain Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Describe why Abu Hanifa insists on classifying it as salam: istisna' is open to interpretation (yahtamil) as salam, and salam has stronger textual evidence (Quran, Sunnah, consensus). Custom alone cannot override explicit proofs.
Abu Hanifa prioritises the a priori evidence over customary practice.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in rulings: under Abu Hanifa, the contract becomes a salam with conditions like advance payment and delayed delivery at term; under the Sahibayn, it remains istisna' with its own rulings (no advance payment required, customer has option upon seeing the item).
This has real-world impact on the parties' obligations.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
Check whether the commentator indicates a preferred view. Although the first part of the text seems to side with Abu Hanifa, later discussions treat istisna' as a valid sale by istihsan, which aligns more with the Sahibayn's view on retaining the istisna' nature.
The relied-upon position in the madhhab on istisna' itself is that it is a sale, not a promise, which supports the Sahibayn's overall approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أجل أجلاً معلوماً كان سَلَماً سواءً جَرَى فيه التعامل أو لا، فيعتبر فيه شرائط السَّلَمُ وإن لم يؤجل، فإنْ كان مما يجري فيه التعامل صح بطريق البيع"" 11 ] قوله : كان سلماً سواء...الخ ؛ وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا : إنَّ ضرب الأجل فيما تعاملوا فيه، فهو استصناع ؛ لأنه حقيقة فيه، فيحمل عليه، ويكون ذكر الأجـل للاستعجال لا للاستهمال، بخلاف ما لم يتعاملوا فيه ؛ لأنه استصناع فاسد فيحملُ على السلم الصحيح. ولأبي حنيفة له : إنَّ الاستصناع يحتمل السلم، فكان حمله عليه أولى؛ لأنَّ جوازه بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وجواز الاستصناع بالتعامل في عرفهم، فلا يحمل عليه.
[٢) قوله : صح بطريق البيع ؛ استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يصح؛ لأنه بيع المعدوم وهو منهي عنه ، وبه قال زفرُ والأئمَّةُ الثلاثة . ووجه الاستحسان : إنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «استصنع خاتماً ومنبراً ، وأنَّ المسلمين تعاملوه من لدنه صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى يومنا هذا من غير نكير والتعامل بهذه الصفة أصل كبير في الشرع، فصار كدخول الحمام بأجر، فإنه جاز استحساناً للتعامل، وإن أبي القياس جوازه ؛ لأنَّ مقدار المكث وما يصب من الماء مجهول.
والمعدوم قد يعتبر موجوداً حكماً ، كطهارة المعذور، وكالنَّاسي للتسمية عند الذبح، فإنَّ التسمية جعلت موجودة بعذر النسيان، فكذلك المستصنع المعدوم جعل موجوداً حكماً للتعامل ، ونَزَل منزلة الإجماع للتعامل.
(۱) فعن ابن عمر ه: «إن رسول الله الله اصطنع خاتماً من ذهب فكان يجعل فصه في باطن كفّه إذا لبسه ، فصنع الناس، ثم إنه جلس على المنبر فنزعه ، فقال : إني كنت ألبس هذا الخاتم ، وأجعل فصه من داخل فرمى به، ثم قال : والله لا ألبسه أبداً فنبذ الناس خواتيمهم» في «صحيح البخاري (٥) : ٢٢٠٥) ، و (صحیح مسلم (۳) : (١٦٥٥) ، و(صحيح ابن حبان (۱۳ : ۳۰۲)، وغيرها.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
فَيُجبَرُ الصَّانِعُ على عمله، ولا يَرْجِعُ الأمرُ عنه ، والمبيع هو العينُ لا عَمَله ، فإن جاء بما صنعه غيره، أو صنعه هو قبل العقد، فأخذه صح، ولا يتعينُ له بلا اختياره، فصح بيعُ الصَّانع قبل رؤية الأمر، وله أخذه وتركه
لا بطريق العِدَة ) ، فإن لم يَجْرِ فيه التَّعامل لا يجوز. ثم ذكر فروع أنَّه بيع لا عِدَة فقال : (فَيُجبَرُ الصَّانِعُ على عمله، ولا يَرْجِعُ الأمر عنه ، والمبيع هو العينُ لا عَمَلُه ، فإن جاء بما صنعه غيره ، أو صنعه هو قبل العقد، فأخذه صح، ولا يتعيَّنُ له بلا اختياره فصح بيع الصانع قبل رؤيةِ الأمر (٢) ، وله أخذه وتركه
[١]قوله : لا بطريق العِدة ؛ فيه تعريض على الحاكم الشهيد له ؛ فإنّه كان قائلا : إذا جاءَ مفروغاً عنه ينعقد بالتعاطي ؛ ولذا يثبت الخيار لكل واحد منهما ، لكنَّ الصحيح من المذهب جوازه بيعاً ؛ لأنَّ محمداً لله سماه شراء. فقال : كان المستصنع إذا رآه بالخيار ؛ لأنه اشترى ما لم يره ، وذكر فيه القياس والاستحسان وفصل بين ما فيه تعامل وبين ما لا تعامل فيه، والمواعيد تجوز قياساً واستحساناً في الكل. (٢) قوله : لا عمله؛ أي عملُ الصَّانع، وقال البردعي الله : عمله نظراً إلى أنَّ الاستصناع مشتق من الصنع، وهو العمل، والأول أصح ؛ لأنَّ المقصود هو العين. [۳]قوله : وله أخذه ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ المستصنع - بكسر النون – بعد الرؤية بالخيار إن شاء أخذه، وإن شاءَ تركه ولا خيار للصَّانع، فيجبر على العمل، وعن الإمام أنَّ له الخيار دفعاً للضرر . والصحيح هو الأوَّل، وعن أبي يوسف له أنَّه لا خيار لواحد منهما، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره.
If he sets a known term, it is a salam whether dealing occurs in it or not, so the conditions of salam are considered. And if he does not set a term, then if it is something in which dealing occurs, it is valid as a sale. [1] His statement: 'It is a salam whether...etc.'; this is according to the Imam. And the two said: Setting a term in what they deal in is istisna'; because it is its reality, so it is carried upon it, and mentioning the term is for haste not for delay, contrary to what they do not deal in; because it is an invalid istisna', so it is carried upon a valid salam. And for Abu Hanifa: verily istisna' is open to interpretation as salam, so carrying it upon it is more appropriate; because its permissibility is by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus, and the permissibility of istisna' is by dealing (ta'amul) in their custom, so it is not carried upon it. [2] His statement: 'Valid as a sale'; by istihsan, and the analogy is that it is not valid; because it is a sale of a non-existent thing, which is prohibited, and Zufar and the three Imams said so. And the face of istihsan: verily the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) commissioned a ring and a minbar, and the Muslims have continuously practiced it from his time to our day without disapproval, and dealing in this manner is a great principle in Shari'ah, so it became like entering a bathhouse for a fee, which is permitted by istihsan due to dealing, even though analogy prohibits it; because the duration of stay and the amount of water poured are unknown. And the non-existent may be considered existent by legal fiction, like the purity of the excused person, and the one who forgets to pronounce the name of Allah at slaughter, for the pronunciation is deemed existent due to the excuse of forgetfulness; similarly, the commissioned non-existent item is deemed existent by legal fiction due to dealing, and it descends to the level of consensus due to dealing. (1) From Ibn 'Umar: that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) commissioned a gold ring and would place its stone on the inside of his palm when wearing it, so the people made rings. Then he sat on the minbar and removed it, saying: 'I used to wear this ring and place its stone inside.' Then he threw it away and said: 'By Allah, I will never wear it again.' So the people threw away their rings. [Sahih al-Bukhari (5:2205), Sahih Muslim (3:1655), Sahih Ibn Hibban (13:302), etc.] [PAGE_282] Zubdat an-Nihaya li-`Umdat ar-Ri`aya: So the craftsman is compelled to do his work, and the matter does not revert from him, and the sold item is the physical object, not his labor. If he brings what he made by another, or made it by himself before the contract, and he takes it, it is valid, and it does not become specifically his without his choice. So the sale by the craftsman before seeing the item is valid, and he has the option to take it or leave it, not by way of a promise. If dealing does not occur in it, it is not permissible. Then he mentioned branches that it is a sale, not a promise. He said: (So the craftsman is compelled to do his work, and the matter does not revert from him, and the sold item is the physical object, not his labor. If he brings what he made by another, or made it by himself before the contract, and he takes it, it is valid, and it does not become specifically his without his choice. So the sale by the craftsman before seeing the item is valid, and he has the option to take it or leave it.) [1] His statement: 'not by way of a promise'; in it is a criticism of al-Hakim ash-Shahid; for he used to say: if it comes finished, it is concluded by mu'atah (hand-to-hand exchange); therefore, the option is established for each of them, but the correct position in the madhhab is that it is permissible as a sale; because Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) called it a purchase. He said: the customer, when he sees it, has an option; because he bought what he did not see, and he mentioned analogy and istihsan and distinguished between what dealing occurs in and what does not, and promises are valid by analogy and istihsan in all cases. [2] His statement: 'not his labor'; i.e., the craftsman's labor. Al-Bardha'i (may Allah be pleased with him) said: his labor, considering that istisna' is derived from san' (making), which is labor, and the first is more correct; because the intended thing is the physical object. [3] His statement: 'and he has the option to take it...etc.'; means that the customer (with kasr of nun) after seeing it has an option: if he wishes, he takes it, and if he wishes, he leaves it, and there is no option for the craftsman, so he is compelled to work. It is reported from the Imam that he has an option to repel harm. The correct is the first. And from Abu Yusuf that there is no option for either of them. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the classification of a manufacturing contract (istisna') when a fixed term is set. The passage first states a general ruling: if a known term is set, it is a salam (advance sale) regardless of whether there is customary dealing in such items. If no term is set, then if the item is one in which people customarily deal (like shoes, utensils, etc.), it is valid as a sale (istisna') by istihsan. The commentator then explains the disagreement behind the first part. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, setting a term makes it a salam because the term is the distinguishing feature of salam. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if people deal in that item (i.e., it is customarily made to order), setting a term does not negate it being istisna'; rather, the term is for expediting the work, not for deferring the price. They argue that if there is no custom, the contract is an invalid istisna', so it must be interpreted as a valid salam. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that istisna' can potentially be interpreted as salam, and since salam has stronger textual evidence (Quran, Sunnah, consensus), it is preferable to carry the contract on salam rather than on istisna' which is only justified by custom. The second part discusses the validity of istisna' itself when no term is set and custom exists. The underlying qiyas (analogy) would invalidate it because it is a sale of a non-existent object (ma'dum), which is prohibited. Zufar and the three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold this view. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) based on the continuous practice of the Muslims since the Prophet's time, who himself commissioned a ring and a minbar. This practice has continued without disapproval, amounting to an implicit consensus (ijma' suktuti). The commentator compares it to entering a bathhouse for a fee, where the duration and water usage are unknown but permitted by istihsan due to custom. Additionally, the non-existent item is deemed existent by legal fiction (hukman) due to necessity, similar to the purification of a person with chronic illness or the pronunciation of basmalah being considered present when forgotten. The passage then provides detailed rulings: the craftsman is compelled to complete the work, and the customer has the option to reject the item upon seeing it (khiyar ar-ru'ya). The subject matter is the physical object, not the labor. The sale is valid even before the item is seen, and the customer has the option to take or leave it. This is a sale, not a mere promise. The commentator critiques al-Hakim ash-Shahid who considered it a promise concluded by mu'atah; the correct view is that it is a sale, as Imam Muhammad termed it a purchase. There is also a subordinate dispute: Abu Hanifa reportedly gave the craftsman an option to avoid harm, but the relied-upon position is that only the customer has an option. Abu Yusuf is reported to say neither party has an option. Shaykh Bardha'i argued that the subject matter is the labor (since the word istisna' derives from 'making'), but the correct view is that it is the physical object, as that is the intended purpose.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'): A contract to manufacture a specific item, valid by istihsan due to custom.
سلم (salam): An advance sale with immediate payment and deferred delivery.
تعامل (ta'amul): Customary practice of dealing in a particular item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the effect of setting a delivery term in an istisna' contract for a customarily dealt item?
A) It becomes a salam contract with all its conditions.
B) It remains an istisna' contract; the term is for expediting delivery.
C) The contract becomes invalid due to uncertainty.
D) It is considered a promise (wa'd) not a binding contract.

Key Takeaway
When a fixed term is set in a customarily dealt istisna', Abu Hanifa treats it as salam, while the Sahibayn uphold it as istisna', with the relied-upon practice favouring the latter due to custom.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Istisna' Valid by Istihsan: Overcoming the Prohibition of Selling Non-Existent Items
Istihsan justification for istisna' and its fiqh details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why istisna' is valid by istihsan despite qiyas, and identify the rulings on subject matter, compulsion, and options in the contract.

Core Idea
Istisna' overrides the qiyas prohibition of selling non-existents through istihsan based on continuous practice and legal fiction.

This lesson examines the second major theme of the passage: the legal justification for permitting istisna' when strict analogy (qiyas) would forbid it. The text presents the istihsan argument using the Prophet's own practice and the continuous custom of Muslims, then derives specific rules about the craftsman's compulsion, the subject matter (physical object vs. labor), and the customer's option upon seeing the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas argument against istisna'
Recognise the analogy: selling a non-existent object (bay' al-ma'dum) is prohibited by hadith. Since istisna' involves an object not yet made, qiyas would render it invalid. Zufar and the three other Imams hold this view.
The shari'ah prohibits gharar and uncertainty.
2
Understand the istihsan justification
Istihsan is based on: 1) The Prophet himself commissioned a ring and a minbar, 2) Continuous Muslim practice (ta'amul) without disapproval, amounting to implicit consensus (ijma' suktuti). This is a 'great principle' in Shari'ah.
Compare to the bathhouse analogy where uncertainty is tolerated due to custom.
3
Analyse the legal fiction (hukman) argument
The non-existent item is deemed existent by legal fiction because of necessity and custom, similar to the purity of a chronic invalid or the basmalah for a forgetful slaughterer. This overcomes the ma'dum objection.
Legal fiction (hukman) treats something as existing in law even if not in reality.
4
Derive the subsidiary rulings from the passage
The text then lists specific rules: the craftsman is compelled to complete the work; the sold item is the physical object (al-'ayn), not the labor; the sale is valid even before seeing; the customer has an option to accept or reject; the craftsman has no option. Also note the intra-Hanafi differences on the option for the craftsman and Abu Yusuf's denial of any option.
These rules flesh out the istisna' contract's practical operation.
5
Contrast with the promise theory
The commentator criticises al-Hakim al-Shahid who considered it a promise (wa'd) concluded by mu'atah. The correct madhhab position is that it is a sale (bay') as affirmed by Imam Muhammad.
This distinction affects binding nature: promises are not enforceable, but sales are.

فإن أجل أجلاً معلوماً كان سَلَماً سواءً جَرَى فيه التعامل أو لا، فيعتبر فيه شرائط السَّلَمُ وإن لم يؤجل، فإنْ كان مما يجري فيه التعامل صح بطريق البيع"" 11 ] قوله : كان سلماً سواء...الخ ؛ وهذا عند الإمام، وقالا : إنَّ ضرب الأجل فيما تعاملوا فيه، فهو استصناع ؛ لأنه حقيقة فيه، فيحمل عليه، ويكون ذكر الأجـل للاستعجال لا للاستهمال، بخلاف ما لم يتعاملوا فيه ؛ لأنه استصناع فاسد فيحملُ على السلم الصحيح. ولأبي حنيفة له : إنَّ الاستصناع يحتمل السلم، فكان حمله عليه أولى؛ لأنَّ جوازه بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وجواز الاستصناع بالتعامل في عرفهم، فلا يحمل عليه.

So the sale by the craftsman before seeing the item is valid, and he has the option to take it or leave it.) [1] His statement: 'not by way of a promise'; in it is a criticism of al-Hakim ash-Shahid; for he used to say: if it comes finished, it is concluded by mu'atah (hand-to-hand exchange); therefore, the option is established for each of them, but the correct position in the madhhab is that it is permissible as a sale; because Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) called it a purchase. He said: the customer, when he sees it, has an option; because he bought what he did not see, and he mentioned analogy and istihsan and distinguished between what dealing occurs in and what does not, and promises are valid by analogy and istihsan in all cases. [2] His statement: 'not his labor'; i.e., the craftsman's labor. Al-Bardha'i (may Allah be pleased with him) said: his labor, considering that istisna' is derived from san' (making), which is labor, and the first is more correct; because the intended thing is the physical object. [3] His statement: 'and he has the option to take it...etc.'; means that the customer (with kasr of nun) after seeing it has an option: if he wishes, he takes it, and if he wishes, he leaves it, and there is no option for the craftsman, so he is compelled to work. It is reported from the Imam that he has an option to repel harm. The correct is the first. And from Abu Yusuf that there is no option for either of them. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the classification of a manufacturing contract (istisna') when a fixed term is set. The passage first states a general ruling: if a known term is set, it is a salam (advance sale) regardless of whether there is customary dealing in such items. If no term is set, then if the item is one in which people customarily deal (like shoes, utensils, etc.), it is valid as a sale (istisna') by istihsan. The commentator then explains the disagreement behind the first part. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, setting a term makes it a salam because the term is the distinguishing feature of salam. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if people deal in that item (i.e., it is customarily made to order), setting a term does not negate it being istisna'; rather, the term is for expediting the work, not for deferring the price. They argue that if there is no custom, the contract is an invalid istisna', so it must be interpreted as a valid salam. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that istisna' can potentially be interpreted as salam, and since salam has stronger textual evidence (Quran, Sunnah, consensus), it is preferable to carry the contract on salam rather than on istisna' which is only justified by custom. The second part discusses the validity of istisna' itself when no term is set and custom exists. The underlying qiyas (analogy) would invalidate it because it is a sale of a non-existent object (ma'dum), which is prohibited. Zufar and the three other Imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold this view. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) based on the continuous practice of the Muslims since the Prophet's time, who himself commissioned a ring and a minbar. This practice has continued without disapproval, amounting to an implicit consensus (ijma' suktuti). The commentator compares it to entering a bathhouse for a fee, where the duration and water usage are unknown but permitted by istihsan due to custom. Additionally, the non-existent item is deemed existent by legal fiction (hukman) due to necessity, similar to the purification of a person with chronic illness or the pronunciation of basmalah being considered present when forgotten. The passage then provides detailed rulings: the craftsman is compelled to complete the work, and the customer has the option to reject the item upon seeing it (khiyar ar-ru'ya). The subject matter is the physical object, not the labor. The sale is valid even before the item is seen, and the customer has the option to take or leave it. This is a sale, not a mere promise. The commentator critiques al-Hakim ash-Shahid who considered it a promise concluded by mu'atah; the correct view is that it is a sale, as Imam Muhammad termed it a purchase. There is also a subordinate dispute: Abu Hanifa reportedly gave the craftsman an option to avoid harm, but the relied-upon position is that only the customer has an option. Abu Yusuf is reported to say neither party has an option. Shaykh Bardha'i argued that the subject matter is the labor (since the word istisna' derives from 'making'), but the correct view is that it is the physical object, as that is the intended purpose.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, comparing istisna' to the sale of a non-existent object.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): The non-existent; something that has no current physical existence.
حكماً (hukman): Legally, by way of legal fiction; considered existent in law for practical purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi istihsan ruling that validates istisna' despite the prohibition of selling non-existent items?
A) The need to facilitate commerce and avoid hardship.
B) The continuous practice of Muslims (ta'amul) from the Prophet's time onward, amounting to implicit consensus.
C) A direct Quranic verse permitting istisna'.
D) The acceptance of uncertainty in all contracts.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is valid by istihsan because of continuous practice and legal fiction; the craftsman is compelled to work, and the customer has an option upon seeing the product.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Istisna' as a Valid Sale by Istihsan
Critique affirming istisna' as sale, addressing option and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna', explain why it is valid by istihsan, and distinguish between customary and non-customary items.

Core Idea
Istisna' is a binding sale by istihsan, not a mere promise; the buyer has the option to reject upon sight.

This passage from 'Umadat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that istisna' (commissioned manufacture) is considered a valid sale in the Hanafi school, overriding the qiyas that would otherwise render it invalid. The author critiques a minority view that treats it only as a promise. The lesson will walk through the ruling, its basis in istihsan and custom, the buyer's option (khiyar al-ru'ya), and the condition that the item must be customarily dealt with in that trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
The text states that the craftsman is compelled to complete the work, and the buyer has an option upon seeing the product. The sold object is the physical item, not the labor. This ruling is based on istihsan overriding qiyas.
The key phrase 'فَيُجبَرُ الصَّانِعُ على عمله' establishes the seller's obligation.
2
Trace the dispute over the nature of istisna'
Al-Hakim al-Shahid argued it is only a promise until delivery, but the majority (including Imam Muhammad) consider it a sale from the outset. Imam Muhammad called it a purchase (شراء).
The author criticizes al-Hakim with 'فيه تعريض على الحاكم الشهيد'.
3
Examine the role of custom ('urf)
Istisna' is valid only in items where such dealings are customary. For non-customary items (e.g., ordering cloth without a deferred term), the contract is invalid unless structured as salam.
The phrase 'فإن لم يَجْرِ فيه التَّعامل لا يجوز' is decisive.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the option
Abu Hanifa reportedly gave the craftsman an option to avoid harm, but the relied-upon position is that only the buyer has the option (Abu Yusuf said no one has an option).
The author states 'والصحيح هو الأوَّل' indicating the chosen view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: istisna' is a valid sale; the buyer has khiyar al-ru'ya; the seller is compelled; the contract is binding from inception; for non-customary items, it reverts to original qiyas (invalid) unless salam conditions are met.
This is the position given in the text and supported by later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَيُجبَرُ الصَّانِعُ على عمله، ولا يَرْجِعُ الأمرُ عنه، والمبيع هو العينُ لا عَمَله، فإن جاء بما صنعه غيره، أو صنعه هو قبل العقد، فأخذه صح، ولا يتعينُ له بلا اختياره، فصح بيعُ الصَّانع قبل رؤية الأمر، وله أخذه وتركه لا بطريق العِدَة، فإن لم يَجْرِ فيه التَّعامل لا يجوز. ثم ذكر فروع أنَّه بيع لا عِدَة فقال: (فَيُجبَرُ الصَّانِعُ على عمله، ولا يَرْجِعُ الأمر عنه، والمبيع هو العينُ لا عَمَلُه، فإن جاء بما صنعه غيره، أو صنعه هو قبل العقد، فأخذه صح، ولا يتعيَّنُ له بلا اختياره فصح بيع الصانع قبل رؤيةِ الأمر، وله أخذه وتركه). قوله: لا بطريق العدة؛ فيه تعريض على الحاكم الشهيد له؛ فإنّه كان قائلاً: إذا جاءَ مفروغاً عنه ينعقد بالتعاطي؛ ولذا يثبت الخيار لكل واحدٍ منهما، لكنَّ الصحيح من المذهب جوازه بيعاً؛ لأنَّ محمداً لله سماه شراء. فقال: كان المستصنع إذا رآه بالخيار؛ لأنه اشترى ما لم يره، وذكر فيه القياس والاستحسان وفصل بين ما فيه تعامل وبين ما لا تعامل فيه، والمواعيد تجوز قياساً واستحساناً في الكل. قوله: لا عمله؛ أي عملُ الصَّانع، وقال البردعي الله: عمله نظراً إلى أنَّ الاستصناع مشتق من الصنع، وهو العمل، والأول أصح؛ لأنَّ المقصود هو العين. قوله: وله أخذه ... الخ؛ يعني أنَّ المستصنع - بكسر النون – بعد الرؤية بالخيار إن شاء أخذه، وإن شاءَ تركه ولا خيار للصَّانع، فيجبر على العمل، وعن الإمام أنَّ له الخيار دفعاً للضرر. والصحيح هو الأوَّل، وعن أبي يوسف له أنَّه لا خيار لواحد منهما، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. ولم يصح فيما لا يُتَعامَلُ كالثَّوب: أي إذا لم يؤجل كما شرحناه. قوله: ولم يصح؛ الاستصناع بلا أجل فيما لا يتعامل هو فيه، كالثوب، يعني لو أمر حائكاً أن ينسج له ثياباً بغزل من عنده بدراهم، لم يجزله، إذ لم يجر فيه التعامل، فيبقى على أصل القياس إلا إذا شرط فيه الأجل، وبين شرائط السلم فحينئذ يجوز بطريق السلم.
The craftsman is compelled to do his work, and the order does not revert from him. The sold item is the physical object, not his work. If he brings what was made by someone else, or he made it before the contract, and he (the buyer) takes it, it is valid. It is not determined for him without his choice. So the sale of the craftsman before seeing the order is valid, and he may take it or leave it, not by way of a promise. If dealings are not customary in it, it is not permissible. Then he mentioned branches that it is a sale, not a promise, and said: (The craftsman is compelled to do his work... etc.). His statement: 'not by way of a promise' contains a criticism of al-Hakim al-Shahid, for he used to say: When it comes finished, it is concluded by mutual exchange (ta'ati), therefore each has an option. But the correct view in the school is that it is permissible as a sale, because Muhammad named it a purchase. Thus he said: The person ordering (mustasni') has an option upon seeing it because he bought what he did not see. He mentioned qiyas and istihsan and distinguished between what is customary and what is not. Promises are valid by qiyas and istihsan in all cases. His statement: 'not his work' means the work of the craftsman. al-Barda'i said: 'his work' because istisna' is derived from san' which is work. The first is more correct because the intended object is the physical item. His statement: 'and he may take it or leave it...' means that the person ordering (mustasni') after seeing has an option: if he wishes he takes it, if he wishes he leaves it. The craftsman has no option; he is compelled to work. From the Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is reported that he has an option to prevent harm. The correct is the first. From Abu Yusuf that neither has an option, as in Majma' al-Anhur and others. And it is not valid in what is not customary, like cloth: i.e., if not deferred as explained. His statement: 'and it is not valid' means istisna' without a deferred term in what is not customary, like cloth: if someone orders a weaver to weave cloth for him with his own yarn for dirhams, it is not permissible, since it is not customary, so it remains on original qiyas, unless a deferred term is stipulated and the conditions of salam are mentioned, then it is permissible by way of salam.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage from 'Umadat al-Ri'ayah is a critical commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah regarding the contract of istisna' (commissioned manufacture). The author clarifies that istisna' is a valid sale (بيع صحيح) by istihsan, not a mere promise (عدة). The opening lines state the ruling: the craftsman is compelled to complete the work, and the order is binding. The sold item is the physical object (العين), not the labor. If the craftsman brings a product made by another or made before the contract, and the buyer accepts it, it is valid. However, the buyer has an option upon seeing the product (خيار الرؤية) because he bought something unseen. The craftsman cannot force the buyer to accept. The author then critiques al-Hakim al-Shahid, who held that istisna' becomes a sale only upon delivery with mutual exchange. The relied-upon view (الصحيح) is that it is a sale from the start, as Imam Muhammad called it a purchase (شراء). The passage distinguishes between customary ('urf) and non-customary items: if there is no custom of dealing in such items (e.g., ordering cloth without a deferred term), the contract is not valid by istisna'; it must be structured as a salam contract with specified term and conditions. The commentary also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa reportedly gave the craftsman an option to avoid harm, but the correct position (المختار) is that only the buyer has the option. Abu Yusuf held that no party has an option. The relied-upon ruling is that the buyer's option exists and the seller's option does not.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisnāʿ): A contract where a person orders a craftsman to manufacture a specific item from the craftsman's own material for a price. It is treated as a sale by istihsan.
العِدَة (al-ʿidā): A promise (مواعدة) to enter into a contract in the future. In this context, some argued istisna' is only a promise until delivery.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’ya): The option to confirm or cancel a sale after seeing the purchased item, which was unseen at the time of contract.
التعامل (al-taʿāmul): Customary practice or dealings among people in a particular trade. Istisna' is valid only in items where such dealings are customary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on istisna' when the item is not customarily dealt with (e.g., ordering cloth without a deferred term)?
A) It is valid as a sale by istihsan with the buyer having an option.
B) It is invalid unless the conditions of salam (advance payment, deferred delivery, specific description) are fulfilled.
C) It is a binding promise, and both parties are compelled.
D) It is valid only if the craftsman completes the work before the contract.

Key Takeaway
Istisna' is a valid sale by istihsan, conditional on custom; the buyer has the option to reject upon sight, and the craftsman is compelled to complete.

Istisna' Without a Term: The Role of Custom
Ruling on istisna' without ajal for cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istisna' without a term for non-customary items and explain how custom overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Custom can override qiyas; without custom or a term, istisna' is invalid.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator clarifies a key condition for istisna' (manufacturing contract). The ruling hinges on whether the transaction is customary and whether a delivery term (ajal) is specified. Understanding this helps distinguish when istisna' is valid and when it must be converted to salam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is istisna' without a specified term (ajal) for an item like cloth. The default ruling under qiyas is invalid due to uncertainty (gharar).
Default qiyas: sale of non-existent subject matter is invalid.
2
Recognize the role of custom
Custom ('urf) can override qiyas in Hanafi fiqh. If such transactions are customary, istisna' is valid even without a term.
Custom must be widespread in that market.
3
Apply the exception: conversion to salam
If the item is not customarily dealt with, the contract is invalid unless a term is set and all conditions of salam are met, transforming it into a valid salam.
Salam requires defined term, price, and description.
4
State the final ruling
For non-customary items like cloth without a term: invalid (batil). Valid only as salam with ajal and conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ الاستصناع ومسائل شتى ولم يصح فيما لا يُتَعامَلُ كالثوب
ثم قوله: ولم يصح فيما لا يُتَعامَلُ كالثَّوب: أي إذا لم يؤجل كما شرحناه. ومعنى قوله: ولم يصح؛ الاستصناع بلا أجل فيما لا يتعامل هو فيه، كالثوب ، يعني لو أمر حائكاً أن ينسج له ثياباً بغزل من عنده بدراهم، لم يجزله، إذ لم يجر فيه التعامل، فيبقى على أصل القياس إلا إذا شرط فيه الأجل، وبين شرائط السلم فحينئذ يجوز بطريق السلم.
Book of Sales / Istisna' and Miscellaneous Issues. And it is not valid in that which is not customarily transacted, like cloth. Then his statement: 'And it is not valid in that which is not customarily transacted, like cloth' meaning if it is not deferred as we explained. And the meaning of his statement: 'And it is not valid: istisna' without a term in that which is not customarily transacted in it, like cloth' means if one orders a weaver to weave garments for him with yarn from himself for dirhams, it is not permissible for him, because such transaction has not been customary, so it remains on the original analogy, except if a term is stipulated and the conditions of salam are clarified, then it becomes permissible by way of salam.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of istisna' (manufacturing contract) when no deferment (ajal) is specified and the item (like cloth) is not customarily transacted in this manner. The commentator clarifies that if someone orders a weaver to produce cloth from the weaver's own materials for a price, but without specifying a delivery term, the contract is invalid because such practice is not widespread in that market. The default principle (qiyas) would forbid it as a sale of non-existent goods. However, if an ajal is set and the conditions of salam (forward sale) are met, then it becomes valid as a salam contract. The ruling underscores the importance of custom ('urf) in overriding qiyas in Hanafi fiqh. The lesson type is a ruling statement with proof by analogy.

Key Terms
استصناع (istisna'): A contract where one orders a manufacturer to produce a specific item using his own materials, with payment agreed upon.
أجل (ajal): A specified future date for delivery or payment in a contract.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice that can override qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid as a regular sale
B) Valid as salam
C) Invalid, remaining on qiyas
D) Valid only if the weaver agrees

Key Takeaway
Istisna' without a term for non-customary items is invalid, but may be valid as salam if a term and conditions are met.

Selling Dogs and Predators: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on selling predatory animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling dogs and predatory animals, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the vicious dog, and explain the basis of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Selling dogs and predators is permitted; only the vicious, untrainable dog is disputed by Abu Yusuf.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on selling dogs, leopards, and other predators, preceded by a linguistic explanation of 'masa'il shatta' (diverse issues). The Hanafi school generally permits the sale of all such animals, but an intra-madhhab dispute arises over the vicious, untrainable dog (al-'aqur). Abu Yusuf prohibits its sale due to lack of benefit, while the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) permit it. Understanding this dispute clarifies how benefit (manfa'ah) functions as a condition for valid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Recognize that the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rules that selling dogs, leopards, and all predatory animals is permissible absolutely, whether trained or not.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Recognize Abu Yusuf's exception
Note that Abu Yusuf restricts this permissibility: he prohibits the sale of the 'aqur (vicious, untrainable) dog but allows other dogs and predators.
The term 'aqur is defined as a dog that does not accept training and yields no benefit.
3
Understand the rationale
Analyze the reasoning: the Hanafi school considers benefit (manfa'ah) as the basis for property value. Abu Yusuf argues that an untrainable dog provides no benefit, so it cannot be validly sold. The majority argue that potential benefit or custom suffices.
This is a talil (reasoning) argument based on benefit.
4
Examine the narrations
Review the textual evidence: al-Hidayah reports Abu Yusuf's prohibition, while Nawadir Hisham reports Muhammad's permission including liability (tadmeen) for killing the vicious dog.
These sources belong to different transmission layers.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the general permissibility (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) is the relied-upon position for fatwa, though some later scholars may adopt Abu Yusuf's restriction.
The text does not explicitly tarjih, but the majority view is more widely transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسائل شتى
صح بيع الكلب والفهد والسباع عُلمت أو لا، هذا عندنا.
ثم قوله: مسائل شتى؛ اعلم: أولاً: أنَّ المسائل جمع المسألة، وهي عند أهل اللغة بمعنى السؤال، وعند أهل النظر: هي الدعوى من حيث أنه يرد عليه أو على دليله السؤال، وقد تطلق المسائل على القضايا التي يطلب بيانها في العلوم ، وهي في الأغلب نظريات، وقد تكون ضرورية، فتوردُ في العلم، إما لاحتياجها إلى تنبيه يزيلُ عنها خفاءها ، أو لبيان لميتها؛ لأنَّ القضية قد تكون بديهية دونَ لميتها، ككون النَّارِ محرقة، فإنّه معلوم الآنية، ومجهول اللمية.
وثانياً: إن شتى جمع شتيت والشتات معنى التفرق، شت الأمر واستشت وتشتت، بمعنى: تفرق، قوم شتى، وأشياء شتى، وجاؤوا أشتاتاً؛ أي متفرقين.
وثالثاً: إن من عادات المصنفين أن يذكروا عقيب الأبواب ما شـبهها من المسائل، فتصير مسائل من أبواب متفرقة، فتترجم تارة بمسائل منثورة، وتارة بمسائل شتى، والمعنى واحد.
ثم قوله: هذا عندنا... الخ؛ توضيح الكلام: إن بيع الكلب والفهد وسائر السباع جائز عندنا مطلقاً، سواءً عُلمت أو لا، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز بيع الكلب إلا العقور: أي الجارح، وهو الذي لا يقبل التعليم؛ لأنه غير منتفع به. وقال في «الهداية»: وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يجوز بيع الكلب العقور. وهذا دال على أنَّه رواية عنه، ونص في «نوادر هشام» عن محمد على جواز بيع العقور، وتضمين من قتله، وعن أبي يوسف منع بيع العقور.
Miscellaneous Issues. It is valid to sell a dog, a leopard, and predatory animals whether trained or not, this is in our school. Then his statement: 'Miscellaneous Issues'; know: firstly, al-masa'il is plural of al-mas'alah, which in lexicography means question, and according to theoreticians: it is a claim (da'wa) from the aspect that a question is directed at it or its evidence. Sometimes masa'il refers to issues whose clarification is sought in sciences, and they are mostly theoretical, and may be necessary (self-evident), so they are presented in a science either due to needing a clarification that removes their obscurity, or to explain their cause (limiyya), because a proposition may be self-evident in its existence (anniyya) but unknown in its cause (limiyya), like fire being burning—it is known in its existence but unknown in its cause. Secondly, 'shatta' is plural of 'shatit', and 'shatat' means dispersion. Thirdly, it is the habit of authors to mention after chapters what resembles them from issues, so these become issues from different chapters, and they sometimes title it 'Masa'il Manthura' (Scattered Issues) and sometimes 'Masa'il Shatta' (Diverse Issues), and the meaning is one. Then his statement: 'this is in our school...' clarifying: Indeed, the sale of dogs, leopards, and all predatory animals is absolutely permissible in our school, whether trained or not. According to Abu Yusuf, the sale of a dog is permissible except the 'aqur (vicious/untrainable dog)—i.e. the one that does not accept training because it yields no benefit. And it is stated in al-Hidayah: 'From Abu Yusuf it is narrated that the sale of a vicious dog is not permissible.' This indicates it is a narration from him. And the text in Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) permits the sale of a vicious dog and includes liability (tadmeen) for killing it, while from Abu Yusuf it prohibits it.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage begins with a linguistic explanation of 'masa'il shatta' (diverse issues) and then presents the Hanafi ruling on selling dogs, leopards, and other predatory animals. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that selling all such animals is permissible regardless of whether they are trained or not, because they have value and can be benefitted from (e.g., hunting or guarding). Abu Yusuf, however, makes an exception: he allows the sale of trainable dogs but prohibits the sale of a 'aqur (vicious, untrainable) dog because it yields no benefit. The text notes that these views are narrations, with al-Hidayah reporting Abu Yusuf's prohibition, while Nawadir Hisham reports Muhammad's permission including liability for killing. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the general permissibility, though some later fatwas may adopt Abu Yusuf's restriction. The lesson type is an intra-Hanafi dispute, and the argument focuses on benefit (manfa'ah) as a condition for valid sale.

Key Terms
عقور ('aqur): A vicious, untrainable dog that does not accept training and provides no benefit.
تضمين (tadmeen): Liability; duty to compensate for damage or loss.
سباع (siba'): Predatory animals, including leopards, wolves, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the view of Abu Yusuf regarding the sale of a vicious (aqur) dog?
A) It is permissible like any other dog
B) It is not permissible due to lack of benefit
C) It is permissible only if it is trained
D) It is permissible with conditions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs and predatory animals; only Abu Yusuf restricts the sale of vicious, untrainable dogs based on lack of benefit.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Ruling on Selling Dogs and Predatory Animals in the Hanafi School
Inter-madhhab dispute on selling dogs and predatory animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on selling dogs and predatory animals, identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit selling beneficial dogs and predators; Abu Yusuf restricts non-beneficial dogs; Shafi'is forbid all.

The sale of dogs and predatory animals is a classic point of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi school and others. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, its internal variation, and the opposing evidence, helping you see how proof and principle interact in a contested area.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi position
Identify the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: sale of dogs, cheetahs, and all predators is permissible (mubah) whether trained or not, based on benefit (entifa').
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference
Note Abu Yusuf's restriction: he forbids the sale of the 'aquur' dog (untrainable, non-beneficial). Later authorities (al-Mabsut) consider this the correct view for untrained dogs.
3
Identify the opposing school
State the Shafi'i position: sale of dogs is absolutely impermissible because dogs are impure in essence.
4
Analyze the proofs
Examine the Hanafi dalil (istihsan, benefit, compensation hadith) and the Shafi'i dalil (impurity, hadith 'thaman al-kalb min al-suht'). Understand how each school interprets the texts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude the practical consequence: Hanafis may transact in dogs for legitimate purposes; Shafi'is may not. Also note the effect on liability (daman) for killing a dog.
