Miscellaneous issues. The sale of a dog, cheetah, and predators is valid whether trained or not. This is according to us (Hanafis). The commentator says: 'Miscellaneous issues' – know first that 'masa'il' is plural of 'mas'ala', which linguistically means question, and according to scholars: it is a claim insofar as a question may be raised about it or its proof. It may also refer to propositions whose clarification is sought in sciences, mostly theoretical, sometimes necessary, introduced in the science either because they need a reminder to remove obscurity, or to explain their why-ness, because a proposition may be self-evident in its that-ness but unknown in its why-ness, like fire being burning – its that-ness is known but its why-ness unknown. Second: 'shatta' is plural of 'shatit', meaning scattered. 'Shatta al-amr' and 'istashatta' and 'tashattata' mean the thing became scattered. 'Qawmun shatta' and 'ashyaa'u shatta' and 'jaa'oo ashtatan' mean separate. Third: It is the habit of authors to mention after chapters what resemble them of issues, so they become issues from various chapters, sometimes titled 'Masa'il manthurah' and sometimes 'Masa'il shatta', meaning the same. The commentator says: 'This is according to us' – clarification: The sale of dogs, cheetahs, and all predators is absolutely permissible according to us, whether trained or not. According to Abu Yusuf, the sale of dogs is permissible except the 'aquur' – i.e., the one that does not accept training, because there is no benefit in it. In 'Al-Hidayah' it is reported from Abu Yusuf that the sale of a 'aquur' dog is not permissible. This indicates that it is a narration from him, while in 'Nawadir Hisham' from Muhammad the permissibility of selling the 'aquur' and guarantee of its killer is stated, and from Abu Yusuf the prohibition of selling the 'aquur'. According to the Shafi'i school, the sale of dogs is absolutely not permissible because the dog is impure in essence according to him. According to us, it is permissible based on benefiting from it and its skin. In 'Al-Mabsut' it is mentioned: the sale of a 'aquur' dog that does not accept training is not permissible, and this is the correct view of the madhhab. Similarly, the lion if it accepts training and is used for hunting its sale is permissible; but if it does not accept training and hunting it is not permissible. The cheetah and falcon accept training so their sale is permissible in any case. A response to Abu Yusuf's argument: Benefit is derived from its skin because it is purified by tanning, and the destroyer is liable because the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled for a dog forty dirhams without specifying a type, as mentioned in 'Majma' al-Anhur' and others. But Al-Tahawi narrated in 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar' from Abdullah ibn Amr that he ruled for a hunting dog killed by a man forty dirhams, so this is specific to a type. Understand. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of a dog is absolutely not permissible, and this is the saying of Ahmad and some Malikis. As for keeping a dog for hunting, guarding crops, livestock, or houses, it is permissible by consensus as stated by Al-Shamni. The reason for the absolute impermissibility according to Al-Shafi'i is that the dog is impure in essence, and impurity indicates degradation of the subject, while the permissibility of sale indicates its honoring. Also, Ibn Hibban narrated in his Sahih from Abu Hurairah that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the dowry of a prostitute, the price of a dog, and the earning of a cupper are unlawful.' The meaning of 'mahr al-baghay' is:
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of dogs, cheetahs, and predatory animals. The author begins by clarifying the term 'masa'il shatta' (miscellaneous issues), explaining its linguistic and technical meaning. The core ruling is that according to the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad), the sale of dogs, cheetahs, and all predators is absolutely permissible, whether trained or not, because benefit can be derived from them (e.g., hunting, guarding, and using their skins). However, Abu Yusuf restricts this by prohibiting the sale of a 'aquur' dog (one that does not accept training and is not beneficial). The correct view in the madhhab, as stated in al-Mabsut, is that the sale of such a dog is not permissible. The Shafi'i school holds that the sale of dogs is absolutely impermissible because dogs are impure in essence. The Hanafi response is based on the principle of permissibility due to benefit (istihsan). The passage also addresses the hadith about the Prophet's ruling on compensation for a dog (forty dirhams) and the hadith condemning the price of a dog. The author shows that the hadith about compensation is specific to hunting dogs, and the hadith about the price being unlawful is interpreted as referring to dogs that are not beneficial. The commentary emphasizes that Hanafis prioritize benefit and istihsan over analogy.

Key Terms
مسائل شتى (masa'il shatta): Miscellaneous issues collected from various chapters after a main section
عقور (aquur): A dog that does not accept training and is not beneficial for hunting or guarding
سباع (siba'): Predatory animals like lions, leopards, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Absolutely permissible, as per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

C) Not permissible, as it provides no benefit.
D) Permissible only if the skin is used.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis permit the sale of trained, beneficial dogs and predators, but prohibit the sale of untrainable animals that offer no benefit.

Selling Dogs: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Positions
Inter-madhhab dispute with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling dogs, identify the Shafi'i objection, and explain the Hanafi response using the hadith of Ibn Abbas and the concept of mal mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
A dog's benefit determines its sale: beneficial dogs are mal mutaqawwim, ferocious ones are not.

This lesson examines the classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the permissibility of selling dogs. You will see how the Hanafi school distinguishes between beneficial dogs (hunting, guarding) and harmful dogs, using hadith and rational arguments to support their view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling for beneficial dogs (e.g., hunting, guard) and for ferocious uneducable dogs. What is the basis of the distinction?
Refer to al-Mabsut for the correct madhhab position.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Explain why Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits all dog sales. What are his main evidences? Consider the concept of najis al-ayn and the hadith from Abu Hurayra.
The Shafi'i view is also held by Ahmad and some Malikis.
3
Examine the conflicting proofs
Study the two hadiths: the Hanafi proof (Ibn Abbas: permission for hunting dog) and the Shafi'i proof (Abu Hurayra: price of dog is illicit). How does each school use these texts?
Note the chain of narration and the principle of takhsis.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school reconciles the conflicting hadith. Why is the hadith of Ibn Abbas considered a mukhassis (specifier) for beneficial dogs?
Also consider the rational argument: dogs are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) because benefit is derived from them.

جواز بيع الكلب مطلقاً كما قال به الشافعي أنه نجس العين، والنجاسة مشعرة بذلة المحل، وجواز البيع معلم بإعزازه. وأيضاً ما روى ابنُ حِبَّانَ في صحيحه عن أبي هريرة له : إنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «إن مهر البغي وثمن الكلب، وكسب الحاجم من السحت»، والمراد بمهر البغي : أجرة الزانية، والسحت بمعنى الحرام. «الفتح» ولنا : ما روى الإمام أبو حنيفة الله في مسنده» : عن الهيثم عن عكرمة عن ابن عباس له أنه قال : «رخص رسولُ الله الله في ثمن كلب الصيد»، قال صاحب (۲) : هذا سند جيد، فإنَّ الهيثم ذكرَهُ ابنُ حِبَّانَ في «الثقات» من أثبات التابعين ، فهذا الحديث على رأيهم يصلحُ مخصصاً ، والمخصص بيان للمراد العام فيجوز، وإن كان دونه في القوة عندهم، حتى أجازوا تخصيص العام القاطع بخبر الواحد ابتداء ، فبطل مدعاهم من عموم منع البيع. ثم دليل التخصيص مما يعلل ويخرج من العام مرّة أخرى ، وتعليل إخراج كلب الصيد ساطع أنه لكونه منتفعاً به، وخصوص الاصطيادِ ملغي فصار الكلب المنتفع به خارجاً، سواء انتفع به في صيد أو حراسة ماشية. انتهى. ولأنه منتفع به حراسة واصطياداً ، أما انتفاعه به حراسة ؛ فلان كل كلب يحفظ بيت صاحبه ويمنع الأجانب عن الدخول فيه، ويغيرُ على الجاني بنباحه عليه، وأما الانتفاع به اصطياداً فظاهر ، فكان الكلب مالاً متقوّماً، وكلُّ مال متقوم يجوز بيعه، فكذا يجوز بيع الكلب. أمَّا كونُهُ مالاً ؛ فلأنَّ المال ما خلق لمصلحة الآدمي شرعاً، وهذا كذلك. وأما كونه متقوّماً ؛ فلانه محرز مأذون شرعاً في الانتفاع والملك يثبت بالإحراز بدار الإسلام، والتقوم بالتموّل، وكلاهما مأذون فيه شرعاً، إذ قد أذن الشرع في اقتناء كلب الماشية والصيد، وإذا كان كذلك فيجوز بيعه. كذا في «الفتح»(۳)، بخلاف الهوام المؤذية ؛ لأنه لا ينتفع بها، بل هي مضرّة. وأما الجواب عن استدلال الشافعي رواية، فبأنَّ ما رواه محمول على ابتداء الإسلام ، فإنّهم كانوا ألغوا اقتناء الكلاب ، فكانت الكلاب فيهم تؤذي الصبيان
And according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, the sale of a ferocious dog is not permissible. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, the sale of a dog is not permissible at all, based on the fact that it is ritually impure in itself according to him. And according to us, it is only permissible based on the benefit derived from it and its skin. So it is mentioned in al-Mabsut: Indeed, the sale of a ferocious dog that does not accept training is not permissible, and he said: This is the correct [position] of the madhhab. And likewise the ruling regarding a lion: if it accepts training and is used for hunting, then its sale is permissible; and if it does not accept training and hunting with it, it is not permissible. The cheetah and falcon accept training, so their sale is permissible in all cases. And a response was given to Abu Yusuf's argument: that benefit is derived from its skin because it is purified by tanning, and the one who destroys it is liable, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, judged regarding a dog with forty dirhams without specifying its type, as mentioned in Majma' al-Anhur and others. And in it that al-Tahawi narrated in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar from Abdullah ibn Amr that he judged regarding a hunting dog that a man killed with forty dirhams, so this is specific to a type. So understand. And al-Shafi'i said: The sale of a dog is absolutely not permissible, and this is the statement of Ahmad and some of Malik's companions. As for keeping a dog for hunting, or for guarding crops, livestock, or houses, it is permissible by consensus, as explicitly stated by al-Shumunni. And the reason for the impermissibility of selling a dog absolutely, as al-Shafi'i said, is that it is ritually impure in itself, and impurity indicates the degradation of the object, and the permissibility of sale indicates its honor. And also what Ibn Hibban narrated in his Sahih from Abu Hurayra: the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed, the dowry of a prostitute, the price of a dog, and the earnings of a cupper are illicit (suh't).' And by 'dowry of a prostitute' is meant the wages of a fornicatress, and 'suh't' means forbidden. al-Fath: And our proof is what Imam Abu Hanifa narrated in his Musnad from al-Haytham from Ikrima from Ibn Abbas that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, permitted the price of a hunting dog.' The commentator said: This chain is good, for al-Haytham was mentioned by Ibn Hibban in al-Thiqat among the reliable tabi'un. So this hadith, according to their view, serves as a specifier, and the specifier clarifies the general meaning, so it is permissible, even if it is weaker than theirs, until they allowed the specification of a definitive general [text] with a solitary hadith initially. So their claim of a general prohibition of sale is nullified. Then the evidence of specification is that it is reasoned and removed from the general once again, and the reason for removing the hunting dog is clear: that it is because it is benefited from, and the specific characteristic of hunting is disregarded, so the dog that is benefited from becomes excluded, whether it is benefited from for hunting or guarding livestock. End quote. And because benefit is derived from it for guarding and hunting. As for benefiting from it for guarding, because every dog protects its owner's house and prevents strangers from entering it, and attacks the aggressor with its barking. As for benefiting from it for hunting, it is obvious. So the dog is valuable property, and every valuable property is permissible to sell, so likewise the sale of a dog is permissible. As for it being property, because property is what is created for the benefit of humans legally, and this is so. And as for it being valuable, because it is acquired and legally permitted to benefit from, and ownership is established by acquisition in the abode of Islam, and value is by being considered wealth, and both are legally permitted, since the law has permitted keeping dogs for livestock and hunting. And if that is the case, then its sale is permissible. So it is in al-Fath, unlike harmful insects because no benefit is derived from them; rather, they are harmful. And as for the response to al-Shafi'i's argument, it is that what he narrated is interpreted as referring to the beginning of Islam, for they had neglected keeping dogs, and dogs among them used to harm children.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of dogs, with particular focus on the Hanafi position that permits selling dogs from which benefit is derived, such as hunting dogs and guard dogs, while the Shafi'i school prohibits all dog sales due to dogs being najis al-ayn (inherently impure). The passage begins by noting that Abu Yusuf within the Hanafi school prohibits the sale of a ferocious dog that cannot be trained, while Shafi'i prohibits all dog sales. The correct Hanafi position, as stated in al-Mabsut, is that a ferocious dog that does not accept training cannot be sold, but a lion that accepts training and is used for hunting can be sold. Cheetahs and falcons always accept training, so their sale is always permissible. A response to Abu Yusuf's argument is given: benefit can be derived from the dog's skin after tanning, and the Prophet (ﷺ) judged a dog's value at forty dirhams without specifying type, indicating it has value. However, al-Tahawi's narration from Abdullah ibn Amr specifies a hunting dog, implying specificity. The Shafi'i position is then presented: prohibition of all dog sales, with consensus on keeping dogs for hunting, guarding crops, livestock, or houses. The Shafi'i evidence includes the hadith prohibiting the price of a dog (thaman al-kalb) as illicit. The Hanafi response provides the proof from Abu Hanifa's Musnad: a hadith from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet (ﷺ) permitted the price of a hunting dog. This hadith, though possibly weaker, can serve as a mukhassis (specifier) for the general prohibition, so dogs from which benefit is derived are excluded. The passage concludes with a rational argument that a dog is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) because it benefits humans in guarding and hunting, and the law permits keeping such dogs, so their sale is permissible. Finally, the hadith cited by Shafi'i is interpreted as referring to the early period of Islam when dogs were neglected and caused harm.

Key Terms
الكلب العقور (al-kalb al-aqur): A ferocious dog that does not accept training and is harmful rather than beneficial.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized as valuable, with permitted benefit and considered wealth.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Inherently impure, meaning the substance itself is considered ritually impure according to some schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under which condition is the sale of a dog permissible?
A) When the dog is a puppy
B) When the dog is beneficial, such as for hunting or guarding
C) When the dog is sold by a Muslim
D) The sale of a dog is never permissible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling dogs from which benefit is derived, as they are considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), while prohibiting the sale of harmful, uneducable dogs.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Beneficial Dogs: Hanafi Evidence and Refutation of Shafi'i
Hanafi proof for selling utility dogs with refutation of Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling beneficial dogs, cite the primary evidence, and refute the Shafi'i objection using the concepts of takhsis and abrogation.

Core Idea
Beneficial dogs are valuable property; their sale is permissible despite any general prohibition.

The permissibility of selling dogs is a point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Hanafis distinguish between dogs that provide lawful benefit and those that do not. This lesson presents the Hanafi evidence from hadith and rational argument, and responds to the Shafi'i objections.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that sale of beneficial dogs (hunting, guarding) is permissible. The key is that the dog is beneficial, regardless of the specific use.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that all dog sales are prohibited because dogs are impure and there are general hadith prohibiting their sale.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Shafi'is cite hadiths such as the prohibition of selling dogs (e.g., 'The Prophet cursed the one who sells dogs') and the ruling that dogs are najis al-'ayn.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond by: (1) citing a specific hadith permitting the price of a hunting dog, which specifies the general prohibition; (2) arguing that the prohibitive hadith was abrogated or pertained to early Islam; (3) noting that impurity does not prevent sale, as seen with dung.
The Hanafi response uses takhsis (specification) of a general text by a weaker but valid hadith.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow sale of beneficial dogs (e.g., guard dogs, herding dogs) while Shafi'is prohibit all dog sales. This affects everyday transactions.

Source Text

ولنا : ما روى الإمام أبو حنيفة في مسنده عن الهيثم عن عكرمة عن ابن عباس أنه قال : «رخص رسول الله في ثمن كلب الصيد»، قال صاحب : هذا سند جيد، فإن الهيثم ذكره ابن حبان في «الثقات» من أثبات التابعين، فهذا الحديث على رأيهم يصلح مخصصاً، والمخصص بيان للمراد العام فيجوز، وإن كان دونه في القوة عندهم، حتى أجازوا تخصيص العام القاطع بخبر الواحد ابتداء، فبطل مدعاهم من عموم منع البيع. ثم دليل التخصيص مما يعلل ويخرج من العام مرة أخرى، وتعليل إخراج كلب الصيد ساطع أنه لكونه منتفعاً به، وخصوص الاصطياد ملغي فصار الكلب المنتفع به خارجاً، سواء انتفع به في صيد أو حراسة ماشية. انتهى. ولأنه منتفع به حراسة واصطياداً، أما انتفاعه به حراسة؛ فلأن كل كلب يحفظ بيت صاحبه ويمنع الأجانب عن الدخول فيه، ويغير على الجاني بنباحه عليه، وأما الانتفاع به اصطياداً فظاهر، فكان الكلب مالاً متقوماً، وكل مال متقوم يجوز بيعه، فكذا يجوز بيع الكلب. أما كونه مالاً؛ فلأن المال ما خلق لمصلحة الآدمي شرعاً، وهذا كذلك. وأما كونه متقوماً؛ فلأنه محرز مأذون شرعاً في الانتفاع والملك يثبت بالإحراز بدار الإسلام، والتقوم بالتمول، وكلاهما مأذون فيه شرعاً، إذ قد أذن الشرع في اقتناء كلب الماشية والصيد، وإذا كان كذلك فيجوز بيعه. كذا في «الفتح»، بخلاف الهوام المؤذية؛ لأنه لا ينتفع بها، بل هي مضرة. وأما الجواب عن استدلال الشافعي رواية، فبأن ما رواه محمول على ابتداء الإسلام، فإنهم كانوا ألغوا اقتناء الكلاب، فكانت الكلاب فيهم تؤذي الصبيان والغرباء، فنهوا عن اقتنائها، فشق ذلك عليهم، فأمروا بقتل الكلاب ونهوا عن بيعها تحقيقاً للزجر عن العادة المألوفة، ثم رخص لهم بعد ذلك ثمن ما يكون منتفعاً به، وهو كلب الصيد والحرث والماشية، ذكره العلامة السغناقي. لا يقال : ما رواه محرم، وما رويتم مبيح، والمحرم يقدم على المبيح؛ لأنا نقول : هذا إذا لم يدل الدليل على السبق، وأما إذا دل فلا، ودليل السبق هاهنا ظاهر؛ لأنه كان يشد في أمر الكلاب، قلنا لهم عن الاقتناء، وكان ذلك في ابتداء الإسلام فيكون المحرم سابقاً، وترى كثيراً من الأحكام نسخت بعد زمان. وأما الجواب عن درايته : فبأنا لا نسلم نجاسة عينه، ولو سلم فنجاسة عينه توجب حرمة أكله، لا منع بيعه، بل منع البيع يمنع الانتفاع شرعاً؛ ولهذا أجزنا بيع السرقين والبعر مع نجاسة عينهما؛ لإطلاق الانتفاع لهما عندنا، بخلاف العذرة، فإنه لم يطلق الانتفاع بها. وبخلاف الخمر والخنزير فإن كلا منهما ليس بمال متقوم في حقنا، وفي «المبسوط»: لا يجوز بيع هوام الأرض وما في البحر إلا السمك، وفي «النوازل»: يجوز بيع الحيات إذا كان ينتفع بها.
And for us (Hanafis): What Imam Abu Hanifa narrated in his Musnad from al-Haytham from Ikrimah from Ibn Abbas that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) permitted the price of a hunting dog.' The commentator said: This chain is good, for al-Haytham was mentioned by Ibn Hibban in al-Thiqat among the reliable successors. So this hadith according to their view is suitable as a specificator (mukhassis), and the specificator is an exposition of the general meaning, so it is permissible, even if it is lesser in strength according to them, until they allowed the specification of a general definitive (qat'i) text with an isolated hadith (khabar wahid) initially. So their claim of a general prohibition of sale is invalidated. Then the evidence for specification is that it is reasoned and taken out of the general once more, and the reasoning for excluding the hunting dog is clear: because it is beneficial, and the specificity of hunting is disregarded, so the beneficial dog is excluded, whether benefited by hunting or guarding livestock. End quote. And because it is beneficial for guarding and hunting. As for benefiting by guarding: every dog protects its owner's house and prevents strangers from entering, and alarms against the aggressor by barking. As for benefiting by hunting, it is obvious. So the dog is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), and every valuable property is permissible to sell, so similarly it is permissible to sell the dog. As for it being property (mal): property is what is created for the benefit of humans according to sharia, and this is so. And as for it being valuable: because it is preserved (muhraz) and permitted by sharia to benefit from, and ownership is established by preservation in Dar al-Islam, and value (taqawwum) is by being considered wealth (tamawwul), and both are permitted by sharia, since sharia has permitted keeping dogs for livestock and hunting. If so, then its sale is permissible. Thus in al-Fath. Contrary to harmful insects (hawamm mu'dhiya) because no benefit is derived from them, rather they are harmful. As for the response to the Shafi'i's proof by narration: that which he narrated is interpreted as relating to the beginning of Islam, for they had abandoned keeping dogs, so dogs among them harmed children and strangers, so they were forbidden to keep them, and that became difficult for them, so they were ordered to kill dogs and forbidden to sell them as a means to deter the habitual practice, then thereafter he (ﷺ) permitted for them the price of that which is beneficial, which is the dog of hunting, farming, and livestock, as mentioned by Allamah al-Sighnaqi. It cannot be said: What you (Shafi'i) narrated is prohibitive, and what we narrated is permissive, and the prohibitive takes precedence over the permissive; because we say: This is when there is no evidence indicating precedence, but if there is, then not. And the evidence of precedence here is apparent: because he (ﷺ) was strict regarding dogs, he forbade them from keeping them, and that was at the beginning of Islam, so the prohibitive is earlier, and you see many rulings abrogated after a time. As for the response to his rational proof (diraya): we do not concede the impurity of its substance (najis al-'ayn), and even if conceded, impurity of substance necessitates prohibition of eating it, not prevention of its sale. Rather, prevention of sale would prevent benefit legally; and thus we permit the sale of dung (sarqin) and animal droppings (ba'r) despite their impurity of substance, because benefit from them is unrestricted according to us, contrary to human waste (adhara) because benefit from it is not unrestricted. And contrary to wine and pork, because each of these is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in our right. And in al-Mabsut: It is not permissible to sell harmful insects of the earth and what is in the sea except fish. And in al-Nawazil: It is permissible to sell snakes if benefit is derived from them.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i school regarding the permissibility of selling dogs. The Hanafi position is that selling dogs that provide lawful benefit (such as hunting, guarding livestock, or property) is permissible, based on a hadith narrated by Imam Abu Hanifa in his Musnad where the Prophet (ﷺ) permitted the price of a hunting dog. The author, commenting on 'al-Fath', argues that this hadith is authentic enough to specify the general prohibition of selling dogs (if any) and that the key factor is benefit, not the specific purpose. The Hanafi proof is twofold: textual (the hadith) and rational (dogs are valuable property because they are useful for guarding and hunting, and sharia permits keeping them). The passage then refutes the Shafi'i objection that dogs are impure, noting that impurity does not prevent sale (as with dung) and that the prohibitive hadith was abrogated by the later permission. The author concludes that the permissibility extends to any beneficial use, and even snakes may be sold if useful.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is considered valuable in sharia, lawful to benefit from and preserved.
مخصص (mukhassis): A text or evidence that restricts the application of a general ruling.
كلب الصيد (kalb al-sayd): A dog trained for hunting, specifically mentioned in the hadith as permissible to sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, selling beneficial dogs is permissible based on a specific hadith of permission and the principle that valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) may be sold.

Dhimmi in Sales: Exception of Wine and Pork
Ruling on dhimmi's capacity in sales with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the legal capacity of dhimmis in sales and identify the exception for wine and pork, explaining the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
A dhimmi is like a Muslim in sales for all lawful goods, but not for wine and pork.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya establishes a foundational principle: non-Muslims residing under Muslim rule (dhimmis) are bound by the same transactional laws as Muslims. The only exception is for wine and pork, which are not considered valuable property in Islamic law. The commentary explains the rationale: dhimmis have accepted Islamic law by living in the Islamic state and paying jizya, and they need transactions for their livelihood, so the rules of sale apply equally.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the statement: 'The dhimmi in sale is like the Muslim.' Understand that this means dhimmis have the same legal capacity in sales as Muslims.
2
Understand the rationale
Explore why dhimmis are treated like Muslims: they have accepted Islamic law by residing and paying jizya, and they require transactions for necessities like food, clothing, and housing.
The rationale is based on necessity and implied acceptance of Islamic law.
3
Identify the exception
Note the exception: except for wine and pork. These are not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in Islamic law, so their sale is prohibited to both Muslims and dhimmis.
The text cites al-Bahr and al-Bada'i to confirm the prohibition.
4
Apply the ruling
Conclude that selling wine or pork to a dhimmi is prohibited (haram) and the contract is void (batil).
This is a binding rule for Muslims even though the dhimmi may be permitted to consume these items according to his own faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذمي في البيع كالمسلم إلا في الخمر والخنزير. قوله : كالمسلم ؛ لأن أهل الذمة قد التزموا أحكامنا بالإقامة في دارنا، وإعطاء الجزية، فهم مكلفون بالمعاملات، محتاجون إلى ما يبقى به نفوسهم كالمسلمين، ولا تبقى نفوسهم إلا بالطعام والشراب والكسوة والسكني، وغير ذلك ما لا بد للإنسان منه. وكل من هذه الأشياء الضرورية لا يتصور حصوله إلا بمباشرة الأسباب المعهودة المشروعة، ومن جملتها البيع، فيكون مشروعاً في حقهم أيضاً، كما هو مشروع في حقنا. قوله : إلا في الخمر والخنزير؛ قال في «البحر» معزياً إلى «البدائع» : لا يجوز.
The dhimmi in sale is like the Muslim except in wine and pork. His statement: 'like the Muslim' because the dhimmis have committed to our laws by residing in our land and paying jizya, so they are obligated in transactions, need what sustains their lives like Muslims, and their lives are not sustained except by food, drink, clothing, housing, and other necessities for man. And each of these necessary things cannot be obtained except by undertaking the customary legitimate means, among which is sale, so it is legislated for them as it is legislated for us. His statement: 'except in wine and pork' - said in al-Bahr quoting from al-Bada'i: It is not permissible.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: a dhimmi (a non-Muslim residing in an Islamic state) is treated like a Muslim in sales transactions, with the sole exception of wine and pork, which cannot be sold to them. The commentary explains the reasoning: dhimmis have accepted Islamic law by living in Muslim territory and paying jizya, so they are bound by the same transactional rules as Muslims. Since they need basic necessities like food, drink, and clothing, and these are obtained through sale, sale is legislated for them. However, wine and pork are exceptions because they are not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in Islamic law for Muslims, and thus cannot be sold even to dhimmis. The text cites al-Bahr and al-Bada'i affirming this prohibition.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim residing under the protection of an Islamic state, who pays jizya and is bound by Islamic transactional laws.
كالمسلم (ka al-muslim): Legally equivalent to a Muslim in the context of sales; subject to the same rules.
الخمر والخنزير (al-khamr wa al-khinzir): Wine and pork; specifically excluded from permissible sales to dhimmis because they are not considered valuable property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi is treated like a Muslim in all sales, but wine and pork are excluded from permissible sale to both.

Wine and Pig in Dhimmi Contracts: Property and Sale
Explains the legal treatment of wine and pig in dhimmi transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of wine and pig sales in dhimmi contracts and explain the reasoning based on istihsan and the treatment of dhimmis.

Core Idea
For dhimmis, wine and pig are valuable property; their sale is valid despite general prohibition.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi position on the property status of wine and pig when transacted by dhimmis. While these items are najis and prohibited for Muslims, the school recognizes them as tradeable goods for non-Muslims based on the principle that they do not believe in the prohibition and treating them as property respects their religious liberty. The passage also discusses an intra-school difference on whether dhimmis are addressed by the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify the ruling: wine and pig in a dhimmi's contract are like vinegar and sheep in a Muslim's contract; they are considered valuable property.
The matn gives the analogous status.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Some mashayikh hold that dhimmis may benefit from wine/pig, so it is property. The correct position (according to the text) is that the prohibition is general for all, but dhimmis are not prevented from selling because they do not believe in the prohibition and we are commanded to leave them to their beliefs.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the practical consequence
Sale by a Muslim of wine/pig is void; sale by a dhimmi is valid. If a Muslim buys juice and it becomes wine before possession, the sale is invalid.
The exception shows the purpose: allowing dhimmis to trade avoids harming them.
4
Note the extension by Al-Qahistani
Al-Qahistani extends the ruling to all prohibited drinks, and distinguishes between guarantee obligations.
This is a further application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهما في عقد الذمي كالخل والشَّاة في عقد المسلم، ومَن زوج مشريته قبل قبضها صح، فإن وطئت فقد قبضت، وإلا فلا. وهما في عقد الذمي كالخل والشَّاة في عقد المسلم، حتى يكون الخمر من ذواتِ الأمثال، والخنزير من ذواتِ القيم. [1] = (ومن زوج مشريته قبل قبضها صح ، فإن وطئت فقد قبضت، وإلا فلا) أي بمجرد التزويج لا يكون قابضاً استحساناً، والقياس أن يصير قابض يمنعون من بيع الخمر والخنزير ، أما على قول بعض مشايخنا ؛ فلأنه يباح الانتفاع شرعاً لهم، فكان مالا في حقهم. وعند البعض حرمتها ثابتة على العموم في حق المسلم والكافر؛ لأنَّ الكفار مخاطبون بالشرائع في المحرمات، وهو الصحيح من مذهب أصحابنا، فكانت الحرمة ثابتة على العموم في حقهم، لكنَّهم لا يمنعون من بيعهما ؛ لأنهم لا يعتقدون حرمتهما ويتمولونَهما، وقد أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون. انتهى. وفائدة الاستثناء أنَّ بيع الخمر والخنزير وشراء هما من المسلم باطل، حتى لـو اشترى عصيراً فلم يقبضه حتى صار خمراً فسد البيع، وأما عندهم فكل منهما مال متقوم، فلو لم يجز لهم بيعهما لم يظهر فائدة المالية في حقهم، فيكون إضراراً لهم. وقال القهستاني : في تخصيص الخمرِ إشعار بجواز بيع سائر الأشربة المحرمة ؛ ولذا وجب الضمانُ على المستهلك عنده ، ولم يجب عندهما. انتهى. [1] قوله : ومن زوج ...الخ ؛ يعني إذا اشترى وزوجها برجل قبل أن يقبضها من البائع صح التزويج ؛ لثبوتِ الولاية عليه بالشراء ؛ لأنه سبب الملك، فيجعل التصرُّف بالتزويج في المبيع المنقول قبل القبض كالإعتاق والتدبير في عدم الانفساخ. بخلاف التصرف بمثل البيع قبل القبض، إذ هو ينفسخ بهلاك المبيع قبل قبضه ؛ ولأنَّ القدرة على التسليم شرط في البيع، وذلك لا يكون إلا بعد القبض، وليس بشرط لصحة النكاح، فعلى هذا لا يجوز بيع الآبق ويجوز تزويجه. [٢] قوله : والقياس أن يصير قابضاً ؛ وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف هلکت بعد ذلك تهلك من مال المشتري ، كذا في «العناية»(۲).  لأنها تعيبت بالتَّزويج ، وجه الاستحسان أن التعيب الحقيقي استيلاء على المحل، فيكونُ قَبْضاً بخلافِ التَّعَيُّبِ الحكمي. [ ١ ] قوله : تعيبت ؛ تعيباً حكمياً ، ألا ترى أنه لو وجد المشتراة مزوّجة يردّها بالعيب. [٢) قوله : وجه الاستحسان ... الخ ; حاصله : أنه لم يتصل بها فعل حسي من المشتري، والتزويج فعل تعييب حكمي بمعنى تقليل الرغبات فيها، كنقصان السعر، والتفصيل في «النهر» و«البحر».
And they two (wine and pig) in the contract of a dhimmi are like vinegar and sheep in the contract of a Muslim. And whoever marries his purchased slave girl before taking possession of her, it is valid. If she has been consummated, then she has been taken possession of; otherwise not. And they two in the contract of a dhimmi are like vinegar and sheep in the contract of a Muslim, such that wine is among the items of like (mithliyyat) and pig is among the items of value (qimiyyat). [1] = (And whoever marries his purchased slave girl before taking possession of her, it is valid, if she has been consummated then she has been taken possession of; otherwise not.) Meaning: by mere marriage, he does not become a possessor by istihsan, and by qiyas he would become a possessor. They are prevented from selling wine and pig. According to some of our mashayikh: because it is legally permissible for them to benefit from them, so it is property in their regard. According to others: their prohibition is general in the case of both Muslim and disbeliever, because the disbelievers are addressed by the laws regarding prohibitions, and this is the correct position of our school, so the prohibition is established generally in their regard, but they are not prevented from selling them because they do not believe in their prohibition and they treat them as property, and we have been commanded to leave them and what they believe. End. And the benefit of the exception is that the sale and purchase of wine and pig by a Muslim is void, so that if he bought juice and did not take possession before it became wine, the sale becomes invalid. In their regard, each is a valuable property, so if they were not allowed to sell them, the benefit of property would not be apparent in their regard, and that would be harmful to them. Al-Qahistani said: The specification of wine suggests the permissibility of selling all other prohibited drinks; therefore, according to him, the consumer is liable for guarantee, whereas according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not obligatory. End. [1] His saying: 'And whoever marries...': meaning, if he buys and marries her to a man before taking possession from the seller, the marriage is valid due to the establishment of guardianship over her through purchase, because it is a cause of ownership. Thus, the disposal by marriage of a movable sold property before possession is like manumission and tadbir in that it does not lead to rescission. Unlike disposal by sale before possession, as it is rescinded by the destruction of the sold item before possession. And because the ability to deliver is a condition in sale, and that only occurs after possession, but it is not a condition for the validity of marriage. Therefore, on this basis, it is not permissible to sell a runaway slave but it is permissible to marry him. [2] His saying: 'And by qiyas he becomes a possessor': and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf, and after that it is destroyed from the property of the buyer. So in al-'Inaya. [page 289] Because it (the slave girl) is rendered defective by marriage. The point of istihsan is that the real defect is taking control of the object, so that constitutes possession, unlike the legal defect. [1] His saying: 'it is rendered defective': a legal defect. Do you not see that if he finds the purchased slave girl married, he returns her due to the defect. [2] His saying: 'the point of istihsan': its summary is that no physical act of the buyer has occurred, and marriage is a legal defect in the sense of reducing desire for her, like a decrease in price. And the detail is in al-Nahr and al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addresses two distinct but related legal issues. First, it discusses the status of wine and pig in the contracts of dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects). The text explains that in a dhimmi's contract, wine and pig are legally considered like vinegar and sheep in a Muslim's contract, meaning they are treated as valuable property. wine is considered among fungible goods (mithliyyat) and pig among non-fungible goods (qimiyyat). There is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: some mashayikh hold that since dhimmis are permitted to benefit from these items, they are property in their right. The correct position (as per the text) is that the prohibition of wine and pig is general for both Muslims and dhimmis because dhimmis are addressed by the laws regarding prohibitions. However, they are not prevented from selling them because they do not believe in the prohibition and treat them as property, and we are commanded to leave them to their beliefs. Consequently, sale of wine and pig by a Muslim is void, but by a dhimmi it is valid. Al-Qahistani extends this to all prohibited drinks. The second issue is the ruling on marrying a purchased slave girl before taking possession. The ruling is that such a marriage is valid. If the slave girl has been consummated, that constitutes possession; otherwise, she is not considered possessed. The text contrasts istihsan (juristic preference) with qiyas (analogy). By qiyas, the marriage would make the buyer a possessor (as per a narration from Abu Yusuf). However, the relied-upon position is istihsan: the mere marriage does not give possession because the buyer has not physically taken control. The marriage is like a legal defect (ta'ayyub hukmi) which reduces desire but does not count as physical possession. The text further explains that marriage before possession is valid because guardianship is established through purchase, unlike sale before possession which is invalid due to lack of ability to deliver. The lesson highlights the nuanced application of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine or any alcoholic beverage that is prohibited for Muslims but may be considered property for dhimmis.
خنزير (khinzir): Pig, similar to wine in terms of property status in dhimmi contracts.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a dhimmi sells wine to another dhimmi, the sale is:
A) Void, because wine is najis and prohibited for all.
B) Valid, because wine is considered property for dhimmis.
C) Valid only if the ruler permits.
D) Void, because dhimmis are also addressed by the prohibition.

Key Takeaway
Wine and pig are tradeable property for dhimmis, but not for Muslims, balancing prohibition with religious liberty.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Marrying a Purchased Slave Girl Before Possession: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Explains the ruling on marriage before possession and the role of istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why marrying a purchased slave girl before possession is valid according to Hanafi istihsan and distinguish between qiyas and istihsan in this context.

Core Idea
Marriage before possession is valid, but possession only occurs through consummation, not mere marriage.

This lesson delves into a specific issue of sales: the validity of marrying a purchased slave girl before physical possession (qabd). By qiyas, marriage would be considered constructive possession; however, the Hanafi school applies istihsan, ruling that marriage does not transfer possession unless consummated. The passage also discusses an intra-Hanafi difference, with Abu Yusuf holding the qiyas position.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
The matn: 'Whoever marries his purchased slave girl before possession, it is valid; if she has been consummated, then she has been possessed; otherwise not.'
2
Understand the effect on possession
Marriage alone does not make the buyer a possessor (qabid). Only consummation (wat') constitutes actual possession.
This is the istihsan position.
3
Analyze the qiyas vs istihsan
By qiyas, marriage would make him a possessor because it is a form of disposal. By istihsan, marriage is a legal defect (ta'ayyub hukmi) that does not amount to physical control.
The commentary explains that real defect (ta'ayyub haqiqi) would be possession; marriage is merely a legal defect like reduction in price.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf narrates that by qiyas, marriage would make him a possessor (so if she perishes, it is from his property). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the istihsan view.
The mufta bihi is the istihsan position.
5
Compare with sale before possession
Sale before possession is invalid and rescinded if the item perishes, because ability to deliver is a condition. Marriage does not require ability to deliver, so it is valid.
This contrast highlights the difference between sales and marriages.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or necessity.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or constructive possession through lawful control.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning to derive a ruling for a new case based on an existing case with a shared effective cause.

According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer marries his purchased slave girl before taking possession, the marriage is:

Key Takeaway

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Selling the Absent Buyer's Property: When and How
Ruling with rational justification by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an item purchased on credit when the buyer is absent, and explain the istihsan-based justification.

Core Idea
The judge may sell the absent buyer's item only if his location is unknown, as an implicit sale to recover the price.

Imagine you sell a car on credit, and the buyer moves away without paying. Can you sell the car to recover your money? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: it depends on whether the buyer's location is known. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the reasoning, and the alternative scholarly view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the core situation: a seller has delivered the item but not received the price; the buyer has disappeared.
The item is still in the seller's possession.
2
Distinguish the two cases
Separate the ruling based on whether the buyer's location is known or unknown. If known, the seller must seek the buyer directly; if unknown, the judge may sell the item.
The text uses 'ghaybah ma'rufah' – a known absence.
3
Understand the prohibition and its exception
Explain why selling is prohibited when location is known (preserving the buyer's right) and why it is permitted when unknown (to prevent loss to the seller).
The exception relies on istihsan (juristic preference).
4
Analyze the justifications
The text addresses two objections: selling without judgment against an absentee, and selling before possession. The evidence is not for judgment but to establish the sale, and the sale is implicit, not the primary goal.
These are rational (aqli) proofs.
5
Note the alternative opinion
Some Hanafis suggest the judge should appoint someone to take possession before selling, but the text criticizes this as it removes the seller's hand before payment.
The preferred view is direct sale by the judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن اشترى شيئاً ، وغاب غيبة معروفة ، فأقامَ بائعُهُ بَيِّنَةٌ على أنَّه بَاعَهُ منه لم يَبيع في دَيْنِه) : أي في تَمَنِ المبيع، بل يطلبُ الثَّمن من المشتري، فإنَّ مكانَهُ م، وإن جَهلَ مكانه بيعَ ) : أي بيعَ وأَوْفَى الثَّمَن . [٣]قوله : فإنَّ مكانه معلوم ; فيمكن أن يتوصل إلى حقه بالذهاب إليه، فلا حاجة إلى بيعه ; لأنَّ فيه إبطال حق المشتري في العين. [٤]قوله : أي بيع وأوفى الثمن ; أي باعَه القاضي ; لأنه نصب ناظراً للعاجزين ، ونظرهما في بيعه ; لأنَّ البائع يصل إلى حقه، والمشتري تَبْرُؤُ ذمته من ثمنه. فإن قلت : القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز، فكيف جاز هاهنا. قلت : ليست بينةُ البائع هنا للقضاء على الغائب، وإنمـا هـي لنفي التهمة وانكشاف الحال. فإن قلت : هذا بيع قبل القبض وهو غير جائز، فكيف يباع. قلت : هذا البيع ليس بمقصود هنا ; لأنَّ المقصود إحياء حقه في ضمنه فصح بيعه ، والشيء يصح ضمناً ، وإن لم يصح قصداً ، وقيل : يوكِّلُ القاضي من يقبضُهُ ثُمَّ يبيعُه ، وفيه نظر لما فيه من إبطال يد البائع قبل إيفاء الثمن، ثم إذا بيع وأوفى ثمنه فإن فضل شيء منه يمسك للمشتري الغائب ; لأنه يدلُّ ملكه ، فإن لم يف بالدين وبقي شيء منه ،
And whoever buys something and is absent with a known absence, and his seller establishes evidence that he sold it to him, he (the seller) does not sell it for his debt (i.e., the price of the sale), but he demands the price from the buyer. If his place is known, (he does not sell), and if his place is unknown, he sells (i.e., the judge sells and pays the price). [3] His saying: 'if his place is known': it is possible for him to reach his right by going to him, so there is no need to sell it, because that would nullify the buyer's right in the property. [4] His saying: 'he sells and pays the price': meaning the judge sells it, because he is appointed as a guardian for the incapable, and his consideration is in selling it, because the seller reaches his right and the buyer's liability is discharged from the price. If you say: judgment against an absent person is not permissible, so how is it allowed here? I say: The seller's evidence here is not for judgment against the absent person, but rather to remove suspicion and clarify the situation. If you say: This is a sale before possession, which is not permissible, so how can it be sold? I say: This sale is not the objective here; because the objective is to revive his right within it, so its sale is valid, and a thing is valid implicitly even if not valid explicitly. And it is said: The judge should appoint someone to take possession then sell it, and there is a consideration in this because of the nullification of the seller's hand before payment of the price. Then when it is sold and its price is paid, if there is a surplus, it is kept for the absent buyer because it indicates his ownership. If it does not cover the debt and something remains,
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This lesson deals with a practical scenario in sale transactions: when a buyer disappears after purchasing an item on credit, and the seller wants to recover the price. The ruling distinguishes between two cases: if the buyer's location is known, the seller cannot sell the item; he must seek the buyer and demand payment. If the location is unknown, the judge may sell the item after the seller establishes evidence of the sale. The commentary addresses two objections: first, that judgment against an absent person is generally not allowed, but here the evidence is not to issue a judgment but to clarify the situation and remove suspicion. Second, that selling before possession is prohibited, but here the sale is not the primary purpose; it is a means to recover the seller's right, so it is valid implicitly. There is also an alternative opinion that the judge should appoint someone to take possession first and then sell, but this is criticized because it removes the seller's possession before payment. After the sale, any surplus is kept for the absent buyer, and if the price does not cover the debt, the seller retains a claim.

Key Terms
غيبة معروفة (ghaybah ma'rufah): An absence that is known and recognized, i.e., the buyer is not simply missing but his absence is established.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, proof, typically two witnesses, to establish the sale transaction.
بيع قبل القبض (bay' qabl al-qabd): Sale of an item before the seller has taken possession from a previous seller; generally invalid except in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, when may the judge sell the absent buyer's property?
A) When the buyer's absence is known and the seller demands it.
B) When the buyer's location is unknown and the seller provides evidence of the sale.
C) When the buyer has been absent for more than one year.
D) When the seller obtains permission from the buyer's relatives.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the judge to sell the absent buyer's property to recover the price only when the buyer's location is unknown, based on necessity and istihsan.

Selling an Absent Buyer's Item: Known vs. Unknown Absence
Ruling and proofs on seller's sale of absent buyer's item when location unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an absent buyer's item when his location is known vs. unknown, and explain the istihsan basis for the sale when location unknown.

Core Idea
If the buyer's location is unknown, the judge may sell the item to recover the debt, even though it is a sale before possession, based on istihsan.

When a seller has not received payment and the buyer is absent, the seller needs a remedy. The Hanafi school distinguishes between two scenarios: if the buyer's location is known, the seller cannot sell the item but must demand payment directly. If the location is unknown, the judge may sell the item after proof is established. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the istihsan reasoning that permits this sale despite it being a sale before possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two cases: (1) buyer absent with known location, (2) buyer absent with unknown location. The ruling for each is different.
The distinction hinges on the possibility of contacting the buyer.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
Known location: seller cannot sell the item; he must claim the price from the buyer. Unknown location: the judge sells the item after the seller proves the sale, and the proceeds go to the debt.
The judge acts as an overseer for the incapacitated.
3
Identify the proof: istihsan
The passage explains that the ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The item becomes defectively affected by the buyer's use even without physical act, analogizing from the case of marriage. Similarly, the seller's right to payment necessitates sale when the buyer is untraceable.
Istihsan overrides the general prohibition of selling before possession.
4
Understand the objection and response
Objection: 'This is a sale before possession, which is invalid.' Response: The sale is not intended for itself but as an incidental means to recover the debt. Hence it is valid incidentally, even if not valid as a primary object.
The principle: 'A thing is valid incidentally, even if not valid intentionally.'
5
Note the practical conclusion
After sale and payment of debt, any surplus is kept for the absent buyer. If the sale does not cover the debt, the seller may pursue the buyer later.
This ensures justice for both parties.

And whoever buys something, and is absent with known absence, and his seller establishes proof that he sold it to him, he does not sell it for his debt: meaning for the price of the sold item, rather he demands the price from the buyer, for his location is known. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the seller's remedy when the buyer is absent. The rule: if the buyer's location is known, the seller cannot sell the item but must demand the price from him. If the location is unknown, the judge may sell the item after the seller proves the sale, and the price is used to pay the debt. The proof is istihsan: the item becomes defectively affected by the buyer's use (e.g., marriage) but since no physical act by the buyer caused the defect, it is treated as a legal defect that allows return. The commentator addresses objections: the proof is not for judgement on the absent person but to clear suspicion; and the sale before possession is valid incidentally because the goal is to recover the right. Any surplus after paying the debt is kept for the absent buyer.

Key Terms
غيبة معروفة (ghaybah ma‘rufah): Known absence: the buyer's location is known and the seller can reach him.
بيع في دينه (bay‘ fi daynihi): Selling for his debt: selling the purchased item to recover the owed price.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: preferring this ruling over strict analogy due to necessity.

Present Co-Buyer's Right to Pay and Withhold Absent Partner's Share
Intra-Hanafi dispute on co-buyer's rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a present co-buyer is a volunteer when paying the full price, and explain the five points of disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Necessity gives the present co-buyer the right to withhold the absent partner's share until reimbursement.

When two people buy an item together and one is absent, the present buyer may have to pay the full price to the seller to get his own share. But is he a volunteer who cannot reclaim the absent partner's portion? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say he is compelled by necessity, not a volunteer, so he can withhold the absent share until paid. Abu Yusuf disagrees. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the five practical consequences of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Dispute
Start by noting the ruling: the present buyer may pay, take possession, and withhold the absent partner's share until reimbursed. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Then note that Abu Yusuf opposes this on the basis that paying another's debt without permission makes one a volunteer.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, so both views are within the school.
2
Understand the Core Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The majority argue that the present buyer is mudtar (compelled): he cannot benefit from his share without paying the entire price because the sale is one transaction and the seller can withhold the whole item. Necessity (idhtirar) overrides the default rule that paying another's debt is voluntary.
The analogy with an agent who pays from his own pocket and retains the item until reimbursement is used.
3
Enumerate the Five Points of Disagreement
The commentator lists five sub-issues: (1) taking possession of the entire item, (2) withholding the absent's share, (3) recourse for payment, (4) forcing the seller to accept payment for the absent's share, (5) forcing the seller to deliver the absent's share. In all five, the majority rule in favor of the present buyer; Abu Yusuf rules against.
This shows the practical scope of the dispute.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, the present buyer can physically retain the absent partner's share until paid. Under Abu Yusuf, the absent partner can claim his share immediately, and the present buyer must seek separate reimbursement.
This affects risk and possession rights.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi Position
The text cites 'al-Ramz' and the commentator's analysis indicates the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the relied-upon position in the school.
Therefore, for fatwa, the present buyer is not a volunteer and may withhold.

And if two buy and one is absent, then the present one may pay his price, take possession, and withhold it when the absent one comes until he takes his share. (And if two buy and one is absent, then the present one may pay his price, take possession, and withhold it when the absent one comes until he takes his share: this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; because he is compelled, he cannot benefit from his share except by paying the entire price. So if he pays, he is not a volunteer. When the absent one comes, he does not take his share unless he pays the price of his share to his partner. And according to Abu Yusuf, he is a volunteer in paying his partner's share, because he paid another's debt without his permission. [1] His statement: this is according to...etc.; and the disagreement is in five places: first, taking possession of the entire sale item on the assumption of paying all the price. Second, withholding the absent one's share when he comes. Third, recourse for what he paid. Fourth, forcing the seller to accept what the present one paid from the absent's share: according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the seller is compelled; according to him (Abu Yusuf), no. Fifth, forcing the seller to deliver the absent's share of the sale item to the present one when full price is paid: according to the two, he is compelled; according to him, no. Thus in 'al-Ramz'. [2] His statement: that is because...etc.; its clarification: the present one in paying all the price is compelled, because he cannot imagine benefiting from his share except if he pays all the price, since the sale is one transaction, and the seller has the power to withhold as long as any part of the price remains. And one who is compelled has recourse established, so he has the right to withhold from the absent one until he recovers his right, just as the agent, when he pays the price from his own, has the sale item from the principal until he gives all the price. (3) His statement: and according to Abu Yusuf...etc.; its summary: He said: when he pays the price, he takes only his share by way of alternate possession (muhayah), and he is a volunteer in what he paid for his partner, because he paid the absent one's debt without his permission.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the rights of a present co-buyer when his partner is absent. The ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) is that the present buyer may pay the entire price to the seller, take possession of the whole item, and retain the absent partner's share until the absent one reimburses him. The reason (ta'lil) is necessity: the sale is one transaction, so the present partner cannot benefit from his share without paying the full price; he is compelled, not a volunteer. Abu Yusuf disagrees, considering the present buyer a volunteer in paying the absent partner's share because he paid another's debt without authorization. The commentator enumerates five points of disagreement: (1) taking possession of the entire item; (2) withholding the absent's share; (3) recourse for what was paid; (4) forcing the seller to accept payment from the present for the absent's share; (5) forcing the seller to deliver the absent's share to the present. In all these, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) rule in favor of the present buyer, while Abu Yusuf opposes.

Key Terms
مضطر (mudtar): One who is compelled by circumstances; here, the present buyer is forced to pay all the price to get his own share.
متبرع (mutabarri‘): A volunteer who pays another's debt without expectation of recourse; Abu Yusuf considers the present buyer such.
مهاياة (muhayah): Alternate possession: Abu Yusuf says the present buyer only takes his share on a time-sharing basis, not full possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The absent partner authorized him in advance.
D) Islamic law always favors the present buyer.

Key Takeaway
The present co-buyer's payment of the full price is not voluntary but compelled by necessity, giving him the right to withhold the absent partner's share until reimbursed.

Ruling on Two Buyers When One is Absent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on joint purchase with absent buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a present buyer's right to pay the full price and hold the absent partner's share, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A present buyer compelled by necessity is not a volunteer; he may pay the full price and hold the absent partner's share.

When two persons jointly purchase an item and one is absent, the present buyer faces a dilemma: pay the full price to get his share or not. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling and the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether the present buyer is a volunteer or compelled, and what consequences follow.

Guided Path
1
State the base scenario
Understand the initial situation: two buyers, one absent. What is the default ruling according to the matn?
The matn states the present may pay, possess, and hold the absent's share.
2
Identify the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the present buyer is compelled (mudtar) and thus not a volunteer. He may pay all, take possession, and hold the absent's share until reimbursed.
Their dalil: necessity (idhtirar) and analogy to the agent (wakil).
3
Understand the rationale for compulsion
Why is he mudtar? He cannot benefit from his share without paying the full price because the sale is one transaction and the seller can withhold until full payment.
The present is not a volunteer because he is forced by circumstances.
4
Identify the dissenting view (Abu Yusuf)
Abu Yusuf considers the present a volunteer (mutabarra') because he paid the debt of another without permission. He has no right of recourse or retention.
Abu Yusuf limits the present to taking only his share via muhayah (time-sharing).
5
Map the five points of divergence
The commentary lists five specific issues where they differ: taking possession of entire item, holding absent's share, recourse, compelling seller to accept payment, compelling seller to deliver absent's share.
In all five, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad allow; Abu Yusuf denies.

وإن اشترى اثنان وغاب واحد، فللحاضرِ دفع ثمنه ، وقبضُهُ وحبسهُ إِن حَضَرَ الغائبُ إلى أن يأخذ حصته : هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ؛ وذلك لأنه مضطر لا يُمْكِنُه الانتفاعُ بنصيبه إلا بأداء جميع الثَّمن، فإذا أداه لم يكن مُتَبَرِّعاً ، فإن حَضَرَ الغائبُ لا يأخذ حصَّتَهُ إِلا إِن سَلَّمَ ثَمَنَ حَصَّيَّهِ إلى شريكه ، وعند أبي يوسف هو متبرع في أداء حصة شريكه ؛ لأنَّه دَفَعَ ديـن غيرِهِ بغيرِ أمره. تبعه البائع إذا ظفر به، كذا في «المنح». [1] قوله : هذا عند...الخ ؛ والخلاف في مواضع : أحدها : في قبض جميع المبيع على تقدير إيفاء الثمن كله. والثاني : في حبس نصيب الغائب عنه إذا حضر. والثالث : من الرجوع عليه بما أدى. والرابع : في إجبار البائع على قبولِ ما أدَّاه الحاضر من نصيب الغائب، عندهما: يجبر، وعنده : لا . والخامس: في إجبار البائع على تسليم نصيب الغائب من المبيع إلى الحاضر عند إيفاء الثمن كلّه، فعندهما يجبر، وعنده : لا ، كذا في «الرمز» [2] قوله : وذلك لأنه...الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنَّ الحاضر في دفع كل الثمن مضطر، فإنّه لا يتصور له الانتفاع بنصيبه إلا إذا أدَّى جميع الثمن ؛ لكون البيع صفقة واحدة ، وللبائع قدرة الحبس ما بقي شيء من الثمن، والمضطر يثبت له الرجوع، فله حق الحبس عن الغائب إلى أن يستوفي حقه، كما أنَّ الوكيل إذا قضى الثمن من عنده فله حبس المبيع عن الموكل إلى أن يعطي جميع الثمن. [3] قوله : وعند أبي يوسف ... الخ ; حاصله : إنّه قال : إذا دفع ثمنه لا يأخذ إلا نصيبه بطريق المهاياة، وكان متبرّعاً فيما أدى عن صاحبه ؛ لأنه قضى دين الغائب بغير أمره ، فكان متبرعاً فيه، ولا جبر على المتبرع ، ولا رجوع في التبرعات، وهو أجنبي فلا يقبضه ؛ ولهذا لو كان حاضراً لكان متبرعاً بالإجماع.
And if two persons bought and one is absent, then for the present (buyer) is to pay the price, and to take possession and to hold it (the purchased item) if the absent one comes until he takes his share. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; and that is because he is compelled, he cannot benefit from his share except by paying the entire price; so if he pays, he is not a volunteer. Then if the absent one comes, he does not take his share unless he pays the price of his share to his partner. And according to Abu Yusuf: he is a volunteer in paying his partner's share because he paid the debt of another without his permission. The seller follows him if he finds him, as in al-Munah. [1] His statement: 'this is...etc.'; and the difference is in five points: first: in taking possession of all the sold item on the assumption of fulfilling the entire price. Second: in holding the absent one's share from him when he comes. Third: in recourse (against him) for what he paid. Fourth: in compelling the seller to accept what the present paid from the absent's share; according to them two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), he is compelled, and according to him (Abu Yusuf), no. Fifth: in compelling the seller to deliver the absent's share of the sold item to the present when the entire price is paid; according to them two, he is compelled, and according to him, no, as in al-Ramz. [2] His statement: 'and that is because...etc.'; its clarification: indeed the present in paying all the price is compelled; because it is not conceivable for him to benefit from his share except if he pays the entire price; because the sale is one transaction, and the seller has the power to withhold as long as any part of the price remains; and the compelled one has recourse established for him; so he has the right to hold (the item) from the absent until he recovers his right, just as when the agent pays the price from his own funds, he has the right to hold the sold item from the principal until he gives all the price. [3] His statement: 'and according to Abu Yusuf...etc.'; its summary: he said: when he pays the price, he does not take except his share by way of muhaayah, and he is a volunteer in what he paid on behalf of his partner; because he paid the debt of the absent without his order, so he is a volunteer therein, and there is no compulsion on a volunteer, and no recourse in donations, and he is a stranger so he does not take possession of it; and for this reason, if he were present, he would be a volunteer by consensus.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in the commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The base matn states that when two persons jointly purchase an item and one is absent, the present buyer may pay the full price, take delivery, and hold the absent partner's share until reimbursed. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad justify this by the present buyer's compulsion (idhtirar): he cannot obtain his share without paying the whole price, so he is not a volunteer (mutabarra'). His position is analogous to an agent (wakil) who pays the price and retains the goods. Imam Abu Yusuf dissents, arguing that paying another's debt without authorization makes the present a volunteer, without right of recourse or retention. The commentary lists five specific points of divergence: (1) taking possession of the entire item upon full payment, (2) holding the absent's share upon his return, (3) recourse for the amount paid, (4) compelling the seller to accept the payment for the absent's share, and (5) compelling the seller to deliver the absent's share to the present after full payment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by necessity and agency analogy.

Key Terms
مضطر (mudtar): One who is compelled by necessity; in this context, the present buyer is mudtar because he cannot obtain his share without paying the full price, so he is not considered a volunteer.
متبرع (mutabarra'): One who voluntarily does an act without expectation of compensation; here, the opposite of mudtar. Abu Yusuf considers the present mutabarra' in paying the absent's share.
مهاياة (muhayah): Division of usage based on time shares; in this context, the present would only take his share via muhayah according to Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position relied upon for fatwa, why is the present buyer in a joint purchase not considered a volunteer (mutabarra') when he pays the full price?
A) Because he received permission from the absent partner.
B) Because he is forced by the seller.
C) Because he is compelled (mudtar) to pay the full price to obtain his own share.
D) Because he intends to make a profit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, necessity (idhtirar) transforms what would normally be a voluntary act into a compelled act with legal rights of recourse and retention.

Purchase with Mixed Currency: Gold and Silver
Explains the ruling on obligatory payment when buying a slave girl with a mixed gold and silver price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of apportionment when a price is expressed in mixed units of gold and silver, and distinguish between the two phrasings.

Core Idea
When a quantity is attributed equally to gold and silver, each is paid half; when attributed collectively, each is paid by custom.

When you buy something with a price expressed in mixed units of gold and silver, the wording of the contract determines what you must pay. This lesson unpacks the two different phrasings covered in the Hidayah and derives a general principle that applies to all transactions with mixed measures or weights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrasings
The matn presents two cases: 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver' (adds mithqal to each individually) and 'a thousand of gold and silver' (adds the thousand to both collectively).
The key is the attribution of the unit (mithqal) versus the raw number.
2
Apply the rule of apportionment
In the first phrase, because 'mithqal' is attributed to both equally, each metal gets half: 500 mithqals each. In the second, because 'a thousand' is attributed to both, it defaults to the customary coin for each: mithqals for gold, dirhams of weight seven for silver.
This is based on the lack of priority when both are mentioned equally.
3
Generalize to other transactions
The text states this is a rule (qa'ida) for all transactions involving weighed, measured, counted, or length-measured items, such as dower, lease, and usurpation.
This shows the systematic nature of Hanafi fiqh: one principle governs many chapters.
4
Consider the role of custom (urf)
The dirham's weight is determined by local custom at the time of contract. The commentary notes that in Egypt, a dirham now refers to a copper coin of four weight-seven dirhams, unless silver is specified.
Custom can override the default, but only if it is sound and established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى أمة بألف مثقال من ذَهَب وفضةٍ يَحِبُّ من كل نصفه، وفي بألف من الذهب والفضة يجب من الذهب مثقايل، ومن الفضة دراهم وزن سبعة [1] قوله : بألف مثقال ؛ قيل : وكان الواجب أن يقيد المصنف بالجودة والرداءة أو الوسط ؛ لأنَّ الناس لا يتبايعونَ بالتَّبر، فلا بد من بيان الصفة قطعاً للمنازعة ؛ ولذا قيَّده محمد له بها في «الجامع الصغير»، وبيوع «الأصل»، ويجوز أن يقال : تركه لكونه معلوماً من أوَّل «كتاب البيوع» أنَّ ذلك لا بُدَّ منه ، كذا في «العناية» [٢] قوله : يجب من...الخ ؛ يعني يجب خمسمئة مثقال ذهب، وخمسمئة مثقال فضة ؛ لأنه أضاف المثقال إليهما على السواء، فيجب من كل واحد نصفه ؛ لعدم الأولوية، بخلاف ما إذا اشترى جارية بألف من الذهب والفضة، حيث يجب من الذهب مثاقيل، ومن الفضَّةِ دراهم ؛ لأنه أضاف الألف إليهما ، فينصرف إلى الوزن المعهود من كل منهما. وعلى هذا لو قال: على كُرِّ حنطة وشعير، وسمسم يجب عليه من كل جنس ثلث الكر، وهذه قاعدة في المعاملاتِ كلّها ؛ كالمهر والوصية والوديعة والغصب والإجارة وبدل الخلع وغيرِه في الموزون والمكيل والمعدود والمذروع. [٣] قوله : وزن سبعة ؛ ذكر في «الفتح» (۳) : إنَّ انصراف الدراهم إلى وزن سبعة إذا كان متعارفاً في بلد العقد، وأما في عرف مصر ، فلفظ الدرهم ينصرفُ الآن إلى زنة أربعة دراهم بوزن سبعة من الفلوس، إلا أن يعقد بالفضة فينصرف إلى درهم بوزن سبعة.
And if he buys a slave girl for a thousand mithqals of gold and silver, there is due from each half; and if for a thousand of gold and silver, there is due from gold mithqals, and from silver dirhams of weight seven. [1] His statement: 'for a thousand mithqals'; it is said: The author should have qualified with quality (good, bad, or medium) because people do not trade in bullion; so description is necessary to avoid dispute. Muhammad qualified it in al-Jami' al-Saghir and the Buyu' of al-Asl. But it may be said he left it out because it is known from the beginning of the Book of Sales that it is necessary, as in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'there is due from...etc.'; meaning, five hundred mithqals of gold and five hundred mithqals of silver are due; because he attributed the mithqal to both equally, so from each half is due, due to lack of priority. Contrary to if he bought a slave girl for a thousand of gold and silver, where mithqals of gold and dirhams are due; because he attributed the thousand to both, so it defaults to the customary weight of each. And upon this, if he said: 'upon a kurr of wheat and barley, and sesame', there is due from each species a third of the kurr, and this is a rule in all transactions, like dower, bequest, deposit, usurpation, lease, khul' compensation, and others in weighed, measured, counted, and measured-by-length items. [3] His statement: 'weight seven'; it is mentioned in al-Fath: that the default of dirhams to weight seven is when it is customary in the land of contract. And in the custom of Egypt, the term dirham now defaults to a coin of four dirhams of weight seven in copper, unless the contract is in silver, then it defaults to a dirham of weight seven.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage addresses the determination of the obligatory payment when the price of a slave girl is expressed in a combination of gold and silver. The matn distinguishes two phrasings: (1) 'a thousand mithqals of gold and silver' — here, because the term 'mithqal' is applied to each metal individually, the payment is 500 mithqals of gold and 500 mithqals of silver. (2) 'a thousand of gold and silver' — here, 'a thousand' is attributed to both together, so it defaults to the customary coin for each metal: mithqals for gold and dirhams of weight seven for silver. The commentary notes that Imam Muhammad specified the quality of metal in his works to avoid disputes, while the author of al-Hidayah may have omitted it due to prior knowledge. A general rule is then derived: whenever a quantity is attributed to multiple items, it is apportioned according to the customary weight or measure of each. This applies to all transactions involving mawzun, makil, ma'dud, and madhru' items. Finally, the definition of 'weight seven' is clarified: it refers to the standard silver dirham of seven daniqs, but local custom may override this, as in Egypt where a dirham is now taken as four copper dirhams of that weight unless silver is specified.

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight for gold; here it means the customary mithqal (approx. 4.25 grams of gold).
درهم وزن سبعة (dirham wazn sab'ah): A dirham of weight seven (daniqs), the standard silver dirham weight in Hanafi law.
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice; here, the custom of the land determines the weight of dirham when not explicitly stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys a slave girl for 'a thousand of gold and silver' (without mentioning mithqal), what is the obligatory payment?
A) Five hundred mithqals of gold and five hundred mithqals of silver.
B) Mithqals of gold and dirhams of silver according to custom.
C) Only gold mithqals.
D) Only silver dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The phrasing of a mixed-metal price determines the obligation: equal sharing when the unit is individually attached, custom when the number is collective.
