Division of Payment When Price is Gold and Silver Combined
Ruling on interpreting mixed metal price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for interpreting a price expressed in combined gold and silver, distinguishing between the two common wordings, and calculate the due amount in customary weights.

Core Idea
When a price is expressed in two metals, the wording determines whether to split equally or use customary weights.

When a contract states a price in combined gold and silver, the exact wording matters. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule: if the phrase uses 'mithqal of gold and silver', half is due in each; if it simply says 'a thousand of gold and silver', gold is paid by the mithqal and silver by the dirham weight seven. This principle extends to all transactions involving measured items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording of the price
Look for the exact phrase used: does it include 'mithqal' attached to both gold and silver, or is 'a thousand' simply attached to both metals?
e.g., 'a thousand mithqal of gold and silver' vs 'a thousand of gold and silver'.
2
Apply the rule for 'a thousand mithqal of gold and silver'
If 'mithqal' is mentioned, the thousand is split equally: 500 mithqal gold and 500 mithqal silver.
Because the unit (mithqal) is attached to both equally; no priority.
3
Apply the rule for 'a thousand of gold and silver'
If 'mithqal' is not mentioned, the thousand refers to the customary weight of each metal: gold by mithqal and silver by dirhams of weight seven (ten dirhams weigh seven mithqal).
So due: 1000 mithqal gold and 1000 dirhams (which equals 700 mithqal silver).
4
Generalize to other transactions
This rule applies to all contracts involving weighed, measured, counted, or length-measured items, such as dowry, wills, deposits, usurpation, leases, and khul' compensation.
When a quantity is expressed with multiple items, the phrase determines the apportionment.

Source Text

And if he buys a slave-girl with a thousand mithqal of gold and silver, due from each half. And if [he buys] with a thousand of gold and silver, due from gold mithqals, and from silver dirhams of weight seven.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school on how to interpret a price stated in combined gold and silver. The commentator first notes that specifying quality (good, bad, or medium) is necessary to avoid dispute, citing that Imam Muhammad did so in his works. The second comment explains that when the price is 'a thousand mithqal of gold and silver', it means half in gold and half in silver, but if it is 'a thousand of gold and silver', it refers to the customary weights of each metal. This principle applies to all transactions involving weighed, measured, counted, or length-measured items. The third comment discusses the weight 'seven' for dirhams, noting that it refers to the customary weight of seven mithqals per ten dirhams, and that in Egypt, the current practice may differ unless silver is specified.

Key Terms
مثقال (mithqal): A unit of weight for gold and silver; standard weight of 20 qirats.
درهم (dirham): A silver coin; in this context, weight of 14 qirats (seven-tenths of a mithqal).
زنة سبعة (zina sab'ah): Weight seven: ten dirhams weigh seven mithqals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of a price in mixed metals hinges on the wording: if 'mithqal' is explicitly attached to both metals, it is split equally; otherwise, each metal is paid according to its customary weight.

Liability for Spending Counterfeit Dirhams Ignorantly: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for spending counterfeit dirhams ignorantly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on liability for unknowingly spending counterfeit dirhams and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Ignorance of counterfeit dirhams divides the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the spender liable; Abu Yusuf excuses him.

Receiving counterfeit currency is a real-world problem. In classical Hanafi fiqh, a buyer who unknowingly receives counterfeit dirhams in exchange for good ones and then spends them faces a dispute: is he liable to replace them with good dirhams? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (who impose liability) and Abu Yusuf (who excuses the ignorant spender). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh proofs within the school.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and parties involved
Identify the precise legal scenario: a buyer receives counterfeit dirhams (zayf) instead of good ones, unaware, and then spends them or they are lost. The dispute is between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (on one side) and Abu Yusuf (on the other).
The passage clearly says the disagreement is when he is ignorant; if he knew, all agree he is liable.
2
Identify each position and its apparent basis
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: liable. Abu Yusuf: not liable. The excerpt does not fully state the proofs, but the basis is that Abu Hanifa views the spender as a guarantor (damin) once he takes possession, while Abu Yusuf considers him a trustee (amin) not liable without negligence.
The commentary hints that the spender is considered a mutabarri' (volunteer) in spending, which triggers liability for Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, the buyer must pay back the value of good dirhams. Under Abu Yusuf, he is excused. This difference matters in everyday transactions.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is typically that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf's view is also recognized.
4
Note the role of knowledge
The dispute is limited to ignorance. If the buyer knew the dirhams were counterfeit and spent them anyway, all three imams agree he is liable and the payment is valid (qada').
This is explicitly stated in the passage: 'because if he knew about it and spent it, it would be a payment by agreement.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قبض زيفاً بدل جيد جاهلاً به وأنفق أو نفق وزن السَّبْعَةِ قد سبق في كتاب الزكاة
[١] قوله : قد سبق ... الخ ؛ حيث ذكر نصاب الفضة ، وقال : وللفضة مئتا درهم، كل عشرة منها سبعة مثاقيل، اعلم أنَّ هذا الوزن يسمّى سبعة، وهو أن يكون الدرهم سبعة أجزاء من الأجزاء التي يكون المثقالُ عشرة منها : أي يكون الدرهم نصف مثقال وخمس مثقال.
فيكون عشرة دراهم بوزن سبعة مثاقيل، والمثقالُ عشرون قيراطاً، والدرهم أربعة عشر قيراطاً، والقيراط خمس شعيرات. انتهى. وقد ذكرتُ هذا البحث مبسوطاً في «حل الضروري لمختصر القدوري».
[٢] قوله : زيفاً ؛ قيَّد بالزيف لأنَّ الدراهم لو كانت ستوقةً أو نَبَهْرَجةً فأتلفها فإنّه يرد مثلها، ويرجع بالخيار اتفاقاً، وفي الواقعاتِ الحسامية» من «البيوع»: تكلموا في معرفة الزيوف والنبهرجة.
فقال أبو نصر الله: الزيوفُ دراهم مغشوشة، أمّا النَّبَهْرَجةُ التي تضرب في غيرِ دار السلطان والستُّوقة صُفْرٌ مموّه بالفضة، وقال الفقيه أبو جعفر له : الزيوفُ ما زيفه بيت المال، يقال في عرفنا غطريفي لا غير، والنبهرجة لا يقبلُهُ التجار، كذا في «البحر»(۳).
[٣] قوله : جاهلاً به ؛ هذا القيد إنّما أفاد بيان محل الاختلاف بين أبي حنيفة ومحمد وبين أبي يوسف ؛ لأنه لو عَلِمَ به وأنفقه كان قضاء اتفاقاً، كما صرحوا به ؛ لأنه
And if he receives counterfeit [dirhams] in place of good, unaware of it, and he spends them or they are lost... weight seven has preceded in the Book of Zakat. [Comment 1: '...etc.'] where he mentioned the nisab of silver and said: For silver two hundred dirhams, every ten of them seven mithqals. Know that this weight is called seven, meaning that the dirham is seven parts of ten parts of the mithqal: i.e., the dirham is half a mithqal plus one-fifth of a mithqal. Thus ten dirhams weigh seven mithqals, the mithqal being twenty qirats, the dirham fourteen qirats, and the qirat five barleycorns. End. I have discussed this extensively in 'Hil al-Daruriy li-Mukhtasar al-Quduri'. [Comment 2: 'His saying: counterfeit.'] It is restricted to counterfeit because if the dirhams were sa-tuqa or naharaja and he destroyed them, he would return their like and retain the option by agreement. In al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyya from Buyu', they discussed the definition of zi-yuf and naharaja. Abu Nasr Allah said: Al-ziyuf are debased dirhams. As for al-naharaja which are struck outside the sultan's mint, and al-satuqa are copper coated with silver. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: Al-ziyuf are what the treasury rejects; in our custom they are called ghatrifi only. And al-naharaja are not accepted by merchants. So in al-Bahr. [Comment 3: 'His saying: unaware of it.'] This restriction specifies the locus of disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other; because if he knew about it and spent it, it would be a [valid] payment by agreement, as they explicitly stated, because...
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a buyer receives counterfeit dirhams in exchange for good ones without knowing and then spends them or they are lost. The commentary first clarifies the definition of the weight 'seven' for dirhams, establishing the standard: 10 dirhams = 7 mithqals, each dirham is 14 qirats. It then defines different types of defective dirhams: ziyuf (debased), naharaja (illegally minted), satuqa (copper coated with silver). The key legal point is the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (who hold that the buyer is liable to replace with good dirhams because he is considered a guarantor) and Abu Yusuf (who holds that he is not liable because he was ignorant and spent in good faith). The commentary states that if he knew, then there is no dispute; he is liable. The passage cuts off but the reasoning would continue that because he is a volunteer (mutabarri') in spending, he is not compelled, etc. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is likely that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
زيف (zayf): Counterfeit or debased dirhams containing less silver.
نبهرجة (nābahraja): Dirhams struck outside the sultan's mint, not officially recognized.
ستوقة (sātūqa): Copper coins coated with silver.
اختلاف (ikhtilāf): Disagreement, here within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition do Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold a buyer liable for spending counterfeit dirhams?
A) If he knew and spent willingly
B) If he spent them ignorantly
C) If they were lost
D) In all cases

Key Takeaway
The spender's knowledge is decisive: if ignorant, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad impose liability; Abu Yusuf excuses. The mufta bihi is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Counterfeit Coins in Debt Payment
Intra-Hanafi dispute on payment with counterfeit coins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abu Yusuf on whether payment with counterfeit coins discharges a debt, and explain the reasoning and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Payment with counterfeit coins discharges the debt according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf requires return and reclamation of good coins.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of debt settlement when the debtor pays with counterfeit coins and the creditor unknowingly spends them. The key question is whether the creditor's right to quality (wasf) overrides the general rule that payment in kind discharges the obligation. We will explore the arguments of the three founding imams of the Hanafi school and identify the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The debtor owes good-quality silver coins. He pays with counterfeit (debased) coins. The creditor is unaware of the defect and spends the coins or they perish. The question is whether this payment is a valid discharge (qada') of the debt.
The scenario assumes the creditor did not know and did not consent to the counterfeit.
2
State the Two Positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the payment is valid and the debt is discharged (fa-huwa qada'). Abu Yusuf holds that the payment is invalid: the creditor must return a like amount of counterfeit coins and reclaim his good coins.
Both views are within the Hanafi school; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the Proofs
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that counterfeit coins are of the same genus (jins) as the owed good coins, and obligating the debtor to pay again for the sake of quality has no precedent in Shari'ah. Abu Yusuf argues that the creditor has a right to the specific description (wasf) of good coins, and since quality has no independent value separate from the genus, the only way to protect that right is to reverse the transaction.
The disagreement hinges on whether the creditor's right to quality can be enforced after payment and disposition.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the creditor has no further claim; the debt is settled. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the creditor can reclaim the good coins by returning the counterfeits, thus the debt is not discharged until good coins are paid.
Practical consequence: Abu Yusuf's view gives the creditor a remedy; the other view does not.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
Although the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the zahir al-riwayah, many later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-'Uyun, al-Durr al-Muntaqa) prefer Abu Yusuf's view for fatwa due to its protection of the creditor's right.
The operative ruling for practice is Abu Yusuf's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قبض زيفا بدل جيدٍ جاهلا به وأنفق أو نفق ... فهو قضاء، وعند أبي يوسف له يرد مثل زيفه، ويرجع بجيده : لأن حقه في الوصف مراعاً ولا قيمة له، فوجب المصير إلى ما ذكرنا ، قلنا: الزيف من جنس حقه، ووجوب الزيف عليه؛ ليأخذ الجيد إيجاب له عليه، ولم يعهد في الشرع مثله.
And if he received counterfeit coins instead of good ones, being unaware of it, and he spent them or they perished, then it is settlement (qada'). According to Abu Yusuf, he must return the like of his counterfeit and reclaim his good ones, because his right to the description is observed and it has no value, so it is necessary to resort to what we mentioned. We said: Counterfeit is of the same genus as his right, and obligating the counterfeit upon him in order to take the good would be imposing an obligation upon him for which there is no precedent in the Shari'ah.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the discharge of a debt when the debtor pays with counterfeit coins (zayf) instead of good quality coins, and the creditor unknowingly spends or loses them. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, this constitutes a valid settlement (qada'), and the creditor has no further claim. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that the creditor must return the like of the counterfeit coins and can demand good coins from the debtor. Abu Yusuf's reasoning is that the creditor's right to the quality (wasf) of the coin is preserved, and since quality has no independent monetary value when compared to its genus, the only way to enforce this right is to annul the transaction and revert. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that counterfeit coins are of the same genus as the debt (since they are both silver coins), and obligating the debtor to pay again for the sake of quality would be imposing a new obligation without precedent in Islamic law. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Yusuf, as stated in some later sources. The passage underscores that the key issue is whether the right to a specific description can override the general rule that payment in kind discharges the debt.

Key Terms
زيف (zayf): Counterfeit coins; in this context, silver coins that are debased or of lower quality.
قضاء (qada'): Settlement or discharge of a debt; here meaning the payment is valid and obligation extinguished.
وصف (wasf): Description or quality of the owed item; here refers to the quality (goodness) of the coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what must a creditor do if they unknowingly receive and spend counterfeit coins as payment for a debt of good coins?
A) He must accept the loss and the debt is discharged.
B) He must return a like amount of counterfeit coins to the debtor and reclaim his good coins.
C) He must forgive the debtor and take no further action.
D) He must report the debtor to the authorities.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, paying a debt with counterfeit coins is a valid discharge according to the majority of the school, but the operative fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's view requiring return and reclamation.

Payment of Debt with Inferior Dirhams (Zuyuf)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada with zuyuf dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions within the Hanafi school regarding qada with zuyuf and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A debt discharged with inferior coins remains valid if spent; but the mufta bihi protects quality by allowing return.

Imagine a debtor pays his creditor 10 good dirhams with 10 inferior dirhams (zuyuf). The creditor, unaware of the inferiority, spends them. Is the debt discharged? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This lesson unpacks the proofs of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf, and concludes with the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
We have a debtor who owes jayyid (good) dirhams but pays with zuyuf (inferior) dirhams. The creditor accepts them without knowledge and then spends them or they are destroyed.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
They hold that the debt is discharged (qada). The zuyuf are of the same genus (jins) as the debt, so payment is valid. The creditor's spending implies acceptance.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the creditor may return the like of the zuyuf (if they still exist) and demand good dirhams. He argues that the creditor's right includes the quality (jooda), which must be protected.
4
Analyze the proofs
For Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the zuyuf are of the same jins, so paying them fulfills the genus. Requiring the creditor to return them to get good dirhams would impose an unprecedented obligation on the creditor. For Abu Yusuf: quality is part of the right, yet has no independent value in this context, so the only remedy is to allow return of the specific inferior coins.
5
Determine the tarjih
The text states 'Wal fatwa ala qawlihi' – the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view. This is the mufta bihi. The practical consequence: if the creditor spends the zuyuf, he may still demand good dirhams according to the fatwa position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو قضاء، ، وعند أبي يوسف له يَرُدُّ مثل زيفه، ويرجع بجيده
أي هَلَكَ، فهو قضاء، وعند أبي يوسف) له يَرُدُّ مثل زيفه، ويرجعُ بجيده ) (۲) : لأنَّ (۲) حقَّهُ في الوصف مراعاً ولا قيمة له، فوجب المصير إلى ما ذكرنا ، قلنا: الزيف من جنس حقه، ووجوب الزَّيف عليه؛ ليأخذ الجيد إيجاب له عليه، ولم يُعْهَدْ فِي الشَّرْع مثله.
صار راضيا بتركِ حقهِ في الجودة.
[١]قوله : فهو قضاء ؛ لحقه فيكون مؤدّياً عليه من تلك الدراهم، ولا رجوع عليه بشيء عند الطرفين.
[٢] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ حق صاحب الدين في وصف الدِّينِ من حيث القدر، فلو كان المقبوض دون حقه قدراً لم يسقط حقه في المطالبة بقدر النقصان ، فكذلك إذا كان دون حقه وصفاً ، إلا أنه يتعدَّرُ ذلك ؛ لأنه لا قيمة للجودة عند المقابلة بجنسها، فيرد عين المقبوض إذا كان قائماً، ومثله إن كان مستهلكاً؛ لأنَّ مثل الشيء يحكى عنه ، كذا قرره العلامة السغناقي . [٣]قوله : قلنا ؛ حاصلُهُ : إِنَّ الزيف من جنس حق القابض، بدليل أنه لو تجوز به في الصرف والسلم جاز، ولو لم يكن من جنسيه لكان استبدالا ، وهو حرام، فلم يبق إلا الجودة ولا قيمة لها، فلا يمكن تداركها بإيجاب ،ضمانها، ووجوبُ الزَّيف عليه ؛ لأخذ الجيّد إيجاب له عليه ، فإنَّ المضمون عليه هو الأصل، وهو مستوفى، فإيجاب الضمان باعتباره إيجاب له عليه، ولم يعهد في الشرع نظيره.
(۱) والفتوى على قوله كما في «العيون»، وأقره صاحب الايضاح (ق۱۰۲/ب)، والشرنبلالية (۲) : (۱۹۹)، وقال صاحب الدر المنتقى ۲ : ۱۱۰) : وبه يفتى. (۲) حاصل المسألة : إذا كان له على آخر عشرة دراهم جياد فقضاه زيوفاً وهو لا يعلم فأنفقها أو هلكت فهو قضاء عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد له ، وقال أبو يوسف له يرد مثل زيوفه ويرجع بجياده ؛ لأن حقه في الوصف مرعي : كحقه في الأصل ولا يمكن رعايته بإيجاب ضمان الوصف، إذ لا قيمة له عند المقابلة بجنسه فوجب الرجوع إلى ما قلنا. ولهما : أنه من جنس حقه حتى لو تجوز به فيما لا يجوز الاستبدال جاز فيقع به الاستيفاء ولا يبقى حقه إلا في الجودة ولا يمكن تداركها بإيجاب ضمانها لما مر ولا بإيجاب ضمان الأصل ؛ لأنه إيجاب له عليه ولا نظير له. كذا في الكتب المشهورة. ينظر: «الدرر»(۲ : ۱۹۹).
So it is qada (discharge). And according to Abu Yusuf, he may return the like of his zuyuf (inferior dirhams) and take back his good dirhams. ... (The text continues with commentary and summary of the dispute.)
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the discharge of a debt when the debtor pays with inferior dirhams (zuyuf) and the creditor unknowingly spends them. The base statement says 'it is qada' meaning the debt is discharged. The commentator explains that according to Abu Yusuf, the creditor may return the like of the inferior dirhams he received (if he still has them) and demand his good dirhams back. The proof for Abu Yusuf is that the creditor's right includes the quality (jooda) of the dirhams, and since quality has no monetary value when compared with its own kind, the only way to protect that right is to allow him to return the inferior ones and demand the good ones. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue that the zuyuf are of the same genre as the creditor's right (since the debt is in dirhams, and zuyuf are also dirhams), and requiring the creditor to pay back the zuyuf in order to get good dirhams would be imposing an obligation on him, which has no precedent in the Sharia. Thus, if the creditor spends them ignorantly, the debt is discharged. The relied-upon fatwa is on the view of Abu Yusuf, as noted in the text.

Key Terms
زيف (zuyuf): Inferior or debased dirhams, of lower quality than the standard jayyid dirhams.
جيد (jayyid): Good-quality dirhams, the standard for the debt.
قضاء (qada): Discharge of a debt, fulfilment of the obligation.
وصف (wasf): Attribute or quality; in this context, the quality (jooda) of the dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi, if a debtor pays a debt with inferior dirhams (zuyuf) and the creditor unknowingly spends them, what is the ruling?
A) The debt is discharged and no further payment is required.
B) The creditor may return the like of the zuyuf and demand good dirhams.
C) The contract becomes void ab initio.
D) The debtor must pay a penalty.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for payment of debt with inferior dirhams is the view of Abu Yusuf, who protects the creditor's right to quality by allowing return of the inferior coins and demanding good ones.

Who Owns Game on Another's Land? The Hanafi Ruling and Its Rationale
Primary ruling with objection and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game found on another's land and explain the rationale distinguishing it from Shariah obligations.

Core Idea
Game belongs to the taker, not the landowner, and that is permissible because both harm and benefit are worldly.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi ruling: if a bird hatches or a deer breaks its leg in someone's land, the finder, not the landowner, owns it. The commentator addresses an objection that such a ruling imposes harm for benefit, similar to Islamic obligations. We will unpack the ruling, the objection, and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the opening statement: if a bird hatches/eggs or a deer breaks its leg on a land, it belongs to the taker. Note that eggs are considered like game (sayd).
This is the default rule when the land is not prepared.
2
Recognize the objection
An objector argues that this ruling imposes a small harm (taking from landowner) for a great benefit (to the taker), similar to Shariah obligations that impose worldly harm for otherworldly benefit.
The objection is based on an analogy (qiyas).
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The commentator rejects the analogy: in Shariah obligations, the benefit is otherworldly and cannot be abandoned; here both harm and benefit are worldly, and abandoning worldly benefit is permissible and even praiseworthy.
This is a clear distinction (farq) that invalidates the qiyas.
4
Extract the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that game (sayd) belongs to the one who takes it, regardless of who owns the land, unless the landowner prepared the land for hunting.
The default is for the taker; preparation shifts ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو فرخ أو باض طير في أرض، أو تكسر ظبي فيها، فهو للآخذ. ويرد عليه: أن مثل هذا في الشرع كثير، فإن جميع تكاليف الشرع من هذا القبيل؛ لأنها إيجاب ضرر قليل لأجل نفع كثير. ( ولو فرخ أو باض طير في أرض، أو تكسر ظبي فيها، فهو للآخذ): أي لا يكون لصاحب الأرض؛ لأن الصيد لمن أخذه، والمراد بتكسر الظبي انكسار رجله، وإنما قال: تكسر؛ لأنه لو كسرها أحد يكون له لا للآخذ، وفي بعض الروايات تكنس: أي دخل في الكناس: وهو مأواه. قوله: يرد عليه...الخ؛ ويمكن الجواب عنه بالمنع، وهو أنه ليس شيء من تكاليف الشرع من هذا القبيل، فإن الضرر فيها دنيوي، والنفع أخروي، ولا يجوز للعبد ترك النفع الأخروي؛ لأنه حق الله. بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإن الضرر والنفع فيه دنيويان، ويجوز للعبد ترك النفع الدنيوي، حتى لو تساهل به جاز، بل هو مما يعد من الصفات الحسنة، والفضائل المستحسنة، فقياس ما نحن فيه على تكاليف الشرع قياس مع الفارق فافهم. قوله: ولو فرخ؛ قال في «الصراح»: فرخ جوزه، فرخة مؤنث أفرخ أفراخ: فراخ ج أفراخ جوزه بیرون آدرون مرغ، يقال: أفرخ الطائر وفرخ. انتهى. قوله: لأن...الخ؛ يعني لأن كل واحد صيد، وإن لم يؤخذ بحيلة، فإن كونه مأخوذاً بغير حيلة لا يخرجه عن كونه صيداً، كصيد انكسر رجله في أرض أحد، والصيد يكون من أخذه، والبيض في حكم الصيد؛ لأنه أصل الصيد. ألا ترى أنه يجب الجزاء على المحرم بكسره، كما يجب بالاصطياد، وهذا إذا لم تكن أرضه مهيأة لذلك، وإن كانت مهيأة للاصطياد، فهو لصاحب الأرض؛ لأن الحكم لا يضاف إلى السبب الصالح إلا بالقصد، فمن حفر في أرضه بئراً ليسقط فيها، أو أعد مكاناً للفراخ، فلما أعدها لذلك لا يملكها الآخذ، بل صاحب الأرض يصير بذلك قابضاً. كصيد تعلق بشبكة نصبت للجفاف، أو دراهم أو دنانير أو سكر نثر فوقع على ثوب لم يعد له، ولم يكف. (كصيد تعلق بشبكة نصبت للجفاف، أو دراهم أو دنانير أو سكر نثر فوقع على ثوب لم يعد له، ولم يكف) حتى إن أعد الثوب لذلك، فهو لصاحب الثوب، وكذا إن لم يعد له، لكن لما وقع كفه صار بهذا الفعل له، حكماً، بخلاف من نصب شبكة للجفاف، فتعلق بها صيد فهو للآخذ؛ لأن صاحب الشبكة لم يعدها الآن للأخذ. قوله: وبخلاف ما إذا عسل النحل في أرضه؛ فإن العسل عد من زيادات الأرض، فيملكه صاحب الأرض تبعاً لأرضه؛ لأن العسل لا يحصل في مطلق المواضع، وبمطلق الأخذ به، بل بغذاء خاص في مكان خاص، فإذا عسل في أرضه علم أنه من نبات تلك الأرض، صرح العلامة السغناقي كما أن الشجر الثابت في أرضه فهو له، وكما أن التراب المجتمع في أرضه بجريان الماء على أرضه فهو له أيضاً.
And if a bird hatches or lays eggs in a land, or a deer breaks its leg therein, it is for the taker. And it is objected: that such is much in the Shariah, for all obligations of the Shariah are of this type, because they impose a small harm for a great benefit. ('And if a bird hatches or lays eggs in a land, or a deer breaks its leg therein, it is for the taker'): meaning it is not for the landowner, because game is for the one who takes it, and the meaning of 'deer breaks' is the breaking of its leg, and he only said 'breaks' because if someone broke it, it would be for him, not for the taker, and in some narrations 'takanasa' meaning it entered its lair, which is its shelter. He said: 'It is objected...' and it is possible to answer by denial, that nothing from the obligations of the Shariah is of this type, for the harm in them is worldly and the benefit is otherworldly, and the slave is not permitted to abandon otherworldly benefit because it is the right of Allah. Unlike what we are discussing, for the harm and benefit in it are worldly, and the slave is permitted to abandon worldly benefit, even if he is negligent about it, it is permissible, rather it is counted among good qualities and commendable virtues, so analogizing what we are discussing to the obligations of the Shariah is an analogy with a difference, so understand. He said: 'and if a bird hatches'; he said in al-Surah: farakh is a young bird, farakhah is feminine, afrakh farakh: farakh pl. afrakh, a young bird emerging from an egg, one says: afrakha al-ta'iru and farrakha. End. He said: 'because...' meaning because each one is game, even if not taken by a trick, for its being taken without a trick does not remove it from being game, like game whose leg is broken in someone's land, and game belongs to the one who takes it, and eggs are in the ruling of game because they are the origin of game. Do you not see that the penalty is obligatory on the muhrim for breaking it, as it is obligatory for hunting, and this is if his land is not prepared for that, but if it is prepared for hunting, then it is for the landowner, because the ruling is not attributed to a valid cause except with intent, so if someone digs a well in his land for something to fall into, or prepares a place for chicks, then when he prepares it for that, the taker does not own it, rather the landowner thereby becomes a possessor. Like game that gets caught in a net set for drying, or dirhams or dinars or sugar that is scattered and falls on a cloth not prepared for it, and he did not scoop it. ('Like game that gets caught in a net set for drying, or dirhams or dinars or sugar that is scattered and falls on a cloth not prepared for it, and he did not scoop it') until if the cloth is prepared for that, it is for the owner of the cloth, and likewise if it is not prepared for it, but when it fell he scooped it, then by this act it becomes his, by law, contrary to the one who set a net for drying, and game gets caught in it, it is for the taker, because the owner of the net did not now prepare it for catching. He said: 'And contrary to the case when bees produce honey in his land', for honey is counted among the increases of the land, so the landowner owns it by virtue of his land, because honey is not obtained in just any place and by just any taking, but by specific nutrition in a specific place, so if it produces honey in his land, it is known that it is from the vegetation of that land, as al-Allamah al-Sighnaqi stated, just as a tree established in his land is for him, and just as soil accumulated in his land by the flow of water on his land is also for him.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a collection of miscellaneous rulings (masa'il shatta) from a Hanafi fiqh commentary, specifically from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. The commentator expands on several cases concerning ownership of game, eggs, and scattered items that are found on another's land or property. The opening ruling states that if a bird hatches young or lays eggs in someone's land, or if a deer breaks its leg in that land, the bird, eggs, or deer belong to the person who takes them (al-akhidh), not to the landowner. The reason is that these are considered game (sayd), and game belongs to the hunter, regardless of where it is found. The commentator then addresses an objection: a critic might argue that this ruling imposes a harm on the landowner (who loses what is on his land) for the benefit of the taker, similar to how Islamic obligations impose small worldly harm for great otherworldly benefit. The commentator refutes this by noting that in Islamic obligations, the benefit is otherworldly and abandoning it is sinful, whereas here both harm and benefit are worldly, and neglecting worldly benefit is permissible and even praiseworthy. Thus the analogy fails. The commentator also clarifies that if the landowner had prepared his land for hunting (e.g., by digging a pit or setting up a trap), then the game belongs to him, not the taker, because his intent and preparation make him the constructive possessor. The passage then extends the ruling to similar cases: if a net is set for drying (jafaf) rather than hunting, and game gets caught in it, the taker gets it. If money or sugar is scattered and falls on a cloth not prepared to catch it, the finder owns it, unless he actively scoops it up, in which case he owns it by his act. However, if the cloth was prepared to catch such items, it belongs to the cloth owner. Finally, the case of bees producing honey in one's land is distinguished: honey is considered an increase of the land (ziyadat al-ard), so it belongs to the landowner, just like trees or soil accumulated on his land. This is because honey depends on the specific vegetation of that land. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi principle that ownership of found items depends on whether the property owner had intent and preparation to acquire them.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game; wild animals that may be hunted, including their eggs.
تكسر (takassur): The breaking of a bone, specifically of a deer's leg, making it easy to capture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a deer with a broken leg is found on someone's land, who owns it?
A) The landowner.
B) The person who takes it (al-akhidh).
C) Both share ownership.
D) It is ownerless until claimed by the state.

Key Takeaway
Game found on another's land belongs to the taker, not the landowner, based on the nature of game and the distinction between worldly and otherworldly benefits.

Intent, Preparation, and Increases of Land: How Ownership Shifts
Expansion on conditions that shift ownership and distinction of land increases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on ownership when a landowner prepares his land, and distinguish game from increases of the land (e.g., honey).

Core Idea
Preparation (tahiyya) transfers ownership of game to the preparer; honey is owned by the landowner as an increase of land, not as game.

This lesson extends the previous ruling by showing how the landowner's intent and preparation change the outcome. If the landowner prepares his land for hunting, he becomes the constructive possessor and owns the game. Similarly, honey produced by bees is considered an increase of the land (ziyadat al-ard) and belongs to the landowner, contrasting with game. The lesson also covers analogous cases of nets and cloths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for landowner ownership
Read: 'if his land is prepared for hunting, then it is for the landowner.' Recognize that preparation (tahiyya) is a condition that shifts ownership from taker to landowner.
Preparation must be intentional (qasd).
2
Understand the principle behind preparation
The commentator states: 'the ruling is not attributed to a valid cause except with intent.' Digging a well or preparing a place for chicks shows intent, making the landowner the constructive possessor.
This is based on istihsan: intent overrides default rule.
3
Apply the principle to analogous cases
Examine the net-for-drying case: if a net is set for drying, and game gets caught, the taker owns it. But if the net was set for hunting, the net owner owns it. Similarly, a cloth prepared to catch scattered items makes the cloth owner the owner.
Contrast: active scooping (kaf) also creates ownership.
4
Distinguish honey from game
Honey is not game but an increase of the land (ziyadat al-ard). It requires specific vegetation from that land, so it belongs to the landowner by virtue of land ownership, not by taking.
Like trees or soil accumulation, honey is an inseparable part of the land's produce.

Commentary

Key Terms
زيادات الأرض (ziyadat al-ard): Increases of the land, such as honey, trees, or soil, that naturally arise from the land.
مهيأ (muhayya): Prepared; used to describe land or objects intentionally set up for acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a bee colony produces honey in a person's land without any preparation, who owns the honey?
A) The first person who takes it.
B) The landowner, because honey is an increase of the land.
C) It is ownerless, so anyone can take it.
D) It belongs to the state.

Key Takeaway

Ownership by Unintended Capture: Fish and Coins
Ruling on ownership of fish and coins in unintended capture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of a fish caught in a net set for drying and of coins falling on a cloth, and identify the role of intention and action in acquiring ownership.

Core Idea
Intent plus act creates ownership; accidental capture without intent does not transfer ownership.

The passage addresses a scenario where a fish gets caught in a net meant for drying laundry, not for fishing. The commentator clarifies that ownership goes to the person who takes the fish, not the net owner, because the net was not set with the intent to catch. Similarly, if coins or sugar fall on a cloth, the cloth owner only acquires them if he intentionally catches them. This lesson unpacks the principle that acquisition requires deliberate action and intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputing parties
Who are the potential owners? The net owner and the taker (for fish); the cloth owner and the finder (for coins).
2
Determine the intent of the current possessor
Was the net set for catching? No, it was for drying. Was the cloth prepared for catching? Initially not, but later the cloth owner catches it.
Intent is key: if the cloth was prepared, ownership still requires an act of catching.
3
Apply the principle of ownership by intentional acquisition
Hanafi fiqh: ownership of mubah (ownerless) property is established by intent (niyyah) and physical possession (qabd). Here, the taker's action fulfills both.
4
Contrast with the case where the net was set for catching
If the net was set for catching, the net owner would own the fish because he prepared it with intent. This passage's scenario is the opposite.
The passage uses 'بخلاف' to contrast with a previous ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كصيد تعلّق بشبكة نصبت للجفاف ، أو دراهم أو دنانير أو سكر نثر فوقع على ثوب لم يعد له، ولم يكف حتى إن أُعِدَّ الثَّوب لذلك، فهو لصاحب الثَّوْب ، وكذا إن لم يُعَدَّ له ، لكن لما وَقَعَ كَفَّهُ صَارَ بهذا الفعل له. حكماً، بخلافِ من نصب شبكة للجفاف، فتعلق بها صيد فهو للآخذ ؛ لأنَّ صاحبَ الشبكة لم يعدها الآن للأخذ.
11 ] قوله : وبخلاف ما إذا عسل النحل في أرضه ؛ فإنَّ العسل عد من زيادات الأرض، فيملكه صاحب الأرض تبعاً لأرضه ؛ لأنَّ العسل لا يحصل في مطلق المواضع، وبمطلق الأخذ به، بل بغذاء خاص في مكان خاص
فإذا عسل في أرضهِ علم أنه من نبات ذلك الأرض، صرح العلامة السغناقي كما أنَّ الشجر الثابت في أرضه فهو له، وكما أنَّ التراب المجتمع في أرضه بجريان الماء على أرضه فهو له أيضاً.
Like a fish that gets caught in a net set for drying, or dirhams or dinars or sugar that are scattered and fall on a cloth not prepared for it, and he does not catch it — even if the cloth was prepared for that, it belongs to the owner of the cloth; likewise, if it was not prepared for it, but when it fell he caught it, it becomes his by this act, legally. Contrary to someone who sets a net for drying, and a fish gets caught in it — it belongs to the taker, because the owner of the net did not now prepare it for catching. 11] His saying: And contrary to when bees produce honey in his land — indeed, honey is considered among the increases of the land, so the owner of the land owns it as a consequence of his land, because honey does not occur in all places nor by mere taking, but by specific nourishment in a specific place. So when bees produce honey in his land, it is known that it is from the vegetation of that land. Al-Sighnaqi declared that just as the trees fixed in his land are his, and just as the soil that accumulates in his land by the flow of water on his land is also his.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a sharh (expansion) on a matn concerning rulings of ownership by unintended acquisition versus natural increase. The commentator explains three scenarios. First, if a fish gets caught in a net set for drying (not for catching), the fish belongs to the person who takes it, not the net owner, because the net was not intended for catching. Second, if coins or sugar are scattered and fall on a cloth, the cloth owner acquires them only if he catches them (intentional act), otherwise they remain for the finder. Third, if bees produce honey in one's land, the landowner owns it as a natural increase, by analogy to trees and soil accumulation. The reasoning (talil) is based on intent and natural attachment: the honey is a product of the land's vegetation, so it follows the land.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game or fish that is caught; in this context, a fish caught in a net.
شبكة (shabaka): A net; here used for either drying or catching fish.
جفاف (jafaf): Drying; the net is set for drying laundry, not for catching.
نثر (nathr): Scattered; coins or sugar that are thrown or fall.
ثوب (thawb): Cloth or garment; here used as a surface for falling coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone sets a net for drying clothes and a fish gets caught in it, who owns the fish?
A) The owner of the net, because the net is on his property.
B) The person who takes the fish (the taker), because the net was not set for catching.
C) The owner of the net, because the net is an instrument of acquisition.
D) No one, the fish is ownerless.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership of ownerless property acquired through a trap or receptacle requires that the trap be set with the intent to acquire; otherwise, the first taker owns it.

Ownership of Natural Increase: Honey from Bees
Ruling on ownership of honey as natural increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that honey from bees on one's land belongs to the landowner and explain the analogy to other natural increases like trees and soil.

Core Idea
Natural increases of the land follow the land in ownership.

The passage draws a contrast: unlike the fish in a net, honey produced by bees on one's land is owned by the landowner as a natural increase. The commentator justifies this by analogizing to trees and soil accumulation. This lesson explores the principle that anything that grows or forms naturally from the land is an accessory (tabi') to the land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and its source
Honey is produced by bees that feed on the vegetation of a specific land.
2
Examine the legal principle: increases follow the principal (al-ziyadah tabi'ah lil-asl)
Hanafi fiqh treats natural increase (like fruit, milk, honey) as belonging to the owner of the original asset (land, animal).
3
Apply the analogy provided by the commentator
The commentator compares honey to trees fixed in the land and soil accumulated by water flow — both are owned by the landowner.
These are considered 'increases' (ziyadat) that are inseparable from the land.
4
Note the distinguishing factor from the earlier fish scenario
Fish are not a natural increase of the land; they are independent creatures that wander. Honey is directly linked to the land's vegetation.
Thus, intent of the landowner is irrelevant for honey; ownership is automatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a sharh (expansion) on a matn concerning rulings of ownership by unintended acquisition versus natural increase. The commentator explains three scenarios. First, if a fish gets caught in a net set for drying (not for catching), the fish belongs to the person who takes it, not the net owner, because the net was not intended for catching. Second, if coins or sugar are scattered and fall on a cloth, the cloth owner acquires them only if he catches them (intentional act), otherwise they remain for the finder. Third, if bees produce honey in one's land, the landowner owns it as a natural increase, by analogy to trees and soil accumulation. The reasoning (talil) is based on intent and natural attachment: the honey is a product of the land's vegetation, so it follows the land.

Key Terms
عسل (asal): Honey; considered a natural increase (ziyadah) of the land.
نحل (nahl): Bees; their honey production is linked to the land's vegetation.
زيادة (ziyadah): Increase or produce; here, what naturally grows from the land.
شجر (shajar): Trees; used as an analogy for natural increases.
تراب (turab): Soil; accumulation of soil by water flow is also owned by the landowner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does honey from bees on one's land belong to the landowner?
A) Because the landowner owns the bees.
B) Because honey is a natural increase of the land's vegetation.
C) Because the landowner takes active possession of the honey.
D) Because the bees are wild and not owned by anyone.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, natural increases directly arising from the land, such as honey from wild bees, are owned by the landowner as accessories to the land.

Definition and Conditions of Sarf (Currency Exchange)
Defining sarf and explaining its placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of sarf as sale of thaman for thaman and identify the condition of hand-to-hand exchange.

Core Idea
Sarf is the sale of currency for currency, requiring immediate hand-to-hand exchange.

Sarf, or currency exchange, is a specific type of sale with unique conditions. This passage defines it and explains why it is placed late in fiqh texts.

Guided Path
1
Define sarf
Identify the exact legal definition from the text: sale of thaman for thaman, same or different genus.
2
Understand 'thaman'
Al-Farra explains thaman as a debt in the dhimmah. Appreciate that dirhams and dinars are thaman because they are owed as debts.
3
Identify the core condition
The text emphasizes hand-to-hand exchange in both countervalues before separation. This is the distinguishing condition of sarf.
4
Note the placement reason
Sarf is placed late because it has many conditions and deals with price, which is secondary to the main sold item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف
هو بيعُ الثَّمَن بالثَّمن جنساً بجنس أو بغير جنس
كتاب الصرف
[١]
(هو بيعُ الثَّمَنِ بالثَّمن" جنساً بجنس أو بغير جنس) : كبيع الذهب بالذهب ، وبيع الفضة بالفضة، وبيع الذهب بالفضة.
[١] قوله : كتاب الصرف ؛ أخره لقلة وجودِهِ بكثرة قيوده، ولأنه عقد على الثمن، والثمنُ في الجملة تبع لما هو المقصود من البيع، وهو المبيع، وأحسن وجوه تأخيره ما سبق في عنوان باب السلم، وبعضُهم عنونَهُ بالكتاب، والباب] أنسب ؛ لأنَّ الصرف من أنواع البيع كالسلّم، فالأولى في بعض نسخ الكتاب من قوله : باب الصرف. قال في «المصباح المنير) : صَرَفَه عن وجهه صرفاً : من باب ضرب، وصرفتُ الأجير والصبي خليت سبيله، وصرفتُ المال : أنفقته، وصرفت الدراهم : بعتها،
واسم الفاعل من هذا : صيرفي وصيروف، وصراف للمبالغة. قال ابن فارس (۲) : الصرف : فضلُ الدرهم في الجودة على الدرهم، ومنه اشتقاق الصيرفي، وصرفت الكلام زينته وصرَّفتُه بالتثقيل مبالغة، واسم الفاعل : مُصَرِّف، و به سمّي. والصرف التوبة في قوله : «لا يقبلُ الله منه يوم القيامة) صرفاً ولا
عَدْلاً (۳)، والعدل : الفدية والصريف الصوت، ومنه : صريف الأقلام. انتهى.
:
وقيل : الصرف في الحديث بمعنى النافلة ، والعَدْلُ بمعنى الفريضة، ومعناه الشرعي ما أفاده المصنف له بقوله : هو بيع ... الخ ؛ ومعنى الفضلُ والنفل متحقق في معناه الشرعي؛ لاشتراط التقابض في بدليه، فهو زيادة عـلـى مـا يشترط في غيره ،
وللحاجة إلى اعتبارِ النقل في بدليهِ من يد إلى يد قبل الافتراق.
[٢]قوله : بيع الثمن بالثمن ؛ قال الفَرّاء : الثمنُ عند العرب : ما يكون ديناً في الذمة، والدراهم والدنانير لا تستحق بالعقد إلا ديناً في الذمة، والعروض لا يستحق
بالعقد إلا عيناً، فكانت مبيعة بكل حال.
Book of Sarf. It is the sale of thaman for thaman, same genus or different genus. Book of Sarf. [1] (It is the sale of thaman for thaman, same genus or different genus): like selling gold for gold, selling silver for silver, and selling gold for silver. [1] His statement: Book of Sarf; he delayed it because of the rarity of its occurrence due to its many conditions, and because it is a contract on the price, and the price is generally secondary to what is intended from the sale, which is the sold item. And the best aspect of its delay is what preceded in the title of the chapter of Salam. Some have titled it as a book, but 'chapter' is more appropriate because Sarf is a type of sale like Salam. So in some copies of the book it is 'Chapter of Sarf'. He said in 'Al-Misbah al-Munir': 'Sarf' meaning to turn away, like 'I turned the worker or boy free', 'I spent money', 'I sold dirhams'. The active participle from this is sayrafi and sayruf, and sarraf for intensity. Ibn Faris said: Al-Sarf means the superiority of a dirham in quality over another dirham, and from this is derived sayrafi. Also, 'I adorned speech' with tathqil for intensification, and the active participle is musarrif, and by it he is named. And al-Sarf means repentance in his statement: 'Allah will not accept from him on the Day of Resurrection any Sarf or Adl.' Al-Adl means ransom, and al-Sarif means sound, like the creaking of pens. End. And it is said: al-Sarf in the hadith means supererogatory, and al-Adl means obligatory. Its legal meaning is what the author expressed by his saying: 'It is the sale...' etc. And the meaning of superiority and supererogation is realized in its legal meaning because of the condition of hand-to-hand exchange in its two countervalues, so it is an addition over what is required in other sales, and due to the need to consider transfer of the countervalues from hand to hand before separation. [2] His statement: 'Sale of thaman for thaman'; al-Farra said: Thaman according to the Arabs is what is a debt in the dhimmah, and dirhams and dinars are not owed by contract except as a debt in the dhimmah, while commodities are not owed by contract except as a specific item, so they are sold in any case.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-Sarf (Book of Currency Exchange) from the commentary. It begins with the author's definition of sarf: it is the sale of thaman (price/currency) for thaman, either of the same genus (e.g., gold for gold) or different genus (e.g., gold for silver). The commentary then explains why this book is placed late in the text: firstly, because sarf has many conditions which make it rare; secondly, because it is a contract on the price itself, whereas in most sales the price is secondary to the commodity. The commentator notes that some versions title this as 'Chapter of Sarf' which is more appropriate since it is a type of sale. He then provides linguistic analysis of the word 'sarf', citing Al-Misbah al-Munir and Ibn Faris, covering meanings such as 'to turn away', 'to spend', 'to sell currency', and relating it to the superiority of one dirham over another. The commentary also addresses a hadith usage where 'sarf' means repentance and 'adl' means ransom. Finally, the passage discusses the legal definition: the requirement of hand-to-hand exchange in both countervalues is a distinguishing feature. The second commentary section quotes al-Farra explaining that 'thaman' in Arabic refers to what is a debt in the dhimmah (obligation), and since dirhams and dinars are owed as debts, they are always sold as such, whereas commodities are sold as specific items.

Key Terms
الصرف (as-sarf): Currency exchange: sale of one type of money for another, either same or different genus, with immediate hand-to-hand exchange.
ثمن (thaman): Price or currency; according to al-Farra, what is a debt in the dhimmah (obligation) – dirhams and dinars are typically owed as debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of sarf according to the text?
A) The sale of a commodity for currency.
B) The sale of thaman for thaman, same or different genus.
C) The exchange of gold for silver only.
D) The loan of money with interest.

Key Takeaway
Sarf is sale of currency for currency, requiring immediate hand-to-hand exchange to avoid riba.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Definition of Sarf and Its Essential Condition
Defines Sarf and states the condition of mutual possession before separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Shar'i definition of Sarf and apply the condition of hand-to-hand exchange to determine the validity of a currency exchange transaction.

Core Idea
Sarf requires immediate hand-to-hand exchange of both countervalues; without it, the contract is invalid.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah opens the Book of Sarf, a specialized chapter on currency exchange in Hanafi fiqh. It first defines Sarf as a sale of a price (thaman) for a price, whether of the same genus (e.g., gold for gold) or different genus (e.g., gold for silver). The core legal rule is then laid out: mutual physical possession (taqabud) of both countervalues must occur before the contracting parties separate physically (iftiraq). This condition distinguishes Sarf from ordinary sales and is proven by explicit hadith and scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Sarf is a contract for exchanging gold, silver, or currency that serves as thaman. The ruling: it is valid (sahih) only if the condition of hand-to-hand exchange is fulfilled; otherwise it is corrupt (fasid).
The hukm level is not fard or wajib but concerns validity.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith: one from Umar (ra) in Muwatta Malik and another from Ubadah ibn al-Samit (ra) in Muslim and Ahmad. Both explicitly require hand-to-hand exchange (yadan biyad) and equality when exchanging the same genus.
The proof is from sunnah ahad, which is zanni in transmission but qat'i in indication.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative phrase "la tabi'u... illa yadan biyad" (do not sell except hand-to-hand) together with the condition "haat wa haat" (take and give) indicate that possession before separation is obligatory for validity.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr (command) indicates obligation (wujub), here applied to the condition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'i scholars allow deferment in non-like exchanges (e.g., gold for silver) as long as delivery is agreed. The Hanafi school, as emphasized here, requires hand-to-hand in all Sarf contracts regardless of genus.
The difference arises from how each school interprets the hadith and the analogical reasoning.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi, supported by the explicit wording of the hadith and the consensus (ijma') cited by the author. The ruling applies to all currency exchanges today, including modern fiat money.
The authority_weight is zahir al-riwayah; later Hanafi fatwa continues this view.

والثمن إذا أطلق يراد به الدراهم والدنانير، فإن كان كلا البدلين في البيع دنانير أو دراهم، فهو بيع الجنس بالجنس ، وإن كان أحدهما دنانير والآخر دراهم، فبيع الجنس
بغير الجنس. ومن حكم الثمن أن لا يشترط وجوده في ملكِ العاقد عند العقد، ولا يبطل العقد بفوات تسليمه، ويصح الاستبدال به، وحكم المبيع بخلافه، والمراد بالثمن هاهنا ما خلق للثمنية، ومنه المصوغ ، فإنَّ بيعه صرف، سواء كان لمصوغ مثله أو بالنقد، ولكنه بسبب ما اتصل به من الصيغة لم يبق ثمناً صريحاً ؛ ولهذا يتعيَّن في العقد كما صرحوا به. [١) قوله : وشرط فيه ؛ أي في الصرف سواء كان بالجنس أو بغيره التقابض : أي قبض العوضين قبل الافتراق بالأبدان ؛ لانعقاد الإجماع عليه، ولما روى الإمام مالك ه في «الموطأ» : عن عمر له أنه قال : لا تبيعوا الذهب بالذهب، إلا مثلاً بمثل، ولا تبيعوا الورق بالذهب أحدهما غائب والآخر ناجز وإن استنظرك أن يلج بيته ، فلا تنظره إلا يدا بيد ، هات وهات، إني أخشى عليكم الربا). ولما روى عبادة بن الصامت له أنه قال : «الذهب بالذهب، والفضة بالفضة»، إلى أن قال: «مثلاً بمثل، سواء بسواء، يداً بيد، فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم إذا كان يداً بيد (۳) رواه مسلم، وأحمد، وغيرهما.
ولأنه لا بُدَّ من قبض أحدهما قبل الافتراق ؛ لئلا يكون افتراقاً عن دين بدين، فلا بُدَّ من قـ من قبض الآخر؛ لعدم الأولوية تحقيقاً للمساواة بينهما ؛ لأنَّ النقد خيرٌ من
The Book of Sarf
It is the sale of a price for a price, of the same genus or of different genus.
The Book of Sarf
[1] (It is the sale of a price for a price, of the same genus or of different genus): like selling gold for gold, selling silver for silver, and selling gold for silver.
[1] His statement: The Book of Sarf; it was delayed due to the scarcity of its occurrence because of many restrictions, and because it is a contract upon the price, and the price is generally subordinate to what is intended in the sale, which is the sold item. The best reason for its delay is what preceded in the title of the chapter of Salam. Some titled it as a Book, but 'Chapter' is more appropriate because Sarf is a type of sale like Salam. In some copies of the book, it says 'Chapter of Sarf'. He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'Sarfahu an wajhihi sarfan: from the form of daraba, and I dismissed the employee and the boy—I set them free; I spent money; I sold dirhams.' The active participle from this is sayrafi and sayruf, and sarraf for exaggeration. Ibn Faris said: al-Sarf: the superiority of the dirham in quality over the dirham, from which sayrafi is derived. I adorned speech and I embellished it (sarraftuhu) with tashdid for exaggeration, and the active participle is musarrif, and it is named by it. And al-Sarf means repentance in His statement: 'Allah will not accept from him on the Day of Resurrection any sarf nor adl' (3), and al-adl is ransom, and al-sarif is sound, from which is sarif al-aqlam (creaking of pens). End.
And it is said: al-Sarf in the hadith means supererogatory deed, and al-adl means obligatory deed. Its legal meaning is what the author expressed by his saying: 'It is the sale...' etc. The meaning of superiority and supererogation is realized in its legal meaning due to the condition of mutual possession of its two countervalues; it is an increase over what is required in other contracts, and due to the need to consider transfer of its two countervalues from hand to hand before separation.
[2] His statement: 'sale of a price for a price'; al-Farra' said: 'ath-Thaman among the Arabs is what is a debt in the liability (dhimma). Dirhams and dinars are not acquired by contract except as a debt in the liability, while commodities are not acquired by contract except as an 'ayn (specific item). So they are sold in any case.' And the condition in it is mutual possession before separation.
And the condition in it is mutual possession before separation.
And what is measured and weighed is called 'ayn by contract sometimes, and debt at other times. If it is specified in the contract, it is the sold item; if it is not specified and is accompanied by the letter ba', and it is opposite the sold item, it is the price. Another type of price is originally a commodity: like fulus (copper coins), if they are current then they are a price, and if they are obsolete then they are a commodity.
And when ath-thaman is used absolutely, it refers to dirhams and dinars. If both countervalues in the sale are dinars or dirhams, it is a sale of the same genus; if one is dinars and the other dirhams, then it is a sale of different genus. Among the rulings of ath-thaman is that its existence in the ownership of the contracting party at the time of contract is not required, nor does the contract become invalid due to failure of delivery, and it is permissible to substitute it. The ruling of the sold item is contrary to this. What is meant by ath-thaman here is what is created for being a price (thimaniyya), which includes manufactured items (masugh). For its sale is Sarf, whether it is for a similar manufactured item or for cash, but due to the form (sigha) attached to it, it no longer remains a pure price; hence it is specified in the contract as they have stated.
[1] His statement: 'And the condition in it'; that is, in Sarf, whether of the same genus or different, is mutual possession (taqabud): i.e., taking possession of the two countervalues before separation by bodies (abdān); due to consensus upon it, and because of what Imam Malik narrated in al-Muwatta' from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'Do not sell gold for gold except like for like, and do not sell silver for gold with one absent and the other present, and if he asks you to wait until he enters his house, do not wait except hand-to-hand, take and give; indeed I fear riba for you.' And because of what Ubadah ibn al-Samit narrated (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver...' until he said: 'like for like, equal for equal, hand-to-hand; and if these categories differ, then sell as you wish provided it is hand-to-hand (3) narrated by Muslim, Ahmad, and others.
And because it is necessary to take possession of one before separation to avoid separation on a debt for a debt, so it is necessary to take possession of the other due to lack of priority, achieving equality between them, because cash is better than...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Sarf from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah. It first defines Sarf as a sale of a price for a price, whether of the same genus (e.g., gold for gold) or different genus (e.g., gold for silver). The author then explains why this chapter is placed late in the book: because Sarf contracts are rare due to many restrictions, and because it is a contract on the price (thaman), which is subordinate to the sold item (mabi'). The commentary then provides linguistic derivations of the word 'sarf' and notes that the legal meaning adds the condition of mutual possession (taqabud) before separation, which distinguishes it from ordinary sales. Next, the text discusses the term 'thaman': it refers primarily to gold and silver dinars and dirhams, but sometimes includes fulus (copper coins) when they are current. The condition of Sarf is then emphasized: mutual possession of both countervalues before the parties separate physically. This is proven by the consensus of scholars and by multiple hadiths, including the narration of Umar ibn al-Khattab and the more explicit hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit, which states that gold and silver must be exchanged hand-to-hand, like for like. The rational argument is also given: if one possession occurs before separation but not the other, it would constitute a sale of debt for debt, which is prohibited. Therefore, both must be possessed to ensure equality. The passage thus establishes the core rule of Sarf: immediate exchange of both countervalues is obligatory.

Key Terms
الصرف (al-sarf): The exchange of gold and silver (or currency serving as both) as defined by the condition of hand-to-hand delivery.
الثمن (al-thaman): The price; in Sarf it refers to gold and silver dinars and dirhams, or currency that serves as a medium of exchange.
التقابض (al-taqabud): Mutual physical possession of the two countervalues before the contracting parties separate.
الافتراق (al-iftiraq): Separation of the contracting parties by their bodies after the contract; before taqabud, such separation invalidates the Sarf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the essential condition for a valid Sarf contract according to this passage?
A) The exchange must be of equal weight regardless of timing.
B) Both countervalues must be possessed hand-to-hand before the parties separate.
C) The contract must be written and witnessed.
D) Only gold can be exchanged for gold, not silver.

Key Takeaway
In Sarf, hand-to-hand exchange of both countervalues before separation is an indispensable condition; violation renders the contract corrupt and exposes it to riba.

Core Ruling of Sarf: Validity with Excess and Taqabud
Defines sarf and outlines its core rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling gold for silver with excess and apply the condition of hand-to-hand exchange (taqabud) to determine validity.

Core Idea
In sarf, gold and silver can be exchanged with excess, but only if both parties receive hand-to-hand before separating.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah establishes the foundational ruling of currency exchange (sarf). It affirms that selling gold for silver is valid even with an excess in weight or estimated quantity, provided the parties physically exchange the items in the same session before separation. The text also clarifies that 'separation' means physical departure of bodies, not mere standing or walking away.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Sarf: sale of gold for silver). What is the ruling? (Valid – sahih).
The text opens with 'Wa ṣaḥa bayʻ al-dhahab bi al-fiḍḍah bi faḍl wa jazāf' – 'And valid is the sale of gold for silver with excess and estimated measure.'
2
Locate the condition for validity
The key condition is taqabud (hand-to-hand exchange) in the same session before separation. The text states: 'al-taqābuḍ sharṭ li ṣiḥḥat al-ʻaqd' or 'sharṭ li baqāʻ al-ʻaqd ʻalā al-ṣiḥḥah'.
There are two views: condition for the original validity vs. for continuance. The latter is preferred.
3
Understand the meaning of separation (iftirāq)
The text explains that iftirāq in sarf means physical departure of bodies, not merely standing or leaving the place. Several examples are given: walking together, sleeping, fainting, or sitting long do not break the session.
Imam Muhammad differentiates: standing or one standing is separation; sleeping while seated is not; prolonged sitting is separation, short sitting is not.
4
Apply to a scenario
If two parties exchange gold and silver by estimated measure (jazaaf) and separate before actual weighing, the sale becomes invalid because the condition of hand-to-hand delivery was not fulfilled before separation.
The text emphasizes that if they separate before taqabud, the contract is void.

لا بيع الجنس بالجنس إلا مساوياً، وإن اختلفا جودة وصياغة، ولا يصح التصرف في ثمن الصرف قبل قبضه ، فلو شَرَى به ثوباً ، فسدَ شراءُ الثَّوب لا بيع الجنس بالجنس إلا مساوياً، وإن اختلفا جودة وصياغة)، وإنما ذكر الفضل والجزافَ ولم يَذكُرُ التَّساوي ؛ لأنَّه لا شُبهة في جواز التّساوي، بل الشبهة في الفضل والجزاف فذكرهما.
(ولا يصح التَّصرُّفُ في ثَمَنِ الصَّرفِ قبل قبضه ، فلو شَرَى به ثوباً ، فسدَ شراء الثَّوب) : أي لو اشترى بثمن الصرف قبل قبضه ثوباً فسدَ شراءُ الثَّوب". ولو باع الفضة أو الذهب بجنسه مجازفة ، ثمَّ علما تساويهما قبل الافتراق صح، وبعده لا يصح، وقال زفر : يصح؛ لأنَّ التساوي حق الشرع، وقد وجد حالة العقد، قلنا : التسوية شرطٌ واجب علينا، فيجب تحصيله بفعلنا. أما وجوده في علم الله الله لا يصلح شرطاً للجواز ؛ لأنَّ الأحكام تبنى على فعل العبادِ تحقيقاً لمعنى الابتلاء ، كذا في «التبيين») ، وغيره من الكتب المعتبرة. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن لا يفسد ؛ لأنَّ الدراهم لا تتعين في العقـودِ عيناً كانت أو ديناً ، فانصرف العقد إلى مطلق الدَّراهم وهو المنقول عن زفر له. قلنا : الثمن في الصرف مبيع ، إذ البيع لا بدَّ له من المبيع، ولا شيء سوى
Book of Sarf: Its definition and rulings. And the sale of gold for silver with excess and estimated measure is valid. ... And the sale of a genus for the same genus is only valid if equal, even if they differ in quality and workmanship. And it is not valid to dispose of the price in a sarf contract before receiving it; so if one buys a garment with it, the purchase of the garment is invalid. ...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of currency exchange (sarf) from the Hanafi tradition, commenting on the matn of Al-Wiqayah. It begins by affirming the validity of selling gold for silver with excess (fadl) and estimated quantity (jazaaf) provided hand-to-hand exchange (taqabud) occurs in the same session before separation. The commentary explains that the requirement of hand-to-hand exchange is a condition for the contract's validity, and the ruling applies whether the items are specified (like jewelry or bullion) or unspecified (like coins). The preferred view is that taqabud is a condition for the contract's continuance in validity. The passage then addresses the meaning of separation (iftiraq): it is physical separation of bodies, not merely standing or walking away. Next, it states that selling a genus for itself (e.g., gold for gold, silver for silver) is only valid if equal in weight, even if quality differs. The commentator clarifies that the matn mentioned excess and estimated measure because equality is indisputably permissible. Finally, the text prohibits disposing of the price in a sarf contract before receiving it; for example, buying a garment with that price invalidates the garment purchase. A dispute with Zafar is recorded: if one sells a genus for itself with estimated measure and later discovers equality before separation, the Hanafi position is that it is valid; after separation, it is invalid. Zafar says it is valid even after separation because equality exists in God's knowledge, but the Hanafi response is that the condition of equality must be actualized by the parties' actions, as rulings are based on manifest acts.

Key Terms
صرف (ṣarf): Currency exchange, specifically the sale of gold for silver or vice versa, governed by strict rules of hand-to-hand delivery and equality when same genus.
تقابض (taqābuḍ): Mutual hand-to-hand delivery of both commodities in the same session before separation; a condition for the validity of sarf.
جزاف (jazāf): Sale by estimated quantity without exact measurement; allowed in sarf for different genera but with same genus must eventually be equal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence if two parties in a sarf contract separate before they have physically exchanged the gold and silver?
A) The contract is valid but they must re-exchange.
B) The contract becomes void (batil) and must be renewed.
C) The contract is suspended until they meet again.
D) The contract becomes a loan.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, the condition of immediate hand-to-hand exchange before physical separation is essential; failure to do so renders the contract void.

Same Genus Exchange: Equality Required and the Zafar Dispute
Clarifies the condition of equality for same-genus exchange and records intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that selling a genus for itself requires equality, and evaluate Zafar's dissenting view on jazaaf.

Core Idea
Gold for gold, silver for silver must be equal weight; estimated sale is only valid if equality is confirmed before separation.

This passage addresses the sale of a commodity for its own genus (e.g., gold for gold, silver for silver). The Hanafi school insists on exact equality in weight, regardless of quality or workmanship. The text also records a dispute with Zafar: if the parties sell by estimated measure (jazaaf) and later discover equality, the majority Hanafi view requires that the discovery happens before separation; otherwise the sale is invalid. Zafar argues that equality in reality suffices, but the Hanafi response emphasizes that conditions must be fulfilled by human action.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for same genus exchange
The text says: 'La bayʻ al-jins bi al-jins illā musāwiyan' – no sale of a genus for the same genus except equally. This applies even if quality differs.
The condition is tamāthul (equality) in weight, not value.
2
Understand why the matn mentioned faḍl and jazāf but not equality
The commentator explains: equality is obviously valid (lā shubhah fī jawāz al-tasāwī), so the matn only mentioned the doubtful cases (faḍl and jazāf).
This is a useful insight into the author's pedagogical method.
3
Analyze the jazaaf scenario for same genus
If two parties sell a genus for itself by estimated measure, then discover equality before separation, the sale is valid. After separation, it is invalid.
The condition of equality must be realized (muḥaqqqaq) before the contract is finalized through separation.
4
Examine Zafar's dissent and the Hanafi response
Zafar argues that the sale is valid even after separation because equality exists in God's knowledge. The Hanafi response: equality is a condition (sharṭ) that must be fulfilled by the parties' actions (bi fiʻlinā). Rulings are based on manifest acts, not hidden reality.
This dispute highlights the Hanafi principle that sharā'iʻ are based on ẓāhir and taklīf.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
If someone buys gold coins of one weight for gold coins of another weight by estimating, they must ensure exact weight before leaving the session, or the sale is invalid.
Modern equivalence: exchanging gold bars of different weights requires precise measurement before separation.

Source Text

(1)4
ثم قيل : التقابض شرط لصحة العقد في الصرف، وكان ينبغي أن يكون مقروناً به، إلا أنَّ حالة المجلس أقيمت مقامَ حالة العقدِ تيسيراً ، وقيل : شرط لبقاء العقد على الصحة، وهو الأصح. وفي فوائد القدوري»: المراد بالقبض هاهنا القبض باليد لا بالتخلية، وفي «المجتبى»: الافتراق في الصرف والسَّلَم يفارق الافتراق في الإيجاب والقبول وخيارِ المخيرة ؛ فإنَّ الإعراض بالقيام أو بالاشتغال بعمل آخر مفارقة ولا كذلك هاهنا. فإنَّ الافتراق بالأبدان هو المراد هاهنا دون المكان، حتى لو قاما فذهبا معاً، أو ناما، أو أغمي عليهما، أو طال قعودهما لا يبطل الصرف، وعن محمد ه : إن قاما أو أحدهما فهو فرقة ، وإن ناما جالسين فلا ، وعنه القعود الطويلُ فرقة دون اليسير، ذكره العلامة الشمني في كمال الدراية (٢).
[١]قوله : وصح ؛ إن تقابضا في المجلس ؛ لأنَّ المستحق هو القبض قبل الافتراق دون التسوية ؛ لما مر من الحديث، فلا يضرُّهُ الجزاف وإن افترقا قبل قبضهما، أو قبل قبض أحدهما بطل ؛ لفوات الشرط وهو القبض. ولهذا لا يصح فيه شرط الخيار والأجل ؛ لأنَّ الخيار يمنع استحقاق القبض ما دام الخيارُ باقياً ؛ لأنَّ استحقاقة مبني على الملك، والخيار يمنعه، وبالأجل يفوتُ القبض المستحقُ بالعقدِ شرعاً، إلا إذا أسقط الخيار أو الأجل فيعود صحيحاً لزوال المفسد قبل تقرره

[۱]
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
لا بيع الجنس بالجنس إلا مساوياً، وإن اختلفا جودة وصياغة، ولا يصح التصرف في ثمن الصرف قبل قبضه ، فلو شَرَى به ثوباً ، فسدَ شراءُ الثَّوب لا بيع الجنس بالجنس إلا مساوياً، وإن اختلفا جودة وصياغة)، وإنما ذكر الفضل والجزافَ ولم يَذكُرُ التَّساوي ؛ لأنَّه لا شُبهة في جواز التّساوي، بل الشبهة في الفضل والجزاف فذكرهما.
(ولا يصح التَّصرُّفُ في ثَمَنِ الصَّرفِ قبل قبضه ، فلو شَرَى به ثوباً ، فسدَ شراء الثَّوب) : أي لو اشترى بثمن الصرف قبل قبضه ثوباً فسدَ شراءُ الثَّوب". ولو باع الفضة أو الذهب بجنسه مجازفة ، ثمَّ علما تساويهما قبل الافتراق صح، وبعده لا يصح، وقال زفر : يصح؛ لأنَّ التساوي حق الشرع، وقد وجد حالة العقد، قلنا : التسوية شرطٌ واجب علينا، فيجب تحصيله بفعلنا. أما وجوده في علم الله الله لا يصلح شرطاً للجواز ؛ لأنَّ الأحكام تبنى على فعل العبادِ تحقيقاً لمعنى الابتلاء ، كذا في «التبيين») ، وغيره من الكتب المعتبرة. [1] قوله : لا بيع...الخ ؛ أي لا يصح بيع الجنس إلا أن يكونا متساويين لما مرَّ من قوله : «الذهب بالذهب والفضة بالفضة، مثلاً بمثل، سواء بسواء، يداً بيد (۲)، وفي المجازفة احتمال الربا.
[٢]قوله : إنما ذكر ... الخ ؛ جواب عمّا يتوهّم هاهنا من أنَّ البيع إما أن يكون لفضل أو جزاف أو تساو، فلم لم يذكر الثالث، وحاصل الجواب أنَّ البيع بالتساوي لا شبهة في جوازه ؛ فلذا لم يذكره
[٣] قوله : فسد شراء الثوب ؛ لأنَّ قبض العين واجب في بدل الصرف، والاستبدال يفوته.
فإن قيل : ينبغي أن لا يفسد ؛ لأنَّ الدراهم لا تتعين في العقـودِ عيناً كانت أو ديناً ، فانصرف العقد إلى مطلق الدَّراهم وهو المنقول عن زفر له. قلنا : الثمن في الصرف مبيع ، إذ البيع لا بدَّ له من المبيع، ولا شيء سوى
Book of Sarf: Its definition and rulings. And the sale of gold for silver with excess and estimated measure is valid. ... And the sale of a genus for the same genus is only valid if equal, even if they differ in quality and workmanship. And it is not valid to dispose of the price in a sarf contract before receiving it; so if one buys a garment with it, the purchase of the garment is invalid. ...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of currency exchange (sarf) from the Hanafi tradition, commenting on the matn of Al-Wiqayah. It begins by affirming the validity of selling gold for silver with excess (fadl) and estimated quantity (jazaaf) provided hand-to-hand exchange (taqabud) occurs in the same session before separation. The commentary explains that the requirement of hand-to-hand exchange is a condition for the contract's validity, and the ruling applies whether the items are specified (like jewelry or bullion) or unspecified (like coins). The preferred view is that taqabud is a condition for the contract's continuance in validity. The passage then addresses the meaning of separation (iftiraq): it is physical separation of bodies, not merely standing or walking away. Next, it states that selling a genus for itself (e.g., gold for gold, silver for silver) is only valid if equal in weight, even if quality differs. The commentator clarifies that the matn mentioned excess and estimated measure because equality is indisputably permissible. Finally, the text prohibits disposing of the price in a sarf contract before receiving it; for example, buying a garment with that price invalidates the garment purchase. A dispute with Zafar is recorded: if one sells a genus for itself with estimated measure and later discovers equality before separation, the Hanafi position is that it is valid; after separation, it is invalid. Zafar says it is valid even after separation because equality exists in God's knowledge, but the Hanafi response is that the condition of equality must be actualized by the parties' actions, as rulings are based on manifest acts.

Key Terms
تساوي (tasāwī): Equality in weight; a condition for selling a genus for itself.
مجازفة (mujāzafah): Synonym of jazāf; sale by estimated quantity without exact measurement.
زفر (Zafar): A Hanafi jurist (Zafar ibn al-Hudhayl) known for his opinion that equality in reality suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if two parties sell gold for gold by estimated measure and discover equality after they have separated?
A) The sale is valid because they were equal.
B) The sale is invalid because they separated before confirming equality.
C) The sale is valid only if they intended to measure later.
D) The sale is suspended pending measurement.

Key Takeaway
When selling a commodity for its own genus, equality in weight is a condition that must be confirmed before separation; separation before confirmation invalidates the contract.
