Basic Rulings of Sarf: Equality and Prohibition of Disposal Before Receipt
Ruling on riba al-fadl in sarf and prohibition of using price before receipt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two fundamental conditions for a valid sarf transaction and apply them to scenarios involving guess (jizaf) and the timing of knowledge of equality.

Core Idea
In sarf, equality and hand-to-hand delivery are mandatory; disposing of the price before receipt invalidates the transaction.

This lesson unpacks the foundational rules of sarf (currency exchange) from the Hanafi school. The passage establishes two key principles: (1) trading same-genus ribawi items requires exact equality in weight, and (2) the price in sarf must be physically received before any disposal. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a scenario where parties sell by guess and later discover equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of equality
Locate the statement 'No sale of a genus for the same genus except equally.' Understand that this applies to ribawi items of the same type (e.g., gold for gold). Equality is required regardless of quality.
The text mentions that equality is so obvious it need not be mentioned explicitly; only excess (fadl) and guess (jizaf) are discussed because they involve doubt.
2
Analyze the prohibition of disposing the price before receipt
Find the part: 'It is not valid to dispose of the price of a currency exchange before taking possession of it.' Understand that using the received currency to buy something else before physically receiving it invalidates that second purchase.
The reasoning: in sarf, each item is both thaman (price) and mabi' (commodity); both must be exchanged hand-to-hand. Using the price substitutes it, breaking the hand-to-hand condition.
3
Examine the case of jizaf (guess) and the dispute
Read the scenario: if parties sell gold/silver by guess (without knowing exact weight) and later discover equality before separation, the sale is valid; after separation, it is invalid. Note Zufar's opposing view.
Zufar argues that equality existed in Allah's knowledge at contract time, so the contract is valid regardless of separation. The majority holds that equality must be actualized by the parties' action.
4
Understand the Hanafi response and the principle of ibtilā'
The Hanafi majority responds that equality is a condition (shart) that must be realized by human action, not merely by divine knowledge. Rulings are built on servants' actions to fulfill the purpose of trial (ibtilā').
This distinguishes between objective existence and subjective fulfillment; the condition is not satisfied unless the parties actively ensure equality and know it.

There is no sale of a genus for the same genus except equally, even if they differ in quality and workmanship. And he only mentioned excess and [sale by] guess and did not mention equality, because there is no doubt about the permissibility of equality, rather the doubt is about excess and guess, so he mentioned them. (And it is not valid to dispose of the price of a currency exchange before taking possession of it. So if one bought a garment with it, the purchase of the garment becomes invalid.) That is, if one bought a garment with the price of a currency exchange before taking possession of it, the purchase of the garment becomes invalid. And if one sold silver or gold for its genus by guess, then they knew their equality before separation, it is valid; after separation, it is not valid. Zufar said: it is valid because equality is the right of the Shari'ah and it existed at the time of the contract. We said: equalization is a condition obligatory upon us, so its achievement is required by our action. As for its existence in the knowledge of Allah, that does not qualify as a condition for permissibility, because rulings are built upon the actions of servants to realize the meaning of trial. Thus in 'al-Tabyin' and other reliable books. [1] His saying: 'No sale...' etc.; that is, a sale of the same genus is not valid unless they are equal, due to what preceded of his saying: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, like for like, equal for equal, hand to hand', and in guess [sale] there is the possibility of riba. [2] His saying: 'He only mentioned...' etc.; is a response to what might be imagined here: that the sale is either for excess, or guess, or equality, so why did he not mention the third? The summary of the answer is that sale by equality has no doubt about its permissibility; therefore he did not mention it. [3] His saying: 'The purchase of the garment becomes invalid'; because taking possession of the specie is obligatory in exchange for currency, and substitution [for it] would miss [that obligation]. If it is said: it should not be invalid, because dirhams are not specified in contracts, whether they are a specific object or a debt, so the contract applies to absolute dirhams, and that is the transmitted [view] from Zufar. We said: the price in currency exchange is a commodity (mabi'), because a sale must have a commodity, and there is nothing other than that. If it is said: if it were a commodity, it would be specified. We said: it is not necessary that being a commodity entails being specified, for salam is not specified in its commodity in absolute terms; rather it is a commodity from one aspect and a price from another aspect, and that is sufficient to negate permissibility. And it is not a commodity because doubt in prohibitions is like certainty. Thus in 'al-Kafi' and others.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the rules of sarf (currency exchange). It begins with the fundamental ruling: selling one genus for the same genus (e.g., gold for gold, silver for silver) is only permissible if they are equal in weight, regardless of differences in quality or workmanship. The commentator explains why the original text only mentions sale by excess (fadl) and sale by guess (jizaf) but not sale by equality (tasawi): equality is clearly permissible without any doubt, so there is no need to state it. The next ruling addresses the prohibition of disposing of the price in a sarf contract before taking possession (qabd). If someone uses the gold or silver received in a sarf transaction to buy a garment before physically receiving it, the purchase of the garment is invalid. The reason is that taking possession of the price is obligatory in sarf (it must be hand-to-hand), and by using it to buy something else, one effectively substitutes it, thus failing to fulfill the condition of immediate exchange. The passage then discusses a scenario where two parties sell gold or silver by guess (without knowing the exact weight) and later discover equality before separating. According to the Hanafi position, if they learn of the equality and ensure equal amounts before parting, the sale is valid; if they separate without knowing, it becomes invalid. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, holds that the contract is valid even if they separate without knowing, because the equality existed in Allah's knowledge at the time of the contract. The Hanafi response is that equality is a condition that must be actualized by the parties' actions; its mere existence in divine knowledge does not suffice for validity, because Islamic rulings are based on outward actions (fi'l al-'ibad) to fulfill the purpose of trial (ibtilā'). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved in favor of the majority view.

Key Terms
صرف (ṣarf): Exchange of gold and silver (or other ribawi items of the same genus) with specific conditions.
مبيع (mabīʿ): The thing sold; in the context of ṣarf, the price (thaman) is considered as the commodity (mabīʿ) from one aspect.
ثمن الصرف (thaman al-ṣarf): The price in a currency exchange transaction; it must be received before any disposal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sarf transaction where two parties sell gold for gold by guess (jizaf) and later discover they are equal, but they separate without knowing the equality, what is the ruling according to the majority Hanafi position?
A) The sale is valid because equality existed in Allah's knowledge.
B) The sale is invalid because equality must be known to the parties before separation.
C) The sale is valid if they intended equality at the time of contract.
D) The sale is suspended until they know the equality.

Key Takeaway
Sarf (currency exchange) requires both equality and hand-to-hand delivery; equality must be actualized and known by the parties before separation for the contract to be valid.

Mixed Ṣarf Cases: When Invalidity Spreads to the Whole Contract
Rulings on mixed ṣarf sales with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between concurrent (muqārin) and incidental (ṭāriʾ) invalidity in mixed ṣarf transactions and state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a ṣarf defect is inherent from contract inception, it corrupts the whole; if incidental, only the affected part.

This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: when a single contract combines a valid non-ribawi sale with an invalid ṣarf element (e.g., deferred payment for silver), does the invalidity spread to the entire contract or only to the ṣarf part? Two analogies compete—one with selling a free person and a slave, the other with losing a slave before delivery. The outcome determines the buyer's right of rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed transaction scenarios
Review the three cases: selling a slave girl with a necklace, selling a sword with silver ornament, and the variations in payment terms. Note which part is ṣarf (gold/silver) and which is non-ribawi.
The key is that ṣarf requires hand-to-hand delivery (qabd) in the session.
2
Apply the presumption of good faith (ḥusn al-ẓann)
For partially paid prices, assume the cash payment is for the ṣarf portion because Muslims avoid ribā. This allocates the deferred amount to the non-ṣarf item, preserving validity.
This presumption is a mechanism to salvage the contract when possible.
3
Recognize the point of dispute
When the entire price is deferred, the ṣarf part is clearly invalid (deferment invalidates ṣarf). Now the question: does this invalidity affect only the ṣarf part or the whole contract?
4
Compare the two analogical arguments
Abu Hanifa analogizes to combining a free person and a slave in one sale: the sale is entirely invalid because the free person cannot be sold (concurrent defect). The Sahibayn analogize to buying two slaves where one perishes before delivery: only the lost slave's part is invalid (incidental defect).
The distinction hinges on whether the defect is present at contract formation (concurrent) or arises later (incidental).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer can rescind the entire contract. According to the Sahibayn, the buyer can rescind only the ṣarf portion and keep the non-ṣarf item at the proportionate price.
This difference directly affects the buyer's remedy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف تعريفه وأحكامه

وم باع أمةً تَعْدِلُ ألف درهم مع طوق ألف بألفين ، ونَقَدَ من الثّمن ألفاً، أو باعها بألفين ألفاً نسيئة وألفاً نقداً ، أو باغ سيفاً حليته خمسون ، وتخلص بلا ضرر بمئة ، ونقد خمسين فما نقدَ ثَمَنُ الفضةِ) ، وهو ألف في بيع الأمة، والخمسون في بيع السَّيف (سكت، أو قال: خذ هذا من ثمنها ، أما إذا سكت فظاهر ؛ لأنَّه لَمَّا الثمن، فيجعلُ واحد من البدلين مبيعاً لعدم الأولوية، وبيع المبيع قبل القبض لا يصح. فإن قيل : لو كان مبيعاً لكان متعيّناً.
قلنا : ليس من ضرورة كونه مبيعاً كونه متعيّناً ، فإنَّ السَّلَم ليس المبيع فيه متعيّناً ؛ مطلقاً، بل هو مبيع من وجه وثمن من وجه، وهو كاف لسلب الجواز ،

إذ الشبهة في الحرمات كالحقيقة، كذا في «الكافي»، وغيره.
[١]قوله : ثمن الفضة؛ أما في الصورة الأولى ؛ فلأنَّ حصة الطوق يجب قبضه في المجلس ؛ لكونه بدل الصرف، والظاهرُ من العاقدين الإتيان بالواجب ؛ لأنَّ دينهما وعقلهما يمنعانه من مباشرة ما لا يجوز شرعاً، فيصرفُ المتأخر إلى الجارية، والمقبوض والحال إلى الطوق ؛ لتحسين الظن بالمسلم.
وأما في الصورة الثانية فكذلك أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ التأجيل في الصرف باطل، وفي بيع الجارية جائز، فيصرف الأجلُ إلى الأمة دون الطوق، إذ المباشرة على وجه الصحة لا على وجه البطلان.
ولو كان كل الثمن مؤجلاً فسد البيع في الجميع عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا : يفسد في الطوقِ دون الجارية ؛ لأنَّ القبض ليس بشرط في حصتهما، فيتقدَّرُ الفاسد بقدر المفسد.
ولأبي حنيفة له أنَّ الفاسد مقارن، فيتعدّى إلى الجميع، كما لو جمع بين عبدٍ وحر في البيع، بخلاف الفساد في المسألة الأولى فإنّه طارئ فلا يتعدى إلى غيره، كما إذا اشترى عبدين فهلك أحدهما قبل القبض أو استحق بعده ، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح
The Book of Currency Exchange: its definition and rulings. 301. And whoever sells a slave girl worth one thousand dirhams with a necklace worth one thousand for two thousand, and pays one thousand of the price; or sells her for two thousand: one thousand on credit and one thousand in cash; or buys a sword whose ornament is fifty [dirhams] and the blade without damage for one hundred, and pays fifty; then what he paid is the price of the silver. That is one thousand in the sale of the slave girl, and fifty in the sale of the sword. He is silent, or says: take this from her price. If he is silent, it is apparent because he made the price, so he makes one of the two countervalues a commodity due to lack of priority, and selling a commodity before taking possession is not valid. If it is said: if it were a commodity, it would be specified. We said: it is not necessary that being a commodity entails being specified, for salam is not specified in its commodity in absolute terms; rather it is a commodity from one aspect and a price from another, and that is sufficient to negate permissibility. And it is not a commodity because doubt in prohibitions is like certainty. Thus in 'al-Kafi' and others. [1] His saying: 'the price of the silver'; in the first scenario, because the share of the necklace must be taken possession of in the session, as it is the countervalue of the currency exchange, and the apparent [conduct] of the two contractors is to fulfill what is obligatory, because their religion and intellect prevent them from directly doing what is not permissible legally. So the deferred [payment] is directed to the slave girl, and the received [amount] in hand is directed to the necklace, to think well of the Muslim. And in the second scenario also similarly, because deferment in currency exchange is invalid, but in the sale of the slave girl it is permissible, so the credit is directed to the slave girl and not to the necklace, because the direct action is on the basis of validity, not invalidity. If all the price were deferred, the sale would be entirely invalid according to Abu Hanifa; but the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: it is invalid only for the necklace, not for the slave girl, because possession is not a condition in their portion, so the invalid part is estimated by the extent of the corrupting element. For Abu Hanifa, the invalid [element] is concurrent, so it extends to the whole, just as if one combines a slave and a free person in a sale; unlike the invalidity in the first issue, which is incidental and does not extend to other parts, like if one buys two slaves and one of them perishes before delivery or is found to be owned by another afterwards. Al-Zayla'i mentioned in his commentary...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdah al-Ri'ayah addresses complex transactions that combine a ṣarf element (exchange of gold or silver) with a non-ṣarf element (sale of a slave girl or a sword). The ruling aims to determine which part of the price corresponds to the silver (the ṣarf item) and which to the non-ṣarf item, and whether the transaction is valid. Three scenarios are presented: (1) Selling a slave girl (worth 1000) with a necklace (worth 1000) for 2000, paying 1000 in cash; (2) Selling the same for 2000 with 1000 credit and 1000 cash; (3) Selling a sword with silver ornament (50) and blade (100) for 150, paying 50. In each, the paid amount is interpreted as the price of the silver (the ṣarf portion), and the unpaid or credit portion is for the non-ṣarf item (slave girl or blade). This interpretation relies on the principle that Muslims are presumed to fulfill obligations; since ṣarf requires hand-to-hand delivery, the cash payment is assigned to the necklace (ṣarf), and the deferred payment to the slave girl (non-ṣarf). If the entire price is deferred, Abu Hanifa rules the entire contract invalid because the invalidity (due to deferment in ṣarf) is concurrent (muqārin) and therefore corrupts the whole contract. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only the ṣarf part is invalid, as the invalidity is proportionate to the corrupting element. Abu Hanifa supports his view by analogizing to a single sale including a free person and a slave, which is entirely invalid because the free person cannot be sold. The Sahibayn analogize to a case where one of two purchased slaves is lost before delivery, which only invalidates that part. The distinction is that in the mixed ṣarf case, the invalidity is inherent in the contract from the start (muqārin), whereas the lost slave is an incidental defect (ṭāriʾ) after the contract. Thus, the ruling depends on whether the defect is concurrent or incidental.

Key Terms
طوق (tawq): A necklace made of silver or gold, representing the sarf item in the mixed transaction.
فساد مقارن (fasād muqārin): Invalidity that is concurrent with the contract from its inception, affecting the entire transaction.
فساد طارئ (fasād ṭāriʾ): Invalidity that arises after the contract due to an external event, affecting only the relevant part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a single sale combines a slave girl and a necklace (sarf) with the price fully deferred, what is the ruling?
A) Only the sale of the necklace is invalid; the slave girl sale is valid.
B) The entire sale is invalid because the invalid element is concurrent.
C) The sale is valid because the buyer can pay later.
D) The sale is valid if the parties had good intentions.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a concurrent defect (muqārin) in a mixed ṣarf transaction corrupts the entire contract, while an incidental defect (ṭāriʾ) only affects its part.

Assigning Payment to Silver in Mixed Sales
Rulings on payment allocation for silver in mixed sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for allocating immediate payment to the silver component in mixed sales and evaluate the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on total deferment.

Core Idea
In a mixed sale, immediate payment is assigned to the silver to satisfy sarf requirements and preserve contract validity.

When a sale includes both silver and non-silver items (e.g., a slave girl with a silver necklace or a sword with a silver hilt), the requirement that silver be exchanged hand-to-hand (qabd fi al-majlis) creates a potential conflict with deferred payment. This lesson explores how Hanafi jurists resolve this by assigning any immediate payment to the silver component, assuming good faith from the Muslim contracting parties. We will also examine the internal dispute when all payment is deferred, and the linguistic reasoning that allows the dual form to refer to the silver alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed-sale scenarios
The source presents three cases: (1) slave girl worth 1000 dirhams with a necklace worth 1000 sold for 2000, with 1000 paid cash; (2) same sale with 1000 cash and 1000 credit; (3) sword with silver hilt worth 50 sold for 100, with 50 paid cash. In each, the immediate payment equals the silver value.
The silver component's value is stated separately to make the assignment explicit.
2
Determine the ruling on payment allocation
Whether the seller is silent or says 'take this as part of its price,' the paid amount is considered the price of the silver. This is because the silver transaction (sarf) requires immediate possession; deferral would invalidate the sarf portion. To avoid that, the law assumes the Muslim parties fulfill their obligation by directing the cash to the silver.
If the payment were assigned to the non-silver item, the silver portion would be on credit, invalidating the sarf.
3
Analyze the dispute on total deferment
If the entire price is deferred (no immediate payment), Abu Hanifa rules the whole sale invalid, likening it to a contract combining a free man and a slave. The Sahibayn hold only the silver portion invalid; the non-silver part remains valid because possession is not a condition for it, and the defect is limited to the corrupting element.
The disagreement hinges on whether the invalidity is concurrent (muqarin) and spreads, or incidental (tari') and confined.
4
Apply the linguistic principle for the dual form
When the seller says 'min thamanihima' (from the price of both), the dual is interpreted as referring to the silver alone to preserve contract validity. The author cites Quranic and prophetic examples where the dual means one, such as 'both's fish' referring to one fish and 'both's prayer' referring to one prayer.
This interpretation reflects the principle of building on the apparent condition of the Muslim (al-zahir min hal al-muslim al-itiya' bi al-wajib).

Source Text

وأما في الصورة الثانية فكذلك أيضاً؛ لأنَّ التأجيل في الصرف باطل، وفي بيع الجارية جائز، فيصرف الأجلُ إلى الأمة دون الطوق، إذ المباشرة على وجه الصحة لا على وجه البطلان.
ولو كان كل الثمن مؤجلاً فسد البيع في الجميع عند أبي حنيفة له، وقالا: يفسد في الطوقِ دون الجارية؛ لأنَّ القبض ليس بشرط في حصتهما، فيتقدَّرُ الفاسد بقدر المفسد.
ولأبي حنيفة له أنَّ الفاسد مقارن، فيتعدّى إلى الجميع، كما لو جمع بين عبدٍ وحر في البيع، بخلاف الفساد في المسألة الأولى فإنّه طارئ فلا يتعدى إلى غيره، كما إذا اشترى عبدين فهلك أحدهما قبل القبض أو استحق بعده، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز».
وأما في الصورة الثالثة فكذلك أيضاً، فإنَّ حصة الحلية يجب قبضُها في المجلس، والظاهر من حال المسلم أن لا يترك الواجب.
[١] قوله: أما...الخ؛ توضيحهُ: إِنَّ كونَ المنقودِ ثمن الفضة في حالة السكوت؛ أي إذا لم يقل: خذ في ثمنهما ظاهر؛ لأن أمرهما يحمل على الصلاح، وأما إذا قال: خذ من ثمنهما، فإنَّ المثنى قد يعبر به عن الواحد كما في قوله علة: يَخْرُجُ مِنْهُمَا اللُّؤْلُو والمرجان)، والمراد أحدهما. وفي قوله: نَسِيَا حُونَهُمَا)، والنَّاسى أحدهما، وهو صاحب موسى: بدليل قوله: فَإِنِّي نَسِيتُ الْحُونَ، وفي قوله جلالة: وفي قوله: قَدْ أُجِيبَت دَعْوَتُكُمَا، والداعي كان موسى، و في قوله: «إذا كنتما في سفركما فأذنا وأقيما، والمراد أحدهما.

And whoever sells a slave girl worth one thousand dirhams with a necklace worth one thousand for two thousand, and pays from the price one thousand, or sells her for two thousand — one thousand on credit and one thousand cash — or buys a sword whose hilt is fifty, and he acquires it without harm for one hundred, and pays fifty, then what he paid is the price of the silver — which is one thousand in the sale of the slave girl, and fifty in the sale of the sword — (whether he is silent, or says: take this as part of its price). As for when he is silent, it is apparent; because he intended the price, so he makes one of the two exchange items a sold item due to lack of preference, and selling the sold item before taking possession is not valid. If it is said: If it were a sold item it would be specified. We say: It is not necessary that a sold item be specified; for salam (advance sale) does not have the sold item specified absolutely, but it is a sold item from one aspect and a price from another, and that is sufficient to negate permissibility. And because it is not a sold item, since doubt regarding prohibitions is like certainty. Likewise in al-Kafi and others.
[1] His statement: the price of the silver. As for the first scenario, because the share of the necklace must be taken in the session, as it is the exchange of sarf, and the apparent condition of the two contracting parties is to perform the obligatory, because their religion and intellect prevent them from doing what is not permissible legally, so the deferred part is directed to the slave girl, and the paid part is directed to the necklace, to have a good opinion of the Muslim.
As for the second scenario, likewise, because deferment in sarf is invalid, and in the sale of the slave girl it is valid, so the deferment is directed to the slave girl and not the necklace, since the action is on the basis of validity, not invalidity.
And if all the price were deferred, the sale would be invalid in all according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is invalid regarding the necklace but not the slave girl, because taking possession is not a condition for their share, so the invalid part is limited to the amount of the corrupting element.
And for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, [the reasoning] is that the invalid is concurrent, so it extends to all, as when a slave and a free man are combined in a sale, unlike the invalidity in the first issue, which is incidental, so it does not extend to others, as when one buys two slaves and one perishes before possession or is claimed afterwards, as mentioned by al-Zaylai in Sharh al-Kanz.
As for the third scenario, likewise, because the share of the hilt must be taken in the session, and the apparent condition of a Muslim is that he does not abandon the obligatory.
[1] His statement: As for... etc. Explanation: That what is paid is the price of the silver in the case of silence, i.e., if he does not say: take it as part of the price of both, is apparent because the affair of the two is assumed to be correct. And if he says: take it as part of the price of both, then the dual may be used to mean the singular, as in His statement, the Exalted: "There comes forth from both of them the pearl and coral" — and what is meant is one of them. And in His statement: "They forgot their fish" — and the one who forgot was one of them, the companion of Moses, as evidenced by His statement: "Indeed I forgot the fish." And in His statement, the Exalted: in His statement: "Your prayer has been answered" — and the supplicant was Moses. And in his statement, peace be upon him: "When you both are on a journey, then call the adhan and establish the prayer" — and what is meant is one of them.
So it is interpreted in our case accordingly, considering the condition of the Muslim. Hence if a man said to his two wives: "When you both menstruate a menstruation or give birth to a child, then you are both divorced," and one of them menstruates or gives birth, she is divorced, because by it is meant one of them, due to the impossibility of both experiencing a single menstruation or a single child.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Hidayah regarding the rules of sarf (currency exchange) when a sale involves an item containing silver (such as a necklace or sword hilt) combined with a non-silver item (such as a slave girl or the sword blade itself). The author explains the ruling on which part of the payment corresponds to the silver component.

The author opens by presenting three scenarios:
1. A slave girl worth 1000 dirhams with a necklace worth 1000 dirhams is sold for 2000 dirhams, with 1000 paid immediately.
2. The same sale but with 1000 paid immediately and 1000 on credit.
3. A sword with a silver hilt worth 50 dirhams sold for 100 dirhams, with 50 paid immediately.

In each case, the payment made (1000 in the first two, 50 in the third) is considered the price of the silver component, regardless of whether the seller is silent or says "take this as part of its price." The reasoning is that the silver transaction must be completed in the session (qabd fi al-majlis) because it is part of a sarf contract (exchange of silver for silver). By assigning the immediate payment to the silver, the contract is preserved as valid regarding the silver portion. If the payment were assigned to the non-silver item, that would imply the silver sale was on credit, which is invalid.

The passage then addresses a potential objection: Why not treat the paid amount as the price of both items? The response is that if it were for both, then the unpaid portion would be treated as a separate sale of the unpaid item before possession, which is invalid. Therefore, for lack of preference, it is determined that the paid amount is for the silver. The author also compares this to salam sale, where the sold item is not specified.

A dispute is then mentioned: If the entire price is deferred (all on credit), Abu Hanifa rules the entire sale is invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that only the silver portion is invalid, and the non-silver part is valid. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the invalidity is concurrent (muqarin) and thus spreads to the whole contract, analogous to combining a slave and a free man in one sale. The Sahibayn distinguish this case from one where invalidity is incidental (tari'), which does not spread.

Finally, the author clarifies the linguistic point: when the seller says "take this as part of the price of both" using the dual, it can be interpreted as referring to one of them (the silver) because the dual form is sometimes used in the Quran to mean one, citing examples like "from both of them come forth pearl and coral" (meaning from one of them: the salt water) and the story of Moses and his companion. So here too, the phrase is interpreted to mean the price of the silver component, to preserve the validity of the contract and assume good faith from the Muslim.

This passage thus demonstrates the Hanafi principle of istihsan (juristic preference) in preserving contracts by assigning payments in a way that avoids invalidity, along with intricate reasoning involving qiyas and linguistic interpretation.

Key Terms
صرف (ṣarf): Exchange of gold for gold or silver for silver with immediate mutual possession.
طوق (ṭawq): A necklace made of silver or gold worn around the neck, considered a separate item of value.
حلية (ḥilyah): The silver fittings or hilt of a sword, considered as silver for sarf purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sale of a slave girl (1000 dirhams) with a silver necklace (1000 dirhams) for 2000 dirhams, if 1000 dirhams is paid immediately and the rest on credit, which part of the price is the immediate payment considered for?
A) The slave girl only
B) The necklace only
C) The entire price of both
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
When a sale includes both silver and non-silver items, any immediate payment is allocated to the silver to satisfy the hand-to-hand requirement of sarf, and dual phrasing can be interpreted to refer to the silver alone to preserve contract validity.

Partial Payment in Sarf: Valid for the Paid Portion
Ruling on partial payment in sarf with reasoning and objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sarf transaction with partial payment, explain why the invalidity does not spread, and evaluate the responses to Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
In sarf, partial payment followed by separation makes the sale valid only for the paid portion; invalidity does not spread.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a specific scenario in currency exchange (sarf): a person sells a silver vessel, receives part of the price, and then the parties separate before full payment. The teacher explains that the sale is valid for the paid portion and the vessel becomes co-owned. The key is that the invalidity is secondary and does not corrupt the valid part. The passage also discusses other scenarios and responds to objections from Imam Zufar and the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a silver vessel (sarf) with partial payment and separation before full payment. The Hanafi ruling: valid for the paid portion, invalid for the unpaid portion, and the invalidity does not spread.
The act involves the condition of receipt before separation (qabd qabl al-iftiraq).
2
Understand the condition and its partial fulfillment
In sarf, receipt of both countervalues must occur before the parties separate. Here, the price is partially received, so the condition is met for that part. For the unpaid part, the condition is not met, causing invalidity.
The invalidity is secondary (tar'iy) because it arises after the contract formation, not at inception.
3
Analyze the spread of invalidity
The invalidity of the unpaid portion does not spread to the paid portion because it is a secondary defect. The contract is treated as partially valid, leading to co-ownership of the vessel.
This is analogous to the chapter of salam (advance payment).
4
Examine the other scenarios and Zufar's objection
The passage lists scenarios where the price equals, is less than, or unknown relative to the vessel's value. All are invalid due to surplus free of consideration. Zufar argues that uncertainty should favor validity; the Hanafi response counts probabilities: invalidity has two aspects (equal or less) against one (more), so invalidity is preponderant.
The Hanafi response uses a rational argument based on the balance of probabilities.
5
Determine the practical outcome and cross-madhhab difference
Practically, the buyer owns a share proportional to payment. The Shafi'i school considers the entire sale invalid. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and rooted in the concept of secondary invalidity.
No option of defect exists for the buyer because co-ownership arose from his own action (not paying in time).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن باع إناء فضة وقبض بعض ثمنه، ثمّ افترقا صح فيما قبض فقط، واشتركا في الإناء. أي صح البيع فيما قبض ثمنه، وفسد فيما لم يقبض، ولا يشيع الفساد كما ذكرنا في باب السلم؛ لأن الفساد طارئ. عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي. وفي الصور الباقية: لا يجوز. أما في صورة المساواة؛ فلأن الجفن والحمائل فضل خال عن العوض، فإن مقابلة الفضة بالفضة في البيع يكون بالأجزاء. وأما في صورة كون الثمن أقل فلظهور الفضل الخالي عن العوض. وأما في صورة أنه لا يدري فلعدم علم المساواة عند العقد، وتوهم الفضل. فإن قيل: الأصل هو الجواز، والمفسد للعقد إنما هو الفضل الخالي عن العوض، وهو غير متيقن فينبغي أن يحكم بالجواز، كما هو قول زفر. قلنا: إن ما لا يدرى لا يخلو إما أن يكون في الواقع مساوياً أو أقل أو زائداً، فإن كان زائداً فلا شبهة في جواز العقد، وإن كان مساوياً أو زائداً فيكون العقد فاسداً، فظهر أن جهة الصحة واحد، وجهة الفساد من وجهين وأحدهما يكفي للحكم، فعند اجتماعهما بالطريق الأولى. قوله: صح فيما قبض فقط؛ لوجود شرطه وهو القبض قبل الافتراق. قوله: واشتركا في الإناء؛ لأن عقد الصرف وقع على كله أولاً، ثم طرأ الفساد، وعلى ما لم يقبض، وهو لا يشيع على ما وجد فيه القبض، فحصلت الشركة في الكل بالتراضي، ولم يلزم تفريق الصفقة على المشتري قبل التمام؛ لأن الصفقة تمت بالتقابض ولو في البعض؛ ولأن هذا التفريق من جهة الشرع باشتراط القبض لا من جهة العاقد، فلا محذور فيه. وصار كما إذا هلك أحد العوضين، ولا يثبت للمشتري خيار العيب أيضاً بالشركة؛ لأن الشركة حصلت من جهته، وهو عدم النقد قبل الافتراق، بخلاف ما إذا هلك أحد العبدين قبل القبض، حيث يثبت الخيار في أخذ الباقي؛ لعدم الصنع منه.
And whoever sells a silver vessel and receives part of its price, then they separate, the sale is valid only for what he received, and they share the vessel. That is: the sale is valid for the price he received, and invalid for what was not received, and the invalidity does not spread, as we mentioned in the chapter of salam, because the invalidity is secondary. According to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. In the remaining scenarios: it is not permissible. As for the scenario of equality: because the scabbard and straps are a surplus free of consideration, since in selling silver for silver the exchange is by parts. As for the scenario where the price is less: due to the appearance of surplus free of consideration. As for the scenario where he does not know: due to lack of knowledge of equality at the time of contract, and the possibility of surplus. If it is said: the original principle is permissibility, and the corrupting factor is only the surplus free of consideration, and it is not certain, so it should be ruled permissible, as is the view of Zufar. We say: what is unknown can only be in reality equal, less, or more; if it is more, there is no doubt about the validity of the contract; if it is equal or less, then the contract is invalid. So it appears that the aspect of validity is one, and the aspect of invalidity is from two aspects, and one of them suffices for the ruling; when they combine a fortiori. He said: 'valid only for what he received' due to the existence of its condition, which is receipt before separation. He said: 'and they share the vessel' because the exchange contract initially applied to the whole, then invalidity occurred in what was not received, and it does not spread to what was received, so co-ownership occurred in the whole by mutual consent, and splitting the transaction is not imposed on the buyer before completion, because the transaction was completed by mutual receipt even partially; and because this splitting is from the Law's side due to the condition of receipt, not from the contracting party, so there is no harm. And it is like the case when one of the counter-values is destroyed; and the buyer is not entitled to the option of defect due to co-ownership either, because the co-ownership arose from his side, i.e., his failure to pay before separation, unlike if one of the two slaves is destroyed before possession, where he has the option to take the remainder due to the absence of his own doing.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of sarf (currency exchange) with its commentary. The matn states: when a silver vessel is sold and part of the price is paid, then the parties separate (prior to full payment), the sale is valid only for the part whose price was received, and the vessel becomes jointly owned between seller and buyer. The commentator explains that the invalidity of the unpaid portion does not spread to the paid portion because the defect (i.e., the lack of receipt for that part) is secondary (طارئ) and not inherent. This is the Hanafi position, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who would consider the entire sale invalid due to lack of full receipt before separation. The passage then lists other scenarios not mentioned in the matn: if the price equals the value of the vessel, or is less, or if equality is unknown—all are invalid because of an unjustified surplus (فضل خال عن العوض). An objection from Imam Zufar is presented: since the original principle is permissibility and the presence of surplus is uncertain, the contract should be deemed valid. The Hanafi response is that the unknown can only be one of three: equal, less, or more. If it is more, it is valid; if equal or less, invalid. Since the probabilities of invalidity (equal or less) are two and the probability of validity (more) is one, the stronger side is to rule invalidity. Moreover, the condition of receipt before separation in sarf is established, and invalidity is the default when it is absent. The commentary further clarifies why partial payment does not grant the buyer an option to return the vessel: the co-ownership arose from his own failure to pay, unlike cases of destruction of one of two slaves before possession where the buyer does get an option due to no fault of his own.

Key Terms
الصرف (al-sarf): A contract of exchange of gold, silver, or currency where immediate possession is required before separation.
الفساد الطارئ (al-fasad al-tari'): A defect that occurs after the contract's formation, not inherent in its inception.
الافتراق (al-iftiraq): The physical separation of the contracting parties after concluding the sale, which in sarf must occur after full payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sarf transaction where part of the price is paid before separation, what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the unpaid portion?
A) The sale is entirely valid.
B) The sale is entirely invalid.
C) The sale is valid only for the paid portion; the unpaid portion is invalid and does not spread.
D) The sale is valid only if the buyer has an option to return.

Key Takeaway
In sarf, when only part of the price is received before separation, the sale is valid for that part and the vessel becomes co-owned; the invalidity does not spread.

Partial Usurpation: Vessel vs. Pure Silver
Ruling on buyer's option when part of the item is claimed by a third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish items where co-ownership from usurpation is a defect (giving option) from those where it is not, and apply the Hanafi rationale.

Core Idea
Co-ownership is a defect only when division harms the item's value — vessel yes, pure silver no.

When a third party claims part of a sold item, the buyer may end up as co-owner. Does that give him the right to return the item? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line: it depends on whether the item is harmed by being divided. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya contrasts a silver vessel with a piece of pure silver (nuqra), showing how the 'harm' criterion decides the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the two items: a silver vessel (إناء الفضة) and a piece of pure silver (نقرة). In each, part is claimed by a third party after the sale.
Pure silver can be melted and remolded; a vessel has a specific shape.
2
State the ruling for each
For the vessel: buyer has option to take remainder at its share or return the whole. For pure silver: buyer must take remainder with no option.
The option is khiyar al-ayb (defect option) based on co-ownership as a defect.
3
Analyze the 'illa (cause)
The illa is whether co-ownership constitutes a defect. In the vessel, partitioning harms the value (التشقيص يضره). In pure silver, division does not harm because it can be melted down.
This is a talil (causation) argument: the ruling changes when the defect is absent.
4
Compare with partial payment
Earlier in the text, if co-ownership arises from the buyer's own partial payment, he cannot return because he consented. Here, usurpation is without consent, so he has the option.
Consent vs. non-consent distinguishes the two sources of co-ownership.
5
Extend to analogous cases
Dirhams and dinars (coins) are like pure silver: co-ownership is not a defect. Also, if usurpation occurs before possession, the buyer also has option due to splitting of the contract.
The principle generalizes to all fungibles that are not harmed by division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استحق بعضه أخذ المشتري باقيه بحصته أو رده، ولو استحق بعض قطعة نقرة بيعت أخذ ما بقي بحصته بلا خيار. أي إن استحق بعض الإناء، فالمشتري بالخيار؛ لأن الشركة عيب في الإناء، وفي صورة قبض بعض الثمن قد ثبت الشركة لكن لا يكون للمشتري الرد بهذا العيب؛ لأنه يثبت برضا المشتري؛ لأن الشركة إنما تثبت من جهته؛ لأنه نقد بعض الثمن دون البعض فتراضيا بهذا العيب بخلاف الاستحقاق إذ المشتري لم يرض به، فله ولاية الرد. ولو استحق بعض قطعة نقرة بيعت أخذ ما بقي بحصته بلا خيار؛ لأن الشركة ليست بعيب في قطعة النقرة؛ لأن التبعيض لا يضره. قوله: لأن الشركة عيب في الإناء؛ لأن التشقيص يضره، وهذا العيب كان موجوداً عند البيع، مقارناً له بغير صنعه بخلاف ما مر؛ لأن الشركة وقعت بصنعه وهو الافتراق قبل نقد الثمن، فالفرق بين. قوله: ولو استحق ... الخ؛ يعني لو باع قطعة نقرة فاستحق بعضها أخذ المشتري ما بقي بحصته بلا خيار؛ لأن الشركة ليست بعيب في قطعة النقرة، فإنه لا يلزم الانتقاض بالتبعيض، فلم يتضرر المشتري بالشركة فيهما، وهذا لو كان الاستحقاق بعد قبضهما. أما لو كان قبل قبضهما فله الخيار؛ لتفرق الصفقة عليه قبل التمام، كذا في «البحر»، والدرهم والدينار نظير النقرة؛ لأن الشركة في ذلك لا تعد عيباً، كذا في «الجوهرة». قوله: نقرة، بضم النون، كما في «المغرب» و«القاموس»: المذابة من الذهب أو الفضة، وقيل: الإذابة تسمى تبراً، كذا في «المصباح».
And if part of it is claimed (by a third party), the buyer takes the remainder at its share or returns it; and if part of a piece of pure silver (nuqra) that was sold is claimed, he takes what remains at its share without option. That is: if part of the vessel is claimed, the buyer has the option, because co-ownership is a defect in the vessel; and in the case of receipt of part of the price, co-ownership has been established, but the buyer does not have the right to return due to this defect because it is established with the buyer's consent; because the co-ownership arises from his side, since he paid part of the price and not the other part, so they mutually consented to this defect, unlike in the case of usurpation where the buyer did not consent, so he has the authority to return. And if part of a pure silver piece that was sold is claimed, he takes what remains at its share without option, because co-ownership is not a defect in a pure silver piece, since division does not harm it. He said: 'because co-ownership is a defect in the vessel' because partitioning harms it, and this defect existed at the time of sale, concurrent with it without his doing, unlike the previous case because co-ownership occurred by his doing, i.e., separation before payment of the price, thus the distinction. He said: 'and if part...' i.e., if he sold a piece of pure silver and part of it is claimed, the buyer takes what remains at its share without option, because co-ownership is not a defect in a pure silver piece, since splitting does not necessitate breaking, so the buyer is not harmed by the co-ownership in it. This is if the claim occurs after both parties have taken possession. But if it occurs before possession, then he has the option due to the splitting of the transaction before completion, as in al-Bahr. And the dirham and dinar are analogous to pure silver, because co-ownership in them is not considered a defect, as in al-Jawhara. He said: 'nuqra' with a damma on the nun, as in al-Mughrib and al-Qamus: it is the melted form of gold or silver, and it is said: before melting it is called tibr, as in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings regarding partial usurpation (استحقاق) of an item sold in a sarf transaction. First, if part of a silver vessel is claimed by a third party, the buyer has the option (خيار التفرق) to either take the remaining part at its proportionate price or return the entire vessel. The reason is that co-ownership resulting from the claim is a defect (عيب) in the vessel because partitioning it harms its value. This defect was present at the time of sale without the buyer's consent, so he may choose to return. This contrasts with the earlier case of partial payment where the co-ownership arose from the buyer's own action (not paying full price), so he could not return. Second, if the item is a piece of pure silver (نقرة), and part of it is claimed, the buyer must take the remainder at its proportionate price without any option, because co-ownership is not considered a defect in pure silver—dividing it does not cause loss since it can be melted down. The commentary adds that if the claim occurs before possession (قبض), the buyer does have the option because the transaction is split before completion, which is a different basis. Dirhams and dinars are analogous to pure silver in that co-ownership is not a defect. The passage concludes with a lexical note defining نقرة as melted gold or silver.

Key Terms
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's claim of ownership of part of the sold item, creating co-ownership.
النقرة (al-nuqra): Melted gold or silver, often in the form of a piece or coin, where division does not harm its value.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The right of a contracting party to annul or uphold a sale due to a defect or other reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the buyer have an option to return the item if part of a silver vessel is claimed by a third party, but not if part of a pure silver piece is claimed?
A) Because the vessel is more valuable than pure silver.
B) Because co-ownership due to usurpation is a defect in the vessel but not in pure silver.
C) Because the buyer consented to co-ownership in the pure silver case.
D) Because the claim occurred after possession in the vessel case.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to return an item due to partial usurpation depends on whether co-ownership is a defect—harmful division triggers an option; harmless division does not.

Buyer's Option When Part of Sold Item Is Reclaimed
Ruling on buyer's choice when part of the sold item is reclaimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between items where partnership (shirkah) after partial reclaim gives the buyer an option to return and items where it does not, and apply the Hanafi rule accordingly.

Core Idea
Partnership after reclaim is a defect only if division harms the item.

When a third party reclaims part of a sold item, the buyer may be left with shared ownership (shirkah) of the rest. Does this give the buyer a right to return the whole item? The Hanafi school answers: it depends on whether the partnership damages the item's value. This lesson examines the ruling and its evidence, focusing on the distinction between utensils and nuggets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage to find the ruling for a typical item (utensil) and a nugget (nuqra). Note the difference in the buyer's option.
The key is whether the reclaim occurs after possession.
2
Extract the ruling for each scenario
State the ruling: for a utensil, buyer takes remainder with its share or returns the whole; for a nugget, buyer takes remainder with its share only, no option to return.
3
Identify the ‘illa (effective cause)
The ruling turns on whether partnership (shirkah) is a defect. For utensils, division reduces value, so it is a defect. For nuggets, division does not cause loss, so it is not a defect.
This is the basis of the qiyas.
4
Understand the qiyas reasoning
The case of the utensil is the asl (original) where partnership is a defect, so the buyer has the option. The nugget is the far' (branch) where the illa (harm) is absent, so the option is not given.
Hanafi qiyas differentiates based on the nature of the item.
5
Recognize the exception before possession
If the reclaim occurs before the buyer takes possession, the transaction is incomplete, and the buyer has an option regardless of the item type due to tafarruq al-safqah (split of the bargain).
This is cited from al-Bahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن استحق بعضه أخذ المشتري باقيه بحصته أو ردّه، ولو استحق بعض قطعة نقرة بيعت أخذ ما بقي بحصته بلا خيار. لأن الشركة عيب في الإناء؛ لأن التشقيص يضره، وهذا العيب كان موجوداً عند البيع، مقارناً له بغير صنعه بخلاف ما مر؛ لأن الشركة وقعت بصنعه وهو الافتراق قبل نقد الثمن، فالفرق بين. ولو استحق بعض قطعة نقرة بيعت أخذ ما بقي بحصته بلا خيار؛ لأن الشركة ليست بعيب في قطعة النقرة، فإنه لا يلزم الانتقاض بالتبعيض، فلم يتضرر المشتري بالشركة فيهما، وهذا لو كان الاستحقاق بعد قبضهما. أما لو كان قبل قبضهما فله الخيار؛ لتفرق الصفقة عليه قبل التمام، كذا في «البحر».
And if part of it is reclaimed, the buyer takes the remainder with its share or returns it. And if part of a nugget piece sold is reclaimed, he takes what remains with its share without option. Because partnership is a defect in the utensil; because division harms it, and this defect existed at the time of sale, accompanying it without his doing unlike what preceded; because the partnership occurred by his action, namely the separation before payment of the price, so there is a difference. And if part of a nugget piece sold is reclaimed, he takes what remains with its share without option; because partnership is not a defect in the nugget piece, for division does not necessitate damage, so the buyer is not harmed by the partnership in either of them. This if the reclaim is after taking possession. But if it is before taking possession, he has the option due to the split of the deal before completion, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This is a ruling and its proof from the Hanafi school. It concerns the case where part of a sold item is reclaimed (istihqaq) by a third party. The buyer has the option: he may either keep the remaining portion in proportion to the price paid, or return the entire item. However, this option applies to items where the resulting partnership (shirkah) is a defect, like an utensil, because dividing it decreases its value. If the reclaim occurs after the buyer has taken possession, the partnership is not due to the buyer's action. In contrast, in the case of a nugget (nuqra) – which is a piece of melted silver or gold – partnership is not considered a defect because the nugget can be divided without loss; hence the buyer has no option to return. If the reclaim happens before possession, the buyer still has an option because the transaction was not completed.

Key Terms
النقرة (al-nuqra): A piece of melted gold or silver, before being coined.
الشركة (al-shirkah): Shared ownership between buyer and reclaiming party after partial reclaim.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's legal reclaim of part of the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if part of a silver nugget (nuqra) is reclaimed after the buyer has taken possession, what is the buyer's option?
A) He must return the entire nugget.
B) He may keep the remainder in proportion to the price or return the whole.
C) He takes the remainder in proportion with no option to return.
D) He may demand a reduction in price but must keep the whole.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sale law, the buyer's right to return a partially reclaimed item depends on whether the resulting partnership actually harms the item's value.

Validity of Mixed Sales of Currencies and Commodities
Hanafi ruling on simultaneous sale of multiple items with mismatched quantities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi justification for validating mixed ribawi sales with unequal quantities and contrast it with the Shafi'i/Zufar position.

Core Idea
A lump-sum exchange of ribawi items is valid if interpreted as each genus exchanged for its opposite.

The Hanafi school permits selling a mixture of dirhams and dinars, or wheat and barley, even when quantities are unequal, by reinterpreting the countervalues to avoid riba. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this position and the objections raised by Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and the opposing view
Identify the exact transaction (two dirhams + one dinar for one dirham + two dinars) and note that Hanafis declare it valid while Zufar and al-Shafi'i declare it invalid.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that a lump-sum exchange necessitates division across all items (shuyu'), which would cause riba because quantities of the same genus are unequal.
Key concept: al-jumlatu bi al-jumlati + al-shuyu'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response argues that we are not forced to divide each item against all others; instead we can pair each genus with its opposite (e.g., two dirhams for the dinar, one dirham for two dinars), thereby avoiding riba.
This is justified by preferring validity (tashih) and giving effect to the parties' intent.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis permit such contracts; Shafi'is and Zufar consider them batil (void).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بيع درهمين ودينار بدرهم ودينارين، وبيع كر بر وكُر شعير بكُري بر وكري شعير: هذا عندنا، وأما عند زفر والشافعي فلا يجوز؛ لأنه قابل الجملة بالجملة، ومن ضرورته الانقسام على الشيوع، وفي صرف الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس تغيير تصرفه. حاصله: إن البائع قابل الجملة بالجملة، ومقابلة الجملة بالجملة يقتضي الانقسام على الشيوع لا على التعيين، ففي حمله على خلاف الجنس تغيير له فلا يجوز، وإن كان فيه تصحيح التصرف؛ لأنه يصير المقابلة غير الأولى، ويكون التصرف تصرفاً آخر. والواجب تصحيح تصرف العاقد على الوجه الذي باشره وقصده، لا على خلاف ذلك، والعاقدان قصدا المقابلة المطلقة لا مقابلة الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس، وهي إنشاء تصرف آخر، وفسخ التصرف الأول. ومن ضرورته الانقسام على الشيوع؛ بأن ينقسم كل بدل من هذا الجانب على البدلين من ذلك الجانب، وكذا كل بدل من ذلك الجانب على البدلين من هذا الجانب، ومتى وجبت المقابلة هكذا جاز التفاضل ضرورة، إذ الحنطة والشعير والدراهم والدنانير من أحد الجانبين أكثر، فيتحقق الربا.
And it is valid to sell two dirhams and a dinar for one dirham and two dinars, and to sell a kurr of wheat and a kurr of barley for two kurrs of wheat and two kurrs of barley: this is with us. But according to Zufar and al-Shafi‘i, it is not permissible; because he made a lump sum against a lump sum, and its necessary consequence is division in common (on the whole), and in shifting a genus to a different genus there is a change in his disposal. The summary: The seller made a lump sum against a lump sum, and a lump sum against lump sum necessitates division in common not individually; so applying it to a different genus is a change and thus not permissible, even if it contains a correction of the disposal; because the comparison becomes other than the first, and the disposal becomes another disposal. And it is necessary to correct the disposition of the contracting party in the manner he undertook and intended, not contrary to that; and the two parties intended absolute comparison, not a comparison of genus to a different genus, which is a creation of another disposal and a rescission of the first disposal. And from its necessity is division in common: because each substitute from this side divides into the two substitutes from that side, and similarly each substitute from that side into the two substitutes from this side; and when comparison is thus necessary, disparity becomes unavoidably allowed, since wheat and barley and dirhams and dinars from one side are more, so riba comes into effect.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of certain mixed sales that involve ribawi items. The Hanafi position (followed by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) allows the sale of two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, and similarly for wheat and barley mixed. The rationale is to validate the contract by assuming that each item of one kind is exchanged for its opposite kind (e.g., the two dirhams are for the dinar, and the one dirham for the two dinars), even though the total quantities differ. This avoids riba because the excess is considered to arise from the exchange of different species. Zufar and al-Shafi‘i disagree, arguing that the contract is a lump-sum exchange which necessitates division across the board, leading to an ambiguous situation where riba may occur because the amounts are unequal. The Hanafis respond that the parties intended a valid exchange; to correct the contract, we interpret it in a way that makes it valid, even if it changes the precise pairing. This is based on the principle of preferring validity over nullity.

Key Terms
الجملة بالجملة (al-jumlatu bi al-jumlati): A lump sum exchange where the total of one side is matched with the total of the other, without specifying individual pairings.
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Division in common, meaning each item is considered as part of a collective whole.
التصرف (al-tasarruf): The legal act of the contracting party, i.e., the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars valid?
A) Because riba does not apply to dirhams and dinars.
B) Because the contract is interpreted as exchanging each genus for its opposite, avoiding riba.
C) Because the total value on both sides is equal.
D) Because the items are of different species, so excess is allowed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates mixed sales of ribawi items with unequal quantities by reinterpreting the countervalues to avoid riba, prioritizing validity and the parties' intent.

Validity of Mixed Currency Exchange in Hanafi Fiqh
Hanafi ruling and rebuttal against Zufar and al-Shafi'i on mixed currency exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on selling mixed currencies and evaluate the opposing arguments from Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A mixed exchange of dirhams and dinars is valid in Hanafi law by interpreting the contract as an absolute exchange, not a specific genus-for-genus swap.

This lesson examines the validity of selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, a classic dispute between the Hanafi school and Zufar/al-Shafi'i. We'll unpack the Hanafi argument that absolute matching (muqabalah mutlaqah) permits corrective interpretation to uphold the contract, and contrast it with the opposing view that matching whole with whole inevitably leads to riba.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding the mixed exchange of currencies and grains. The ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) and al-Shafi'i hold the opposite: the sale is invalid due to riba from division in commonality (shuyu').
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opponent's reasoning: matching whole with whole forces each item to be divided proportionally among items on the other side, creating unequal values within the same genus, which is riba.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafi school rebuts the opponent: absolute matching (muqabalah mutlaqah) does not specify genus-for-genus; when literal matching is impossible, interpret the contract in a way that corrects it, changing only the description (wasf) not the essence of exchange.
Key evidence from the text: the analogy of selling two dinars for two dinars where each takes one dinar and parts – the sale is valid for all, not just half.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
See the practical consequence: Hanafis allow such mixed exchanges with unequal amounts, while opponents require either equal exchange of each genus or specific assignment to avoid riba.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position, as stated in 'al-Kifaya' and the text.

وصح بيعُ درهمين ودينار بدرهم ودينارين، وبيعُ كُرِّ بُرٍّ وكُرِّ شعيرٍ بكُرَّيْ بُرٍّ وكُرَّيْ شعيرٍ. هذا عندنا، وأما عند زفر والشافعي فلا يجوز؛ لأنه قابل الجملة بالجملة، ومن ضرورته الانقسام على الشيوع، وفي صرف الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس تغيير تصرفه.
قوله: وصح...الخ؛ يعني لو باع درهمين وديناراً بدرهم ودينارين جاز البيع تصحيحاً للعقد، وجعل كل جنس من الدرهم والدينار بخلافه، واعتبر الدرهمان بالدينار، والدرهم بالدينارين.
قوله: وبيعُ كُرِّ بُرٍّ...الخ؛ بأن تجعلَ كُرًا بُرٍّ بكُرِّ شعيرٍ وكُرًا شعيرٍ بكُرِّ بُرٍّ، ولو صرفا إلى جنسه فسد، والتفصيل في «البحر»(۳)، فليطالع.
قوله: لأنه قابل...الخ؛ حاصله: إنَّ البائع قابل الجملة بالجملة، ومقابلة الجملة بالجملة يقتضي الانقسام على الشيوع لا على التعيين، ففي حملِهِ على خلاف الجنس تغيير له فلا يجوز، وإن كان فيه تصحيح التصرف؛ لأنه تصير المقابلة غير الأولى، ويكون التصرف تصرفاً آخر.
والواجب تصحيح تصرُّف العاقد على الوجه الذي باشره وقصده، لا على خلاف ذلك، والعاقدان قصدا المقابلة المطلقة لا مقابلة الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس، وهي إنشاء تصرف آخر، وفسخ التصرُّفِ الأول.
قوله: ومن ضرورته الانقسام على الشيوع؛ بأن ينقسم كل بدل من هذا الجانب على البدلين من ذلك الجانب، وكذا كلُّ بدل من ذلك الجانب على البدلين من هذا الجانب، ومتى وجبت المقابلة هكذا جاز التفاضل ضرورة، إذ الحنطة والشعير والدراهم والدنانير من أحد الجانبين أكثر، فيتحقق الربا.
قلنا: المقابلة المطلقة تحتمل الصرف المذكور، وفي حذف الجنس إلى خلافِهِ تغيير تصرفه، وإثبات مقابلة لا دليل عليها في لفظه، نعم فيه تصحيح تصرفه، ولكن تغيير التصرُّف لا يصح لتصحيح التصرف، كذا في «الكفاية». قوله: قلنا...الخ؛ توضيحهُ: إِنَّ في صرف الجنس إلى خلافه تصحيح العقد، وإلى جنسه فساده، ولا معارضة بين الفاسد والصحيح؛ لأنَّ الصحيح مشروع بأصلِهِ ووصفه؛ والفاسد بأصله دون وصفه، ولأنَّ العقد يقتضي مطلق المقابلة من غير أن يتعرض لمقيد، لا مقابلة الكلِّ بالكلِّ بطريق الشيوع، ولا مقابلة الفرد من جنسه، ولا من خلافِ جنسيه؛ لما عُرِفَ أنَّ المطلق غيرُ متعرض للمقيَّد، ولكن مع هذا عند الوجودِ لا يوجد إلا مقيداً؛ لتعذرِ وجودِ ذات بدون صفة، وإن كان اللفظ غير متعرض للصفةِ بل للذات فقط على ما عرف في موضعه. فيحمل على المقيد المصحح عند تعذر العمل بالإطلاق، ألا ترى أنه لو قال عند المقابلة: على أن يكون الجنس بخلاف الجنس صح، ولو كان منافياً لم يصح، فكان حمله على المقيَّد المصحح أولى من حمله على المقيَّدِ المفسد، وهو مقابلة الكلِّ بالكلِّ شائعاً، طلباً للصحة. ألا ترى أنَّ الكلام أصله أن يكون مستعملاً في حقيقته، ثم إذا تعذرت الحقيقة حمل على المجاز الممكن إذا كان لا يصح إلا بالحمل عليه، وإن كان تغيراً فهو تغيير للوصف لا تغير لأصل المقابلة، إذ هي موجودة؛ لأنَّ أصل المقابلة فيه إفادة الملكِ في الكلِّ بمقابلة الكل، وذلك لم يتغير. والدليل على أنه يحمل عليه عند التعذرِ طلباً للصحة أنه لو باع الجنس بالجنس بأن باع دينارين بدينارين مثلاً، فقبض كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ديناراً، ثمَّ افترقا صح البيع في المقبوض كله. ولو كان كما قالاه لما صح إلا في نصف كلِّ واحدٍ من المقبوضين؛ لأنَّ مقابلة
And it is valid to sell two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, and to sell one kurr of wheat and one kurr of barley for two kurrs of wheat and two kurrs of barley. This is our position. As for Zufar and al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible because the seller matched the whole with the whole, and from that necessarily follows division in commonality, and in diverting a genus to a different genus there is a change of his transaction. His statement: 'And it is valid...' etc.; meaning if someone sells two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, the sale is valid as a correction of the contract, making each genus of dirham and dinar opposite to the other, considering the two dirhams against the dinar and the dirham against the two dinars. His statement: 'And selling one kurr of wheat...' etc.; by making one kurr of wheat in exchange for one kurr of barley and one kurr of barley in exchange for one kurr of wheat, and if they had diverted each to its own genus it would be invalid. The details are in 'al-Bahr' so consult it. His statement: 'Because he matched...' etc.; summary: the seller matched the whole with the whole, and matching whole with whole necessitates division in commonality not in specificity. So interpreting it as diverting genus to a different genus is a change and thus invalid, even if it corrects the transaction; because the matching becomes contrary to the original and the transaction becomes a different transaction. The obligation is to validate the contracting party's transaction in the manner he performed and intended, not contrary to that. The two parties intended absolute matching, not matching a genus to a different genus, which would be creating a different transaction and canceling the first transaction. His statement: 'And from its necessity division in commonality'; meaning each equivalent from this side divides onto the two equivalents from that side, and likewise each equivalent from that side divides onto the two equivalents from this side. Whenever matching is like this, permissible disparity becomes necessary, since wheat and barley, and dirhams and dinars on one side are more, so riba occurs. We said: The absolute matching permits the mentioned exchange, and in diverting genus to other than its own there is change of his transaction and affirming a matching for which there is no evidence in his wording. Yes, it corrects his transaction, but changing the transaction is not permissible for the sake of correcting the transaction, as in al-Kifaya. His statement: 'We said...' etc.; its clarification: In diverting the genus to other than its own, the contract is corrected, and to its own it is invalidated. There is no contradiction between invalid and valid because the valid is legitimate in its essence and description, while the invalid is legitimate in its essence but not in its description. Also because the contract implies absolute matching without specifying any restriction, neither matching whole with whole in a common way, nor matching an individual of its genus, nor of a different genus, since it is known that the absolute does not consider the restricted. But still, when it exists, it only exists as restricted because it is impossible for an essence to exist without a quality, even if the wording does not consider the quality but only the essence as known in its place. So it is interpreted as the restricted that corrects when acting on the absolute is impossible. Do you not see that if he said at the time of matching: 'on condition that genus be opposite to genus', it is valid, and if it were contradictory it would not be valid. So interpreting it as the corrective restricted is better than interpreting it as the corruptive restricted, which is matching whole with whole in a common way, seeking validity. Do you not see that speech is originally used in its reality, then when reality is impossible, it is interpreted as possible metaphor if it is only valid by that interpretation? And even if it is a change, it is a change of description, not a change of the essence of matching, because it exists; for the essence of matching here is conferring ownership of all in exchange for all, and that has not changed. The evidence that it is interpreted as such when impossible seeking validity is that if he sold genus for genus, e.g., sold two dinars for two dinars, then each of them took possession of one dinar, and then they parted, the sale is valid for all of what was taken possession. If it were as they said, it would only be valid for half of each of what was taken possession, because the matching

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of selling two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars, and similarly selling one kurr of wheat and one kurr of barley for two kurrs of wheat and two kurrs of barley. The Hanafi school (our position) holds that such a sale is valid. The commentator explains that the ruling involves correcting the contract by assuming each genus is exchanged for the opposite genus: the two dirhams are matched against the dinar, and the dirham against the two dinars. This interpretation makes the contract valid. For the grains, the correction similarly involves matching wheat with barley and barley with wheat.

Zufar and al-Shafi'i (the opponents) argue that the sale is invalid because the exchange involves matching a whole against a whole, which necessarily leads to division in commonality (shuyu'). They claim that diverting a genus to a different genus changes the intended transaction. The commentator clarifies their reasoning: when the entire set from one side is matched against the entire set from the other side, each individual item from one side must be divided proportionally among the items from the other side, leading to shared ownership and potential disparity in value, which causes riba (usury).

The Hanafi response (qulna) is that absolute matching (al-muqabalah al-mutlaqah) permits the exchange as described. The contract aims at absolute exchange of ownership, not necessarily a specific matching of each genus to its own kind. When the literal absolute is impossible to implement, we interpret it in a way that corrects the transaction, not invalidates it. The change involved is only in the description (tasrif al-wasf), not in the essence of the exchange (asl al-muqabalah). The essence—exchange of ownership of all items in return for all items—remains intact. The Hanafis support their view by analogy: if someone sells two dinars for two dinars, and each party takes one dinar and then parts, the sale is valid for the entire amount taken, not just half. This shows that the contract can be interpreted in a manner that validates it as much as possible.

Key Terms
المقابلة المطلقة (al-muqābalah al-muṭlaqah): An absolute exchange of ownership without specifying which item is exchanged for which, accepting any valid assignment.
الشيوع (al-shuyūʿ): Commonality or shared ownership; here referring to the necessary division of each item's value proportionally among multiple items on the other side.
تصحيح التصرف (taṣḥīḥ al-taṣarruf): Correcting the transaction by interpreting the parties' words in a manner that makes it valid, as opposed to invalidating it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the sale of two dirhams and one dinar for one dirham and two dinars made valid?
A) By matching dirham to dirham and dinar to dinar, accepting the loss.
B) By considering the two dirhams as exchanged for the dinar and the single dirham for the two dinars.
C) By invalidating the entire contract due to riba.
D) By requiring the buyer to pay additional dirhams to balance the value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates mixed currency exchanges by interpreting absolute matching as a corrective device, rejecting Zufar and al-Shafi'i's insistence on division in commonality.
